School of Architecture for the opportunity to share many of these walks with its students.

to Angela Watters and Sara Benson for their patience and support, and to Becky Standard for her

McCown, Mike Bohlmann, and Jeff Poss for generously providing images used in this book.
I am particularly grateful to the individuals at Capital Planning and Facilities and Services. I would
like to thank Anthony Battaglia, architect, for opening doors--physically and intellectually--and for
sharing his extensive knowledge with the students. Morgan White, director of Strategic Initiatives

Introduction

This book is for novices interested in the design of the built world and those interested in the Uni-
versity of Illinois and its context. It examines the diversity of influences that inform and affect the
structures and landscapes that humans occupy. It seeks to clarify this wide range of factors and
determine how they come together. Through a series of walking tours, organized around themes,
the reader is guided through concepts and strategies that affect the human-built world. The Uni-
versity of Illinois Urbana-Champaign's campus and its surrounding context form the backdrop for
teaching these concepts. Each tour is framed with introductory material. The walks are designed
to be perceived through the senses as the reader conceives the related subjects. The Design of the
Built Environment is a broad exploration of the reasons why the world looks the way it does. Its
approach is an attempt to make the material accessible to all.

The built environment is both culturally significant and contextually based. It reflects individual
creativity and collective values. The text will explore interior design, architecture, landscape
architecture, urban design, and planning while concentrating on place making. The components
emphasized are the interdependent aspects of culture: design, economics, politics, technology,
and tectonics. The text is built on the belief that context forms the basis of design and that culture
forms the basis of context.

The book is divided into five parts.

Part 1. Approaches to Architecture

Architecture is considered the mother of the arts. As such it holds both aspirations and practical
requirements. This part illustrates approaches to architecture from different points of view. It
introduces the reader to how varied approaches to the built environment shape its design. The
definition of architecture from different points of view demonstrates approaches from a func-
tional, aesthetic, or structural bias. Later, vocabulary and principles are presented to inform future
examinations.

Part 1 introduces concepts (transparency, parti, poché) while exploring ideas of architecture,
design, structure, and use. The Architecture Building, designed by Charles Platt (1926), is used
to provide examples of these principles, clarify the points raised, and familiarize the reader with
architectural graphics.

xxiv | Introduction
Part 2. The Making of Place and the University Plan

The Urbana-Champaign campus is a product of over 150 years of planning and development. From
the early years of land acquisition to the present, ideas about what a university can and should
be, have helped form the campus. Ideals create a strong framework for generations to contribute
structures and designs of and for their time. As times change, subsequent generations determine
the value of these edifices for future generations. The walks in this part seek to explore the major
principles used by designers in the creation of the major public realms of the University of Illinois
Urbana-Champaign: the South Quad, the Main Quad, the engineering campus, and Campustown.
Each walk introduces themed concepts and historical information. Before undertaking these walks
a history of the university plan is offered to lay a foundation for the structures and architectural
approaches.

Part 2 uses the campus and its public realm to discuss ordering principles in the built environment
and the role buildings and landscape play in the creation of public and private spaces. This portion
has three walks through the central axes of the campus and the main quadrangles: the South Quad,
the Main Quad, and the engineering campus north of Green Street (the Bardeen Quad and the
North Quad). It uses the campus to introduce design principles (figure-ground, axis, hierarchy,
spatial closure).

Part 3. The University Context

The context surrounding the university predates its founding. The walks in this part explore the
context surrounding the university and the formative roles that these contexts play in the lives of
its inhabitants. These contexts include Champaign, Urbana, Philo, a local cemetery, and a typical
residential street with an atypical residence. Each part looks at issues that are local, regional, and
sometimes national in their scope. Physical, economic, and social contexts play an enormous role
in determining the form of the built environment. Traversing these environments reinforces gen-
eral concepts while providing specific examples of how structures work to create a larger set of
spaces and places.

Part 3 discusses the context surrounding the university, including Campustown; the towns of
Champaign, Urbana, and Philo; and the physical landscape through an examination of Mount Hope
Cemetery. This section examines national influences and regional values that informed settlement
and subsequent development. It includes a discussion of a typical Midwestern residential street
(Indiana Avenue) and an atypical house (Erlanger House) that provide insight into the norm.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | xxv
Part 4. Technical Aspects of Architecture

Design is a complex combination of art/aesthetics on one hand and technical aspects on the
other. The designer must evaluate both the needs of the owners/occupants and the general pub-
lic. Codes and regulations are used to protect the health, safety, and welfare of the community
while the owners' and occupants' needs must be considered to satisfy the construction's funder.
Part 4 deals with the codes, contracts, and basic principles of structure and mechanical systems,
including heating and ventilation systems (both supply and demand). This portion provides greater
insight into the infrastructure and technical aspects of design and how they influence the forms
that buildings and places take.

Part 5. Individual Buildings and Their Design

This part explores concepts in design and how they may reveal themselves in individual buildings.
The walks in this part provide insights into the causes and effects of principles and their applica-
tion.
Part 5 uses campus buildings to illustrate specific aspects of design such as precedents, the use of
the orders, servant space versus served space, function and design, meaning and metaphor, and
design synthesis and sustainability.
The parts are intended to present the concepts simply. The goal is for individuals to experience the
built environment and make connections for themselves. The text can be read as a general discus-
sion of how humans occupy the natural world and create the built environment to suit their needs.
Experiential examples are used to illustrate principles. Sometimes these examples are unique to
their local context, but often the principles can be seen more generally across a region or globally.
The Design of the Built Environment is meant to be experienced. To understand our world, one
must be a part of it. Once engaged with the environment the observer can make associations
with past experiences and make connections to things learned previously. In this way there are
two worlds, the world of perceptions and the world of conceptions. The two basic philosophi-
cal approaches to understanding our environment, the phenomenological and the ontological, are
addressed. The phenomenologist uses an approach that concentrates on the study of conscious-

xxvi | Introduction
                                                                                              1

  ness and the objects of direct experience, while the ontologist seeks to create a set of concepts

                                                                                                                                                                                                                    2

  and categories in a subject area or domain that shows properties and the relations between them.
  With this in mind, this book can be used in a variety of ways. The reader may sift through the
  material to gain a better understanding of the principles or to link their own experiences to those
  presented. Throughout the text, images and diagrams are presented to help clarify ideas regarding
  the principles and the edifices discussed. Photographs are used to reinforce ideas and aid in visu-
  alizing conditions for those not going on the walks. These tours discuss the venues and concepts
  while providing a hands-on/eyes-on opportunity.

 PART 1. APPROACHES TO
 ARCHITECTURE

        Overview

        One begins with the exploration of the basic question: What is architecture? Entire books have been
        dedicated to the subject. Here this question is used, instead, to frame approaches taken to design.
        After exploring the concepts outlined by Vitruvius--that architecture is the masterful combination of
        firmitas, utilitas, venustas, that is, it must be solid, useful, and beautiful--other opinions about what
        constitutes architecture are examined to introduce how values can affect the built environment.
        Some initial terms and concepts are outlined to present ideas used in the environments to be
        explored.

  What is architecture? This question is often posed to those interested in the creation of the built
  environment. Here, the question is used to frame how the many different approaches to design
  have arisen. Its many answers over the millennia, when examined collectively, begin to approxi-
  mate a definition given by the Roman architect Marcus Vitruvius Pollio (Vitruvius), who lived in
  the first century BCE. Vitruvius wrote De Architectura, a work of ten books that cover subjects
  such as city planning, public and private buildings, construction, military and civil engineering,
  and building materials. Vitruvius famously stated that architecture was the masterful combination

                                                                           4

  of firmitas, utilitas, and venustas. Sir Henry Wotton translates Vitruvius in the seventeenth cen-

                                                                                                                                                                                                5

  tury, writing: "Well building hath three conditions. Commoditie, Firmenes, and Delight," or func-
  tion, structure, and delight.
  Examining the Vitruvian triad leads to an understanding of values in different epochs. When struc-
  ture is emphasized, the goal to produce something bigger, taller, and other superlatives has led
  to material and technical advancements and new construction methods for edifices ranging from
  Gothic cathedrals to the world's tallest buildings. The emphasis on function has led to unique

4. Vitruvius, De Architectura, book 1, chap 3.
5. Wotton, Elements of Architecture, 1.

   1 | Part 1. Approaches to Architecture
  solutions to programmatic needs. The idea that "form follows function" has led to the creation of
  edifices that will better suit the needs of those using them. The emphasis on delight has led to

                                                                                                  6

  aesthetic refinements and poetic responses.

  A collection of quotations about architecture from the twentieth and twenty-first centuries indi-

                                                                                                                                                                                                7

  cates a bias toward the venustas, whether that be interpreted as delight, beauty, or love.

          Architecture is the will of an epoch translated into space.

          --Ludwig Mies van der Rohe

          Architecture is a learned game, correct and magnificent, of forms assembled in the light.

          --Le Corbusier

          Architecture is the triumph of human imagination over materials, methods, and men, to
          put man into possession of his own Earth. It is at least the geometric pattern of things, of
          life, of the human and social world. It is at best that magic framework of reality that we
          sometimes touch upon when we use the word "order."

          --Frank Lloyd Wright

          Architecture is really about well-being. I think that people want to feel good in a space. . . .
          On the one hand it's about shelter, but it's also about pleasure.

          --Zaha Hadid

          Architecture is basically a container of something. I hope they will enjoy not so much the
          teacup, but the tea.

          --Yoshio Taniguchi

          Architecture is inhabited sculpture.

          --Constantin Brancusi

6. In his discussion of venustas--Built upon Love: Architectural Longing after Ethics and Aesthetics--Alberto Pérez-Gomez
   offers a particularly phenomenological interpretation, expressing a view that venustas is love rather than delight or
   beauty, having been born of the root for Venus, goddess of love.

7. Ludwig Mies van der Rohe quoted in Johnson, Mies van der Rohe, 188; Le Corbusier, Towards a New Architecture, 203;
   Frank Lloyd Wright quoted in Library of Congress, Frank Lloyd Wright; Zaha Hadid quoted in SOA Sketchbook for High
   School Students (School of Architecture, Oklahoma State University), 13; Yoshio Taniguchi, quoted in The Japan Archi­
   tect, 1996, exact page number unavailable; Constantin Brancusi, AZQuotes, https://www.azquotes.com/quote/34514, 11;
   Maya Lin quoted in Boundaries, 15.
                                                                                                                                                                    Kevin J. Hinders | 2
          Architecture is like a mythical fantastic. It has to be experienced. It can't be described. We
          can draw it up and we can make models of it, but it can only be experienced as a complete
          whole.

          --Maya Lin

  The Vitruvius triad (or portions of it) might be interpreted as an equation: Structure Function
  Delight = Architecture. When one element is missing Architecture is not attained: Structure
  Delight = Sculpture; Structure Function = Building; Delight Function = Ruins. Before examining this
  triad in detail and undertaking a field study, it is helpful to introduce concepts and terms used by
  designers of the built environment.

  Phenomenal transparency is when two distinct figures appear to occupy the same space simulta-

                                                                                                                                                                    8

  neously. Designers have employed phenomenal transparency for centuries, but the term was cre-
  ated in the twentieth century by Gyorgy Kepes:

          If one sees two or more figures overlapping one another, and each of them claims for
          itself the common overlapped part, then one is confronted with a contradiction of spatial
          dimensions. To resolve this contradiction, one must assume the presence of a new optical
          quality. The figures are endowed with transparency: that is they are able to interpenetrate
          without an optical destruction of each other. Transparency however implies more than an
          optical characteristic; it implies a broader spatial order. Transparency means a simulta-
          neous perception of different spatial locations. Space not only recedes but fluctuates in a
          continuous activity. The position of the transparent figures has equivocal meaning as one

                                                                                                                                             9

          sees each figure now as the closer, now as the further one.

  Thus art is dependent on viewers' participation, and architecture is one of the most interactive
  arts. Buildings are meant to be experienced. As Maya Lin noted, any structure "can only be expe-
  rienced as a complete whole," and environments can be fully understood only through immersion.
  The ability for the observer to "read" a building has been consciously manipulated by designers
  using phenomenal transparency for centuries.

  Parti is a shortened term derived from partis prix, which is a term from the École des Beaux-Arts
  (the influential French art schools) that literally means "departure point." In architecture it refers
  to the basic idea or organizational strategy that is fundamental to the design. A parti is often a
  basic spatial diagram. One example is the primary shape used by the campus designer Charles
  Platt for the Architecture Building, whose T shape is formed by linking its major spaces with hall-
  ways, classrooms, and offices.

8. See Rowe and Slutzky, "Transparency"; Rowe and Slutzky, "Transparency Part II."
9. Kepes, Language of Vision, 77-78.

   3 | Part 1. Approaches to Architecture
Poché is also derived from the École des Beaux-Arts and literally means "poached" but can be a
noun or a verb. As a noun, it refers to something that gives shape to space like the irregularly
formed white in a poached egg that gives shape to the yolk. A poché may be the walls or masses
used to define an area of a building. Another way to conceptualize a poché is to think of a pocket
whose cloth defines the void into which one may place hands or keys. As a verb, architects use
poché to mean the filling in of the area between walls or spaces. For example, the buildings sur-
rounding the Main Quad act as poché, giving shape to the grassy space that without the buildings
would have no clear definition.

Part 1.1. The Architecture Building

     About the Walk
     This walk uses the Architecture Building to clarify basic design concepts such as literal and phenom-
     enal transparency, poché, and parti. The notion that a building can be read or interpreted is then
     discussed and illustrated.
     This walk begins outside the Architecture Building at 608 East Lorado Taft Drive in Champaign.

Transparency, parti, and poché, along with the concepts of rhythm and proportion, will be
explored in relation to the Architecture Building, which was designed by Charles Platt and com-

pleted in 1926. Located at 608 East Lorado Taft Drive, it is part of a composition of buildings with

Study Hall), and Commerce West (now Wohlers Hall). In Platt's design for campus, the Surveying
Building was to be razed, and a fourth building similar to the Architecture Building was to be con-

Platt created a set of building partis that could be combined in a variety of ways to create block

                                                                                                                                                                   1

plans. These partis can be likened to letters of the alphabet with variations. These blocks fit into
the city grid and were expected to serve as a model for university development. Examples of these
building partis and block plans can be seen on campus, though Platt's design for an extensive rep-
etition of these blocks was never realized.

In Platt's 1923 Sketch for the Development of Campus (see figure 1.1.3), the block configurations in
figure 1.1.2 can be seen flanking the campus's main axis. In Platt's Study of the Ultimate Development
(see figure 1.1.4), the composition with DKH, Wohlers Hall, and the Architecture Building, along

1. For example, the Architecture Building is a T building and Mumford Hall is a C building.

                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 6
with several other block compositions, is seen repeated in a cookie cutter manner. The result is a
diversity of scales from grand axial quadrangles to handsome streets to intimate courtyard spaces.

                                          Figure 1.1.2. Building partis and block plans.
                                          A. Platt's basic partis included an "I" building, a "T"
                                          building, a "C" building, a "Block I" building.

Platt designed numerous campus buildings, including the Main Library, Louise Freer Hall, DKH,
Mumford Hall, and the President's House, but the Architecture Building is his most remarkable
campus structure. One can see the care Platt took in creating a space for "Architecture and Kin-
dred Subjects," as the engraved stone above the main entrance heralds.
This structure readily demonstrates the concept of phenomenal transparency. The building's
design allows for different ways for it to be seen or "read." Like a text, it may be translated in dif-
ferent ways. Within the larger composition it relies on DKH and Wohlers Hall to create the illusion
of a tripartite structure similar to designs by the Renaissance architect Andrea Palladio in which
a central pavilion is flanked by two end pavilions and connected with a loggia. This compositional
strategy was used by architects who admired Palladio's The Four Books of Architecture including
Colen Campbell, a noted Scottish architect and neo-Palladian credited with the creation of the
Georgian style who published a volume entitled Vitruvius Britannicus. The influence of Vitruvius
and Palladio are thus brought to campus through its Georgian buildings (see figure 1.1.5).

of coherency to campus. Under his direction cam-

pus began to consistently use this style for a wide

range of building types including classrooms (DKH and Mumford Hall), the addition to the Armory,

the Main Library, and gymnasiums (Freer and Huff Halls). Platt's adoption of the Georgian style

allowed him to use the Palladian compositional strategy of a central volume with two connected

pavilions at either end (see figure 1.1.6).

It is a transformation of this compositional parti that Platt uses to connect DKH and Wohlers Hall
with the Architecture Building. By providing gates flanking the Architecture Building, Platt visu-
ally connects the three buildings in a composition reminiscent of Palladio's design style. Figure 1.1.7
shows different ways in which principles of rhythm, repetition, hierarchy, and scale are used in
this block plan.

2. It is not unusual for a campus to adopt a particular building style. Princeton University, the University of Notre Dame,
   and Washington University in St. Louis favor the collegiate Gothic style, while the first buildings at Stanford University
   reflect a coherence because of the consistent use of the Romanesque style.

                                                                                                                                                                    Kevin J. Hinders | 8
Figure 1.1.5. Villa Barbaro at Maser, a 16th C. villa designed by Palladio. The center pavilion is pushed forward
and linked to the end pavilions with a connecting loggia. This composition is repeated in the Georgian style at
Mumford Hall and other university buildings.

The front facade of the Architecture Building is a rich weaving of vertical and horizontal elements
that can be interpreted in many ways because of its use of phenomenal transparency. Looked at
one way, the building appears to be a giant red brick aqueduct. Looked at another way, the building
can be seen as a series of stacked windows standing in soldier-like attention. The viewer is made
to interpret the facade and its many transparencies (see figure 1.1.8).

The ribbon of stone between the first and second floors that weaves through the limestone win-
dow detailing carries the transparency further (see figure 1.1.9). The window base is a remarkable
mixture of a dominant horizontal stone that turns into a vertical reading (see figures 1.1.10 and
1.1.11).

On entering the Architecture Building one comes to an entry hall with steps up to two intersecting
hallways. The intersection is marked by two columns with matching pilasters against the wall.
These squared-off columns match the freestanding columns but are engaged (attached) to the
adjacent walls. The columns and pilasters form an aedicule space, which typically marks a location
like an important opening or spot where walkways adjoin (see figure 1.1.12).

9 | Part 1.1. The Architecture Building
       Figure 1.1.6. Mumford Hall line drawing with diagram. Above, the front elevation facing the South
       Quad. Below, the three pavilions are shaded. The central pavilion is five bays wide and occupies a
       central axis and secondary pavilions to the north and south have their own entry axes. Note the
       manner in which downspout placement and chimneys subtly reinforce the north and south entries/
       masses.

This aedicule calls attention to the intersection of the entry that leads back to Room 120 (a promi-
nent lecture room) and the cross-axis hallway that goes west to the large Temple Hoyne Buell
Gallery and east to a series of offices used by the dean of the College of Fine and Applied Arts.
These offices are relatively new and replace a large room that was used for teaching. Prior to the
college offices being inserted into this space, the building had a straightforward parti: a T-shaped
building with large rooms terminating the ends of each hallway (See figures 1.1.13-1.1.16).

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 10
The second floor has also been altered. It once had a library above Room 120 and two large studio
spaces at the east and west ends of a corridor lined with bathrooms and offices. These rooms
acted as poché to give form to the hallways and the major rooms. The third-floor area above the
library has a simple flat roof, but the parti continues to have a large studio space at either end of a
long hall. Offices, bathrooms, and secondary classrooms line this hall. Tying the building together
vertically is a beautiful curving staircase that occupies the northwest corner off the intersection
of the hallways.

The facade of the Architecture Building is hierarchical, rhythmic, and rich. Figure 1.1.17 shows a
comparison of the plan and interior spaces set below the facade. The major spaces have been
shaded to show the relationship between the plan and the facade.
Phenomenal transparency may also be seen in three-dimensional space. Temple Hoyne Buell
Gallery has spaces within spaces. First the space may be read as a single volume, and then vertical
and horizontal aspects of the gallery can influence the impression of dimension and volume. Fig-
ure 1.1.18 shows the many spatial transparencies of the gallery.

17 | Part 1.1. The Architecture Building
       Figure 1.1.18. Spatial transparency of Temple Hoyne Buell Gallery.

With these basic architectural concepts and techniques for reading spaces, a foundation is in place
for exploring more concepts and principles. Additional examples will focus on the University of
Illinois campus and the surrounding environment, but many concepts are applicable to the Mid-
west, the United States, and global settings. The reader is encouraged to reflect on their own
experiences and the environments they occupy when walking through campus spaces, reading the
text, and considering the details highlighted in the illustrations.

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 18
PART 2. THE MAKING OF PLACE AND
THE UNIVERSITY PLAN

       Overview

       After passage of the Morrill Act in 1862, the university was created in 1867 as a land-grant institution
       on acreage between Urbana and Champaign. Since its early days the university has gone through
       periods of rapid growth (for example 1910-30) and periods of little construction (such as during the
       1970s). There have been significant contributors to the growth of the university. Following
       World War II the Servicemen's Readjustment Act of 1944 (and subsequent government programs
       commonly referred to as the GI bill) afforded many the opportunity to access higher education
       without regard to cost or ability to pay. This expanded the size of the university in an era in which
       public and private research were seen as essential postwar tools to keep America at the forefront of
       technology. The Civil Rights Act of 1964 led to diversification of the student body, and the Higher
       Education Act of 1965 provided financial assistance to universities for scholarships, low-interest
       loans, and the National Teaching Corps. These new programs greatly increased the size of universi-
       ties' administration across the nation and its subsequent footprint on campuses. Finally, the growth
       of intercollegiate athletics through commercial television and outside funding has significantly
       altered the university's physical landscape.
       The planning of the campus has been a conscious effort to allow for needed growth of individual
       colleges while managing the goals and objectives of the overall institution. This section gives a his-
       torical introduction to the campus.

  The new Industrial University of Illinois, founded in 1867, was the result of the Morrill (Land Grant)

                                                                                                                                    1

  Act of 1862, signed into law by President Abraham Lincoln. The act's goal was to help establish

1. The original bill was passed by Congress in 1859 but was vetoed by President James Buchanan. Edmund James asserts
  that the bill was created by Johnathon B. Turner and his Illinois associates years prior to the election of Justin S. Morrill:
  "There is also evidence that Justin S. Morrill was selected by Turner and other friends of the measure to introduce the
  bill because he was from an older state which had not thus far benefitted by the land grant of the Federal Government.
  And that in this way he [Morrill] for the first time became connected with the bill" (see James, Origin of the Land Grant
  Act, 27).
  19 | Part 2. The Making of Place and the University Plan
institutions of higher education that would provide practical education in agriculture and the
mechanical arts and extend higher education to the working classes. The Morrill Act provided for
30,000 acres of federal land per US senator and congressperson for each state to help fund these
new schools. Because Illinois had sixteen representatives in 1867, it was given 480,000 acres of
federal land in Minnesota and Nebraska.

Figure 2.1. 1871 Plan of the grounds and acreage of the
Illinois Industrial University. Drawn by A. G. Kennedy & J.
W. Rhodes. Courtesy of the University of Illinois
Archives.Read long description of figure.

                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 20
The people of Urbana and Champaign (Champaign County) placed a bid to host what was to
eventually become the University of Illinois. There was heated competition from two other com-
munities: Normal (Maclean County) and Jackson (Logan County). After considerable debate and
lobbying from the different constituents, Champaign County's bid was selected in February 1867.

                  Figure 2.2. University of Illinois Land Acquisitions 1867- 1925. Image by Kevin Hinders
                  and Stephen Ferroni. First published by Kevin Hinders and Benjamin Bross in The
                  University of Illinois Memorial Stadium: The First 100 Years (London: Routledge,
                  2024). https://doi.org/10.4324/9781032643885.

21 | Part 2. The Making of Place and the University Plan
  When the Board of Trustees for the new educational institution met in March 1867, its land hold-
  ings were considerable. On the condition that the new university be built in Urbana-Champaign,
  the citizens of Champaign County contributed assets including 405 acres south of Mount Hope
  Cemetery known as the Busey Farm; 400 acres of farmland southeast of Urbana (Griggs Farm,
  destined to be sold off over the next 25 years to support the university's coffers); 160 acres of
  land north of the cemetery bounded by Lincoln Avenue to the east and Fourth Street to the west
  with a northern boundary just south of where Foellinger Auditorium now stands (Armory Avenue
  extended); an old seminary building and its surrounding 10 acres; $100,000 in Champaign County
  bonds; $2000 in trees donated from the Dunlap nursery; and $50,000 in freight charges from the

                                                       2

  Illinois Central Railroad. Almost immediately the board decided to link the 10-acre property to the
  north with the 160-acre property to the south. Thirty-six acres, known as the Wells Tract, were
  purchased to connect these properties before the university opened to students in March 1868.
  This central core of more than 600 acres still resonates today. The university has a clear presence

                                                                                                                                           3

  where the boundaries were established (see figures 2.1 and 2.2).

  The land south of University Avenue and north of Springfield Avenue has been altered somewhat
  since the university's founding, as Mathews Avenue now extends north of Springfield Avenue.
  Today this area is organized by structures surrounding the North Quad, also known as the Beck-
  man Quad, and the area south of Springfield Avenue and north of Green Street is organized by
  the internalized Bardeen Quad. The 1871 plan shows the Green Street offset as the grid shifts to
  the south between Urbana and Champaign along Green Street. This was originally planned to be a
  boulevard (see figure 2.3).

  The Main Quad was established between Green Street and Armory Avenue (extended), while the
  160-acre section south of Armory Avenue encompasses the South Quad and what would become
  the military axis. Aspects of this early map still reverberate. Illini Grove remains on land designated

in 1871 as a forest plantation. The Division of Intercollegiate Athletics (DIA) and Campus Recre-
ation still share control of the stock farm area south (and west) of the cemetery with the College
of Agriculture, Consumer, and Environmental Sciences. The extension of Fourth and First Streets
down to St. Mary's Road is depicted at the bottom left of figure 2.1, foreshadowing the creation of
Assembly Hall (now State Farm Center) almost 100 years later.

Figure 2.3. Detail from the 1871 map of farms, buildings, and grounds showing the Green Street offset between
Urbana and Champaign. Courtesy of the University of Illinois Archives.

Land ownership, more than any early plan or vision, set the stage for subsequent campus designs.
The early administrations were quick to recognize the need for securing adjacent lands and tried
to make certain the university did not get into a position where it was unable to expand. Located
between the towns of Urbana and Champaign, the university now sits as a mediator between the
two communities, straddling Wright Street between Champaign and Urbana (see figure 2.4).
As the university grew, the need for the orderly use of land in a planned manner was balanced by
the immediate demands of college units. Early plans show buildings set in a landscape, but these
soon evolved into the familiar setting of buildings banding together to form collective spaces. Act-
ing Regent Thomas Burrill, a botanist and professor, recognized the need for ordering elements
on campus and planted elm trees along the avenue that would bear his name. The elms and the
avenue are now gone, transformed into a walkway on the west side of the Main Quad known as
the Broadwalk.
Numerous plans were produced in the first two decades of the twentieth century. From 1909 to
1917 the university enlisted the aid of the Burnham Campus Plan Commission, whose members

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 24
                                                                                                 7  8
included chair Daniel H. Burnham of Chicago, William C. Zimmerman of Chicago, and Clarence

                                                   9

H. Blackall from Boston. Professors James M. White and Frederick M. Mann of the Department of

                                                                                             10

Architecture joined the commission in 1910.

                                                diagram.

   Burnham made notable pronouncements about design:

 7. Burnham is well known for his work on the plans for Chicago, San Francisco, Washington, DC, and Manila. Burnham had
    gained fame as the director of works for the 1893 World's Columbian Exposition in Chicago, where he employed the U
    of I sculptor and alumnus Lorado Taft to create sculptures. Burnham later served on the McMillan Commission, whose
    members included landscape architect Frederick Law Olmsted and Charles F. McKim of the New York firm McKim,
    Meade, and White that later designed the Women's Building at the university. The McMillan Commission produced a
    master plan for the Washington, DC, Mall and park system. The ideas from the Burnham Commission reflected their
    belief that large, open public spaces surrounded by buildings of appropriate size and density was a model for both cities
    and universities.

 8. Zimmerman served as Illinois State Architect for 1905-15. In this capacity, he is credited with designing many buildings
    on campus, including the Natural History Building additions, the Armory (in its original state), the Commerce Building
    (now the David Dodds Henry Administration Building), the Physics Building, and the Stock Pavilion.

 9. Blackall was an alumnus of the class of 1877 who became an eminent architect in Boston. He prepared plans for the uni­
    versity as early as 1903. He also designed Foellinger Auditorium, which was completed in 1907.

10. White was the supervising architect for the university beginning around 1909. He is credited with designing or code­
    signing approximately fifty buildings on campus including Smith Memorial Hall. He worked in collaboration with Charles
    Platt on many projects including the President's House, the Women's Gymnasium (now Louis Freer Hall), the Armory
    addition, and the Main Library; Mann is credited with R. C. Jones and R. T. Jones for the design of the Acacia fraternity
    house (now the Library and Information Science Building), built in 1914.
     25 | Part 2. The Making of Place and the University Plan
           Make no little plans; they have no magic to stir men's blood and probably themselves will
           not be realized. Make big plans; aim high in hope and work, remembering that a noble, log-
           ical diagram once recorded will never die, but long after we are gone will be a living thing,
           asserting itself with ever-growing insistency. Remember that our sons and grandsons are
           going to do things that would stagger us. Let your watchword be order and your beacon

                                11

           beauty.

   This was Burnham's charge to the other commission members: He acted in only an advisory capac-
   ity and never produced a plan. Each of the other members created drawings and plans that were

                                                                                                                                                                                                        12

   shared to provide a broad, comprehensive set of ideas to develop over the next ten years. Most
   plans featured a Main Quad with southern expansion and an attempt to organize an engineering
   area. Eventually the plans began to expand westward along what became known as the military
   axis, which included a large area for recreation and military activities south of the Armory. Follow-
   ing World War I, plans by the architectural firm Holabird and Root and a commission study fixed
   the site of Memorial Stadium.

   In 1922, Charles Platt was employed to study the campus master plan and subsequently as archi-

                                                                                                                         13

   tect for the Agriculture Building (now Mumford Hall). Platt accepted most of the principles and
   recommendations of landscape architect Frederick Law Olmsted and the Burnham Campus Plan
   Commission and then set out to establish the relationship between the plan and the individual
   structures. Platt determined that a unifying Georgian style would establish a presence for the uni-
   versity while uniting the buildings and the landscape into a cohesive entity.

   The university then commenced on a series of periods of slow growth followed by rapid building.
   With the Great Depression came a general slowing of campus construction. One notable exception
   was the building of the Illini Union. This important structure fulfilled Olmsted's prediction that a
   new and better building would replace the old University Hall. The positioning of the Union is crit-
   ical in the overall plan.

   After World War II the university experienced a period of rapid growth. The Servicemen's Read-

                                                                                                                         14

   justment Act of 1944, familiarly known as the GI bill, allowed unprecedented access to higher

   education. New construction was necessary to provide both housing and classroom space for the
   influx of students, and new research concentrations demanded lab space. This turn toward sci-
   ence and technology drove a simple modern style that replace Platt's Georgian model for campus
   structures.

   By the 1960s the Baby Boomers were making their way onto campus and with them came a con-
   struction surge. Some buildings constructed in this period are Krannert Center for the Perform-
   ing Arts, Assembly Hall (now State Farm Center), Turner Hall, Commerce West (now Wohlers Hall),
   the Psychology Building, the Foreign Languages Building (now the Literatures, Cultures, and Lin-
   guistics Building), Morrill Hall, the Newmark Civil Engineering Laboratory, the Education Building,
   and the Intramural Physical Education Building (now the Activities and Recreation Center or ARC).
   Examined collectively these structures can be seen as newcomers to the campus master plan that
   filled in open spaces. No new quadrangles were developed to situate the great variety of research,
   classroom, sports, performance, and exhibition structures. The Foreign Languages Building was
   the only new structure on the Main Quad. The majority of these newcomers were constructed on
   sites marked in earlier plans for future structures or on newly acquired sites near the perimeter
   of campus. Perhaps this reflects the strength of Burnham's call to "make no little plans" as the plan
   provided before World War II was clear and inspiring to direct construction for decades.
   During this period the university also dedicated its efforts to housing, building over twenty dormi-

                                                                        15

   tories at the campus periphery. These units were often in complexes with shared dining facilities

15. These dormitories replaced housing that had been located south of Gregory Drive and west of Fourth Street. The uni­
    27 | Part 2. The Making of Place and the University Plan
   and multiple structures working together to form a composition. For example, the collection of
   dormitories known as the Six Pac consisted of three structures located on Peabody Drive--Syn-
   der, Scott, and Weston Halls--and three on Gregory Drive--Hopkins, Garner, and Forbes Halls (see

                            16

   figure 2.5). This composition included shared dining and support facilities. Each building had
   small courtyards, and the overall composition rejected the quadrangle precedent, preferring less
   defined spaces between the dormitories.

   These and many other structures built at this time were designed by Ambrose Richardson. His
   Ten-Year Development Plan was submitted in 1960, but growth stalled as stagflation--a combi-
   nation of economic stagnation and inflation--spurred in part by the energy crisis of the 1970s
   reduced funding. As the modernist period gave way to postmodernism, the style and formulation
   of campus were reexamined. Just as the country was exploring its past, so the university began to
   draft strategies to preserve its heritage while exploring the possibilities of its master plan. Sasaki
   and Associates began a 30-plus-year relationship with the University of Illinois about this time and
   described a different approach:

           The central theme of planning and design at Illinois expands and perpetuates the best tra-
           ditions of the university's past physical development. In developing several major campus
           quadrangles, for example, Sasaki drew inspiration from plans from the 1920s and the Main
           Quad at the heart of campus. Sasaki's work reinforces the desired relationships among aca-
           demic, research, and support units and achieves an overarching spatial order that clearly
           links the various campus precincts together while maintaining functional integrity. The
           result is an organized system of campus spaces and land-use precincts that accommodate

                                                                                                                                                        17

           future growth and reflect the school's international reputation.

   Whereas the Burnham Campus Plan Commission, Holabird and Root, and Platt sought to make a
   great plan for a great university, the Sasaki approach claims to follow those traditions but refers
   mainly to functional relationships and organized systems, making no reference to a plan's "magic
   to stir men's blood."

   The Six Pac has been dramatically altered. In the 21st century several of the dorms along with the
   dining facilities have been replaced. The Ikenberry Commons now organizes the composition.

   In the 1990s the university began a new joint venture that dramatically altered the campus. In 1997
   the Board of Trustees sent out a request for proposals for two new research parks: one east of

    versity also constructed married student housing in Urbana east of the President's House and south of Kirby Avenue,

The strategy for development of Research Park south of campus has largely proven to be success-
ful, while the spot intended for a northern research zone has transformed into an extension of
the engineering campus with localized public spaces such as the courtyard between the Thomas
M. Siebel Center for Computer Science and the National Center for Supercomputing Applica-
tions. Farther east, Campbell Hall and the University Laboratory High School occupy signifi-
cant real estate. Parking structures and parking lots along University Avenue serve the area, but
growth there has been stagnant for several decades. The research spaces and labs once consid-
ered for the north part of campus have given way to new plans as the Carle Illinois College of
Medicine--opened in 2018 as the world's first engineering-based college of medicine--has begun
exploring additional spaces for educating its students.

   The footprint of the DIA has grown on the fields south of Mount Hope Cemetery into a small cam-
   pus with the State Farm Center, Memorial Stadium, the Bielfeldt Athletic Administration Building,
   the Ubben Basketball Complex, the Atkins Tennis Center/Khan Outdoor Tennis Complex, Illinois
   Field, and the Henry Dale and Betty Smith Football Performance Center, along with facilities for
   baseball, softball, track, and soccer. Nearby are the Demirjian Indoor and Lauritsen/Wohlers Out-
   door Golf Practice Facilities. Plans to build new spaces for volleyball, gymnastics, and wrestling
   continue to be discussed.
   The expansions of Research Park, the domain of the DIA, and the intramural fields have come at
   some cost to the feel of the campus. Many of the agriculture facilities that had been so important
   for the development and the psyche of the university have been moved far away from the central
   core and now typically are south of Windsor Road and beyond.
   In the mid-1990s David Dressel, then director, Office of Facilities, Planning and Management pre-
   dicted that the university would fill all its available building sites by 2030 and then would have a
   denser environment. Today that prediction is challenged by the university's dedication to the
   environment and its desire to create a more sustainable campus. The goal set in 2008 for carbon
   neutrality by 2050 has significantly affected growth. The 2010 plan for climate action and subse-
   quent iterations limit consumption and promote resiliency. One aspect of the overall strategy is

                                                                                18

   the commitment to no net growth. As the university embarks on the next phases of its planning
   it will reevaluate its objectives and methods for achieving those goals.

Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the
Farms

     About the Walk

     It is a fitting place to begin: the Stock Pavilion. With its earthy smell, dirt floor, grandstand seating,
     and trussed roof, the structure is a beautiful example of a collective building for the South Quad. A
     collective building is more than an individual department or school within a university. It is a place
     where all people can come together to celebrate the common good and mark the passing of time.
     The Stock Pavilion thus represents the College of Agricultural, Consumer, and Environmental Sci-
     ences and becomes a focal point of the South Quad. This walk will discuss the hierarchy of the South
     Quad and its secondary quadrangle, the military axis. The general plan of the university will be fur-
     ther discussed with particular attention to David Kinley Hall, Mumford Hall, the brilliance of the
     library's placement, and the effect of Dutch elm disease. Concepts such as figure-ground, axis of
     symmetry, and balance will be introduced.
     This walk begins at the Stock Pavilion located at 1402 West Pennsylvania Avenue in Urbana and tra-
     verses the South Quad to the Undergraduate Library and the back of Foellinger Auditorium.

The concept of figure-ground is one of the first taught to design students and is often illustrated
with a vase/face drawing. In figure 2.1.1 it is possible to see the silhouettes of two faces (illustrated
here in black) looking at one another across a short distance (in white). It is also possible to see a
vase (white on a black background). The drawing demands for the viewer to decide what they see.
The faces form the vase, and the vase forms the faces. It is impossible to perceive the faces and the
vase simultaneously. The viewer's mind flips back and forth, seeing the faces, then the vase, and
then the vase in the foreground must mentally recede to become a background before the faces
can appear. It is up to the viewer to determine which figure they perceive.

This type of diagram is referred to as a figure-ground. The tool is used by designers, especially
architects and urban designers, when diagramming buildings and cities (see figure 2.1.2).

In a diagram like figure 2.1.3, which depicts the city of Champaign, Illinois the buildings are shown
as black on a white field. This can be reversed so that white buildings are seen on a black field, as
shown in Figure 2.1.3.

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 32
In this type of reversal, the mind senses the image differently, and these alternate perceptions can
help provide the designer with insights.

                                            Part 2.1. Orientation Map.

A second useful concept is an axis. An axis is a central or pivotal element, typically a line, around
which a composition is arranged. Nature has a multitude of symmetrical creatures that have dif-
ferent kinds of axes. An example of a bilaterally symmetrical creature is a butterfly. The axis of
symmetry runs through the butterfly's body from head to tail. Another kind of symmetry is radial
symmetry, whereby a symmetry exists around a central point. Think of a starfish with its five arms
radiating outward from its body. The South Quad has several important axes of different types.

33 | Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the Farms
                                                   Figure 2.1.1. Vase-Face diagram.

Situated at the south end of the main axis, the Stock Pavilion has a rich, earthy smell. Years of use
have given the structure an appropriately rugged patina. The central portion of campus ends in
this low, vaulted structure that functions as both a visual terminus to the South Quad and a sym-
bolic representation of the College of Agricultural, Consumer, and Environmental Sciences (ACES).
This walk will proceed to the other bookend to the South Quad--Foellinger Auditorium, which
serves as the representation of both today's College of Liberal Arts and Sciences and the univer-
sity's 1867 founding, when that land-grant institution offered instruction in the liberal arts.

The Stock Pavilion is a figural building--which means that it stands out from the surrounding
background structures--because of calculated design decisions. To understand the idea of what
makes a structure figural, think of a stop sign. A stop sign needs to stand out from its surroundings
to communicate an important message. To facilitate this communication the following principles
help make a stop sign figural: position, shape, color, contrast, and size. Similarly, a figural building
uses these principles to communicate importance. The Stock Pavilion is positioned at the end of
the South Quad--a long space focused on the Stock Pavilion's facade and its curved roof beyond. It
sits on a raised earthen platform so it is slightly elevated above the surrounding buildings. To fully
appreciate the Stock Pavilion's importance in this setting it is necessary to know that the original
design had a two-story, nine-bay loggia--an open-ended, exterior arcade or gallery--that ran the
length of its north facade. This terra cotta loggia was white and contrasted dramatically with the
surrounding structures. In addition, it was the largest structure in the vicinity when it was con-
structed in 1913 (see figures 2.1.4 and 2.1.5).

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 34
                                                                                                                                                                                                    1

The terra cotta facade--including fifty-two decorative terra cotta animal head panels --was
removed during a 1958 restoration because it had been badly damaged by water infiltration (see
figures 2.1.6 and 2.1.7).

  Though the structural systems of the buildings are different, it is interesting to imagine these two
  under construction at the same time and the subsequent facade changes that have given them a
  similar appearance.

the University YMCA's Dump and Run (a fall rummage sale and university tradition since 2004).
The space has a series of curved trusses that create a long span that allows for good sight lines for
spectators. The vault is visible from the outside and thus expresses the presence of people coming
together.

                          Figure 2.1.8. Stock Pavilion with Georgian front. Courtesy of the
                          University of Illinois Archives.

The building is strategically positioned on an axis with both Foellinger Auditorium and the Illini
Union beyond. This distinguishes it as a building that represents a group larger than a single
department. In the university's early history the three main areas of study were Engineering, Lib-
eral Arts and Sciences, and Agriculture. To this day, the university master plan protects sites
located on major university axes. Only structures that represent a larger, collective group are given
these positions of honor. Secondary structures line the east and west sides of the Main Quad and
the South Quad. As this walk moves north these buildings will be discussed (see figure 2.1.9).
Leaving the Stock Pavilion through the north doors one sees to the west Temple Hoyne Buell Hall
(Buell) and to the east the Agricultural Engineering Sciences Building (Ag Engineering). Both struc-
tures, built in 1992 and 1983, respectively, attempt to use historical references while creating con-
temporary buildings that move away from the traditional Georgian style. Buell and Ag Engineering
narrow the South Quad by using pathways that pinch in movement from the wider areas to the
north. Buell has a walk aligning with the entry to Gregory Hall (two blocks to the north) and allows
pedestrian passage through the structure. Similarly, Ag Engineering has walkways that redirect

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 38
movement from the north to its entrance or diverts pedestrians to the west past a large, orange
metal structure, Aurora I by sculptor Bruce White (1981).

             Figure 2.1.9. Engineering, Liberal Arts and Sciences, and Agriculture diagram. Read long
             description of figure.

Beyond Buell and Ag Engineering one comes to two remarkable structures, seemingly indepen-
dent, that have an entwined history. The first is a small farmhouse, one of the earliest univer-
sity buildings and the oldest surviving structure. The second is one of the newest on campus, the
McFarland Memorial Bell Tower. Both have a historic relationship to the military axis of the South
Quad.
The military axis is a long, thin cross axis that once linked the South Quad to a military parade
grounds that is now the area bounded by First Street to Fourth Street and Gregory Drive to

39 | Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the Farms
                                                                                                                                           3

  Peabody Avenue. This area was once the home of the Six Pac --six student dormitories located
  between Euclid Street and First Street--and an additional set of dormitories located between

                                                                        4

  Euclid Street and Fourth Street. The university is phasing out and replacing the Six Pac dormito-
  ries with new housing and student service structures in an area called Ikenberry Commons. There
  is a poetry in the building of these dormitories at the time of the cold war and the Vietnam War on
  land previously dedicated to military training and then to housing veterans returning from WWII.
  One senses the desire by university officials to mediate at a time when calls to "make love, not
  war" were part of a societal issue of tremendous controversy.

  The military axis has long been a concept for planning but never a clearly defined entity. Never-
  theless, numerous master plans have maintained the idea well enough to prohibit structures from
  inhabiting the area between Fourth Street and the South Quad. Plans have called for figural build-
  ings of equal square footage to bookend the military axis since the 1970s. The Funk ACES Library
  now occupies the short arm of the military axis and the distant termination piece is the Siebel
  Center for Design along Fourth Street. The Funk ACES Library is an octagonal brick veneer build-
  ing with stone accents and a copper roof. This postmodern building takes its design cues from
  the precedent that dominates the Main Quad: Foellinger Auditorium (which will be discussed in
  greater depth in part 2.2). Foellinger Auditorium is strategically placed on the high point of the
  Main Quad and has an elaborately figural front door facing it. Unfortunately, unlike Foellinger
  Auditorium, the Funk ACES Library lacks the presence of its precedent and has a main entrance
  away from the South Quad. Its octagonal shape is a transformation of the round Foellinger form
  that is then capped with an ornate copper crown. It is a much stripped-down version of its model
  without the remarkable ornamentation and commanding presence of the original.

  At what is now the western end of the military axis stands the Siebel Center for Design (SCD). Its
  creators asserted before the building opened: We believe in using Design Thinking as an approach
  to promote Human-Centered Design (HCD) and mandated quick iterations. We also aim to foster

                                                                                                         5

  multidisciplinary collaborations across campus. Reflected in this statement is the idea of bringing
  together cross-campus participants. The site is carefully located near the School of Art and Design
  but provides opportunities for many innovative interactions from participants in all the univer-
  sity's colleges. This collective aspect is a crucial reason for allowing SCD to be located on the mili-
  tary axis's western terminus. The new structure is rather short in comparison to its neighbors, but
  its irregular geometry reinforces its figural quality.

While turning toward Mumford House and the McFarland Memorial Bell Tower, it can be seen
that these structures are somewhat at odds with one another. This makes for an interesting study
of changing ideals and values over time. Mumford House was originally a small farmhouse near
experimental farms. The farmhouse was subsequently occupied by professors and deans for many
years, including the dean of the College of Agriculture, Herbert W. Mumford, for which it is named.
The small farmhouse is visible near the bottom of the early plan of the Illinois Industrial University,
which had imagined only a few buildings (see figure 2.1.10).
As the university grew, the Main Quad and the South Quad became demarcated by the structures
lining their edges and the landscaping that came to define their walkways. Numerous architects
were involved in shaping these, most notably Charles Platt and the University of Illinois professor/

43 | Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the Farms
collaborator James White. White introduced the idea of a tower on a site near Mumford House

                                                           6

(see figures 2.1.11 and 2.1.12).

In his proposal, White illustrated a tower similar to the bell tower in the Piazza San Marco in
Venice. Not only did this tower look like its predecessor in Venice but it worked in a similar man-
ner. Just as the campanile (bell tower) in Venice serves to turn the energy of the Piazza San Marco
and form a hinge linking the piazza to the Grand Canal, the proposed University Tower would have
marked the military axis and provided a similar hinge to link the South Quad to the parade grounds
in the distance (see figure 2.1.13).

Figure 2.1.13. Bell tower diagram. Figure-ground comparison of Piazza San Marco, Venice (left), and the South
Quad (right).

For decades the bell tower was not constructed, and Mumford House began to deteriorate. Con-
cerned students and faculty, determined to save Mumford House from being torn down, decided
to have it named a historic building. In 1989, the structure was placed on the National Register of

                                                                                                 7

Historic Places, listed simply as Farm House. It continued to fall into greater and greater disre-

6. Tilton and O'Donnell, History, 81.
7. National Register of Historic Places, "Illinois--Champaign County."

                                                                        Kevin J. Hinders | 44
pair until 2010, when it underwent emergency restoration. Additions that had accrued on the main
house were removed, and the building is now used as office space.

       Figure 2.1.14. Mumford Hall, west elevation. Note the oval window above the center doorway, the brick
       pilasters on the central area with the pediment, and the ornamentation around each entry. Courtesy
       of the University of Illinois Archives.

In spring 2000, a School of Architecture graduate studio conducted a series of design exercises
for the South Quad. One was to design a tower to be located in relation to the military axis and the
South Quad. University Architect Roland Kehe was invited to the project's review, which reignited
the idea for a South Quad tower. Completed in 2008, the new structure is dedicated to Sarah
"Sally" McFarland. Shortly before the placing of the bells, students pulled a prank, hanging a replica
of the Eye of Sauron (from J. R. R. Tolkien's novel The Lord of the Rings). No arrests were made.
Today, any climbing of the carillon is strictly forbidden.

  The McFarland Memorial Bell Tower marks three axes. It stands at the intersection of the South
  Quad and the military axis. It also creates a third axis: a vertical one that strategically marks the
  intersection of the two primary organizational design features. This tower acts as an axis mundi
  or world axis: "a line or stem through the earth's center connecting its surface to the underworld

                                                                                                                                  8

  and the heavens and around which the universe revolves." Typically one considers the axis that
  runs through the North and South Poles as an axis mundi about which the world spins. There are,

8. Merriam-Webster Dictionary, "axis mundi," accessed June 8, 2025, https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/
   axis%20mundi.
                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 46
  however, other ways to use this concept. Tall vertical elements, such as commemorative columns
  and obelisks--and in this case, a carillon--have long been used to mark important locations and

                                                                                                                                                                                                 9

  create local centers. These local centers provide organization and can help create order.

        Figure 2.1.16. Mumford and Kinley Hall comparison.

  Moving north, the next two structures on the South Quad are fraternal twins: David Kinley Hall
  (DKH) and Mumford Hall. At first glance these structures, both designed by Charles Platt, appear

9. To get students to understand the idea of an axis mundi I ask whether anyone has ever watched or listened to a sporting
   game and felt that their actions--such as getting up to fix a sandwich or using the restroom--might change their team's
   luck. Though intellectually one can be pretty certain that unrelated activities in one's own location--miles away from an
   event--do not have any effect on the game's outcome, it is still a question that produces blushing from many students.
   This is a psychological representation of the person's perception of themselves as the axis mundi--the belief that the
   world revolves around one's self. When we are infants this is natural, and as we mature we are taught empathy and the
   notion that we are part of a world but not the world's center.
   47 | Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the Farms
   identical. Closer inspection reveals notable differences, including in the entries, the central win-
   dows, and the presence or absence of pilasters. Each building has a center with a large pediment
   and implied pavilions on the ends (see figures 2.1.14-2.1.16).

   The architect used careful placement of both chimneys and downspouts to enrich the composi-
   tional strategies for these buildings as they look at one another across the South Quad.

   The space between DKH and Mumford Hall is quite wide. Tree-lined walks run parallel with the
   long buildings. Though these trees are substantial, they have not reached their maturity, which is
   significant. Once elms formed beautiful alleys of trees almost ninety feet tall that lined the cam-
   pus. They were one of the most striking features of the area. In the early 1950s Dutch elm disease
   caused the death of the trees:

           The University of Illinois was especially hard hit--a whopping 90 percent of campus trees
           were American elms. The elm catastrophe of the 1950s may be the single most devastating
           blow ever dealt to the beauty of the UI campus.

           A campus without trees was unthinkable. Experts such as horticulturist J. C. Blair and
           botanist C. F. Hottes credited trees with making the UI campus one of the nation's most
           beautiful. "Without them [the trees] to relieve the stern hues of the brick buildings and the
           monotonous level of the greensward, the campus would be as dull and uninteresting as the
           prairie," an observer remarked in 1908. . . .

           In a little over a decade, only 92 of the 1,850 campus elms remained, while all of the elms
           located on the Quad were wiped out.

                                                                                                                                                                              10

           Afterwards, the Quad resembled a blast zone (see figures 2.1.17 and 2.1.18).

   Alumni were greatly dismayed when they returned to their beloved university to find its character
   so much altered. Dutch elm disease caused the university to lose the building-landscape relation-
   ship that at maturity had created such a striking campus. Seemingly overnight the university had
   to reline its walks with trees. The new trees vary in species, much like those in other Midwestern

                                                                                                                                                                                                              11

   towns and cities, which have not planted singular species so as to avoid a similar catastrophe.

   Further north one comes to the primary libraries. The Main Library, again designed by Platt, has
   at its core a very clever idea. Instead of placing a single library in the geographic center of cam-
   pus, Platt decided that the nature of libraries, like the nature of research and knowledge, is to

10. Franch, "Memory Lane."
11. Today, the Midwest is confronted with a new arbor epidemic caused by the spread of the emerald ash borer. Millions

grow. Thus, the problem of the library and its placement becomes an issue of both convenience
and symbol. Symbolically the library represents and houses the knowledge accumulated by our
culture. Indeed, all the research being generated by this great research institution is documented
and aspires to be included in the library--the great repository of human knowledge. What hap-
pens when a centrally planned library needs to expand? The problem was neatly solved by Platt
when he determined to build two structures that would face one another across the South Quad,
just south of Foellinger Auditorium. Each library would then be free to expand to the east or west.
One imagines that just like DKH and Mumford Hall, these libraries would have been similar but not
identical.

Every first-year student at Illinois learns of the Morrow Plots, whether through a song--often
sung by the a cappella group The Other Guys--or an orientation that the university has the oldest
continuous-use experimental farming soil in the Western Hemisphere: the Morrow Plots. Instead
of following Platt's notion of turning the space opposite the Main Library into a site for another
library, the university recognized its importance for research and prohibited its development. In
fact, extensive work has been undertaken to ensure that the crops grown on this site are not
adversely affected by any construction in the area. As "Morrow Plots Song" goes: "You can't throw

                                          12

shade on the corn." The Carl R. Woese Institute for Genomic Biology (IGB), dedicated in 2007,
illustrates the concern for casting shade on the Morrow Plots. Careful massing studies and strate-
gic construction led to the IGB being set back from the Morrow Plots while subtly reflecting

12. Carl R. Woese Institute for Genomic Biology home page, accessed June 21, 2025, https://www.igb.illinois.edu/.
    49 | Part 2.1. The South Quad: Organizing the Farms
   stylistic aspects of the Main Library and Mumford Hall. In contrast to its more agricultural sur-
   roundings, the IGB houses a contemporary set of labs and learning facilities "where science meets

                      13

   society."

   Just to the north of the Morrow Plots stands the Astronomical Observatory, built in 1896. Despite
   its small size, its unique dome hovers above the plots as a sentinel searching the skies.

   Given the dilemma of desiring a central location and a need for expandability, it was decided to
   put the two-story Undergraduate Library underground. This choice allowed for both the symbolic
   centering of knowledge on campus and the opportunity for growth. The entrances flank a square
   central well that allows light down into the Undergraduate Library. The courtyard is known as the
   Edmund James Memorial Courtyard and contains quotations from his writings that focus on the
   importance of education, social service, and the power of books as sources of inspiration lining
   the cornice.

   The garden space has changed over time but still provides access to light and gives an opportunity
   for an outdoor break when studying. As new space is needed the library can expand either down
   or horizontally below grade--though today many books are housed off-site in storage facilities
   on Oak Street in Champaign. The collection has become very large--the forethought to plan for
   expansion has proven extremely wise. Overall the University Library is the fourth largest academic
   library by volumes in the United States.

   Today, much of the university book collection has been moved to Oak Street High Density Storage.
   The Oak Street Library at 809 South Oak Street now provides a full-service library circulation
   point and a research space for patrons. The Undergraduate Library closed in 2022. The space is
   being transformed into the Archives and Special Collections building. Allowing its keeping its use
   to match its symbolic location on campus.

   The rear facade of Foellinger Auditorium completes this walk. It is a mass of brick topped in cop-
   per. Though the rear of Foellinger is stark, it works with Gregory Hall and Smith Memorial Hall to
   close the South Quad. Paths on either side have a noticeable grade change leading into the Main
   Quad. It is interesting to note that the back side of Foellinger Auditorium used to be the location
   of the campus's most famous sculpture: the Alma Mater (relocated in 1962 by the Alumni Associa-

                14

   tion).
