Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the
Main Quad: The Symbolic Center

     About the Walk

     Foellinger Auditorium and the Illini Union create focal points for the Main Quad. These are places
     for literal and symbolic coming together. The entire Main Quad is the real and symbolic center of the
     campus. The use of a sloping topography toward Boneyard Creek and its axial placement establishes
     the hierarchical placement of Foellinger Auditorium, while the Illini Union's southern veranda sur-
     mounted by a decorative cupola terminates the Main Quad's north side. The secondary axis of the
     Main Quad is the Daniel Street corridor leading to Krannert Center for the Performing Arts. This
     cross axis retains the memory of an old tram system and a tremendous east entry called the Centen-
     nial Plaza, which has changed over time.
     This walk begins in front of Foellinger Auditorium on the Main Quad located at 709 South Mathews
     Avenue in Urbana and ends at the Illini Union located at 1401 West Green Street in Urbana.

       From the earliest colleges of the colonial era to the land-grant universities of the frontier,
       the American campus has been a simulated city that, with few exceptions, is distinctly
       unlike European models. Loose arrangements of freestanding buildings meld with the
       landscape to suggest an almost urban space. Not all campuses possess clearly defined
       quadrangles, but the ones that do seem to be more focused and provocative. They also

                                                                                                                                                                                             1

       represent the most tangible tradition of enclosed urban space in the United States.

The Illinois campus is organized using two fundamental strategies involving figural solids and fig-
ural voids. These can be considered using two historic prototypes: The Acropolis of ancient Athens
is a figural solid that organizes space around it, and the Forum of ancient Rome organizes struc-
tures around a series of spatial voids (see figures 2.2.1 and 2.2.2).

The University of Illinois relies significantly on these two strategies. The Acropolis's equivalent at

1. Dennis, Court and Garden, 237.

                                   Kevin J. Hinders | 52
Illinois is Krannert Center for the Performing Arts, which organizes the east side of the campus,
and the most notable Forum equivalent is the Main Quad (see figure 2.2.3).

                Part 2.2. Orientation Map.

53 | Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the Main Quad: The Symbolic Center
Figure 2.2.1. Plan of the Athenian Acropolis.  Figure 2.2.2. Plan of the Roman Forum.

Figure 2.2.3. Campus Solid Void diagram. Figural void: Main Quad;
figural solid: Krannert Center for the Performing Arts.

                                                                                                                         Kevin J. Hinders | 54
  The Main Quad is a spatial void defined by the presence of buildings at its perimeter. Conceptually
  one might imagine filling the entire Quad with water and freezing it--the buildings acting like an
  enormous ice cube tray--and then the "ice cube" created in the shape of the Quad can be visu-
  alized (see figure 2.2.4). The Main Quad plays a significant role in the overall organization of the
  campus.

                                                  Figure 2.2.4. Aerial Main Quad diagram.
                                                  Photograph courtesy of Mike Bohlmann.

  The theologian and philosopher John Henry Newman offers one idea for a way to organize a uni-
  versity:

          If I were asked to describe as briefly and popularly as I could, what a University was, I
          should draw my answer from its ancient designation of a Studium Generale, or "School of
          Universal Learning." This description implies the assemblage of strangers from all parts in
          one spot; from all parts; else, how will you find professors and students for every depart-
          ment of knowledge? and in one spot; else, how can there be any school at all? Accordingly,
          in its simple and rudimental form, it is a school of knowledge of every kind, consisting of
          teachers and learners from every quarter. Many things are requisite to complete and sat-
          isfy the idea embodied in this description; but such as this a University seems to be in
          its essence, a place for the communication and circulation of thought, by means of per-

                                                                                                                                2

          sonal intercourse, through a wide extent of country. The Main Quad, home to the College

2. Newman, The Idea of a University, 31. Contrasting with Newman's view is the expanding use of distance learning which
   relies on a virtual presence of "strangers" whose "one spot" is a shared computer website. Primary challenges are in
   55 | Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the Main Quad: The Symbolic Center
                                                                              3

          of Liberal Arts and Sciences, is the real and symbolic center of the campus. It provides
          the one spot where all members of the university can gather. The space hosts organized
          and impromptu events that speak to the life of the university. The Quad is created by the
          surrounding buildings gathered together for the good of all. Each structure contributes to
          the overall quality of the space. Just as the strength of a university is the coming together
          of many disciplines, the Quad symbolizes the coming together of the disciplines to form
          something greater than the sum of its parts. It is a physical manifestation of John Henry
          Newman's "assemblage of strangers from all parts in one spot."

                                       Figure 2.2.5. Author's sketch of Freedom from Want by
                                       Norman Rockwell.

   coming together and how meetings--formal and informal--can take place how people can meet one another and the
   felicity of interaction.
3. The College of Liberal Arts and Sciences encompass the two great branches of knowledge: moral philosophy and natural
   philosophy. This knowledge of liberal arts and sciences predicate human freedom. The Main Quad embodies free indi­
   viduals coming together to create something of value together.

                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 56
  As such it is like the family table from the Norman Rockwell painting Freedom from Want (see figure
  2.2.5).

  The patriarch (presumably the Illini Union) sits at one end with the matriarch (Foellinger Audito-
  rium) at the other. In the Rockwell painting, it is presumed the grandmother will take her seat at
  the opposite end of the table from the patriarch, just as Foellinger Auditorium and the Illini Union
  create focal points for the Main Quad. Note that these, too, are places for literal and symbolic
  gathering and represent the coming together of different groups--they are collective buildings. In
  the painting, the rest of the family and friends--the various departments such as those for English,
  foreign languages, chemistry, etc., along with the administration, are represented at the table. As
  is the case with some large family gatherings, not everyone can be seated at the main table. Some
  other departments, like children at a Thanksgiving feast, need to sit in proximity but cannot fit at
  the head table. The Quad uses the topography and slope toward Boneyard Creek to establish the
  hierarchical placement of Foellinger Auditorium at the crest of the south end. The Illini Union cre-
  ates a southern veranda to view the Quad. One imagines that after the carving of Rockwell's turkey
  the patriarch and matriarch in the image will take their seats to commence with the feast.

                                                                                                                                                           4

  Foellinger Auditorium in fact acts as a bookend to two quadrangles. As has been discussed in

4. In a 1906 preliminary report and letter to the University Board of Trustees, Frederick Law Olmsted, who was working
   with Clarence H. Blackall, wrote:

          We note that the Trustees have evidently planned to have two rows of buildings, with an open campus between
          them extending southward from University Hall. It is not so evident, but we judge that it must have been the
          idea that this arrangement would extend to the cross row of new permanent farm buildings, a distance of over
          2400 feet. Mr. Blackall proposes to interrupt this extraordinarily long campus by placing the new Auditorium in
          the middle of it. One justification for thus breaking the long campus in two is found in the low crosswise ridge
          that breaks the view. This ridge could be eliminated by grading, and it has been suggested that this should be
          done. The ridge, however, should be preserved, and its presence does justify, from an architectural standpoint,
          the forming of two quadrangles in place of one. The Auditorium is designed as a permanent monumental build-
          ing--one which will be a beautiful object, symmetrical, grand in scale, and sufficiently elaborate in detail to be
          interesting and effective, when fully completed, from whichever side it is seen. It is manifestly such a building
          as is thoroughly well adapted to a central symmetrical, commanding position in respect to all other buildings
          in the vicinity. It is not such a building as can just as well take a place in a miscellaneous row of buildings. In
          architectural character it will correspond in symmetry and ornamental effect to the great Low Library Building
          which forms the architectural center of the group of principal buildings of Columbia University, and therefore
          will probably be located in the middle of the great south campus. We unhesitatingly approve of the general loca-
          tion proposed. We have one recommendation as to its location in detail. This is that the center of the dome
          be placed on the center line (extended) of Nevada Street. Heretofore no attention has been paid to relating the
          University buildings to the axis lines of intersecting streets. This we believe is a great mistake. The University
          grounds and the surrounding street system should be considered together from the esthetic point of view. The
          opportunity to place this fine dome so that it will be seen at the end of one street at least should not be lost.
          In determining the position of the center of the Auditorium east and west, we advise as follows: Lay out along

   57 | Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the Main Quad: The Symbolic Center
  part 2.1, Foellinger works with the Stock Pavilion to frame the South Quad. On the Main Quad,
  Foellinger's main entrance presents the space with a refined portico that welcomes people to
  gather and participate in lectures, presentations, and events. The dome, long a symbol of gather-
  ing, is not the location of the speaker, but rather where the audience gathers.

                                                                                                                                                                                            5

  Foellinger Auditorium is modeled on an ancient Roman structure called the Pantheon. Foellinger,
  like the Pantheon, is situated as a focal point in a figural space. The ancient precedent is a purely
  urban model, while the Illinois interpretation integrates landscape features. The Pantheon is a
  masonry/concrete structural system requiring thick walls, while Foellinger Auditorium has a steel
  structure and a brick exterior--thus the difference in the wall thicknesses (see figure 2.2.6).

                 Figure 2.2.6. Comparison of the plans and images of the Pantheon and Foellinger
                 Auditorium. The buildings are shown at the same scale.

          the east side of the new campus a new avenue with a double row of trees to correspond with Burrill Avenue.
          Burrill Avenue is not parallel with the Agricultural Building (Davenport Hall) and the meridian. Therefore the new
          avenue should converge slightly toward the Auditorium so that it and Burrill Avenue will be precisely symmetri-
          cal about the meridian line. The position of this avenue we determine arbitrarily by assuming that the east row of
          trees should be 50 feet from the middle of the west front of the Agricultural Building. The Auditorium should be
          in the middle of the space between the two avenues with its axis upon the meridian line. Tilton and O'Donnell,
          The Illinois Campus Plan, 40.
5. The Pantheon is an archetype--an original pattern or thing that is then copied, transformed, or represented. The Uni­
   versity of Virginia's Rotunda Building, designed by Thomas Jefferson, is also inspired by the Pantheon.

                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 58
The facade of Foellinger Auditorium is also modeled on an ancient Roman archetype--the tri-
umphal arch (see figure 2.2.7).

                   Figure 2.2.7. Comparison of the Arch of Constantine and the Foellinger Auditorium
                   main entrance.

In the Pantheon model a front porch creates the entry to the cylindrical space and dome. Given
the need for a weather-enclosed foyer to Foellinger, a variation on a triumphal arch allows for a
grand processional entrance that celebrates the transition from the Quad to the hall while creat-
ing a dominant facade at the south end of the Quad's main axis. The architect has chosen to use
contrasting, two-story, limestone Ionic columns set in front of three arched, red brick entryways.
Similar to the Arch of Constantine, the facade is then embellished with decorative carvings and
details. To broaden the facade without causing it to become too high (which would block the view
of the dome) the columns are flanked by brick masses. These brick masses employ brick pilasters

59 | Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the Main Quad: The Symbolic Center
to frame windows in between. The two side masses work to establish a solidity to the edges of the
facade--effectively buttressing the composition and reinforcing the central axis and the voids for
the entry (see figure 2.2.8).

Heading north from the top of Foellinger's steps one descends to a plaza that overlooks the Quad.
The buildings lining the Quad respectfully sit back at a relatively uniform distance from sidewalks
lining both sides. Secondary spaces are then created between these walks and the buildings. Some
spaces incorporate yew hedges to mask bike racks or present extended entries to the Quad (Henry

                                                                                6

Administration Building for example).

Figure 2.2.8. Foellinger Auditorium façade diagram.

The Main Quad is eclectic in comparison with the South Quad. Like a city built over time, the
Main Quad is composed of buildings that vary stylistically. Beginning with Lincoln Hall and mov-
ing clockwise, one can take a tour of these styles. Lincoln Hall is well known for its terra cotta
reliefs that depict the life of Illinois' most famous US president. This large structure is a Midwest
American translation of an Italian palace. Stylistically it merges the Prairie School with its Italian
precedent. The dramatically oversized cornice at the top emphasizes the horizontal extension of
the roof to cap the building.

6. For basic history of the buildings on the Main Quad see Kalev Leeturev's UIHistories Project.

                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 60
  The English Building was originally designed as the Women's Building by the famous New York firm
  McKim, Meade, and White and opened in 1905. An addition in 1913 supplied the distinctive colon-
  nade that faces the Quad. The Georgian style of the original structure is somewhat overshadowed
  by this two-story colonnade and the adjacent towers.

  Henry Administration Building--originally the College of Commerce and Business Administration
  Building--has three distinct pieces and awaits a fourth. The first was built along the Quad in 1913
  and has a stone base with second and third stories composed of red brick. Two-story pilasters are
  crowned with ornate carvings located on the Quad side. An addition was built a year later along
  Wright Street. In 1964 an addition was placed on the south side of these structures. Currently a
  parking lot occupies the area just north of the building where an addition would complete the
  composition. With its stone base and repetitive bays, the building gives a nod to classical archi-
  tecture, but again translated to fit the campus.

  Before the Illini Union was constructed, the site was occupied by University Hall. As early as 1906
  landscape architect Frederick Law Olmsted Jr. had called for its replacement and had asked that

          Whenever University Hall is replaced by a larger and better building, the new University
          Hall should center on the axis of the Auditorium Building. The end pavilions of this future
          building should be designed so they will center one on Burrill Avenue and the other on the

                                          7

          new avenue.

  The building Olmsted describes is the Illini Union, whose construction was funded by the Works
  Progress Administration as part of a New Deal effort in the 1930s to employ people and create
  public works projects. Following Charles Platt's guidelines, the Georgian-style Union anchors the
  north end of the Main Quad. Even its name stresses the importance of the structure as a place
  to come together. The two cupolas, one overlooking Green Street and the other overlooking the
  Quad, stand as clock towers reminding students and faculty of the need for punctuality. At first
  glance, it is a symmetrical structure, and it was supposed to be just that. As early as 1906 the inten-
  tion was to eventually raze the adjacent Harker Hall and replace it with the Union's fourth and final
  pavilion.

                                                                                                                     8

  Harker Hall was designed by Nathan Clifford Ricker, the first graduate of the university's archi-
  tecture program (1873) and the School of Architecture's first head, and it is his first significant
  design. It is the second oldest building on campus (after Mumford House). This structure in the
  Second Empire style had a mansard roof until a lightning strike caused significant damage and it
  was replaced with a hipped roof. Because of its perceived significance, Harker Hall was placed on

7. Tilton and O'Donnell, The Illinois Campus Plan, 43.
8. Ricker chaired the architecture program 1873 to 1910.

the National Register of Historic Places in 1986. Even so, it was gutted and renovated in 1992 to
house the University of Illinois Foundation. Once Harker Hall was designated a historic place, that
ended the idea that the Illini Union would be completed as imagined (see figure 2.2.9).

                                      Figure 2.2.9. Plan of the Illini Union with Harker Hall (top)
                                      and plan of the Illinois Union with a fourth pavilion
                                      (bottom) as was originally conceived. The stylized block I
                                      becomes apparent.

Noyes Lab is one of only a handful of Richardson Romanesque buildings on the Illinois campus.
This style developed by Henry Hobson Richardson became immensely popular in the United States
from the 1870s to World War I. The Noyes Lab entry displays a typical Richardson Romanesque
portal--a low arch with rough-hewn stone surround, flanked by grouped arches. The rest of the
structure is less distinguished. By 1916 the Chemistry Department had outgrown its building and
an addition was made along Mathews Avenue. In 1930 the Chemistry Annex opened to serve the

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 62
needs of the expanding discipline and a tunnel was created to connect the two buildings. This tun-
nel runs under what was once one of the most beautiful exterior spaces on campus.
There exists a secondary axis to the Main Quad. Running from Krannert Center for the Performing
Arts in the east, through the Quad, and west along Daniel Street is a major organizing cross axis
(see figure 2.1.9). This cross axis has significant thresholds prior to entering the Main Quad. The
east threshold was redesigned to mark the 100th anniversary of the university. Timed to corre-
spond to the opening of Krannert Center in 1969, this aptly named Centennial Plaza has seen sig-
nificant decay over the past decades. The plaza between Noyes Lab and the Chemistry Annex is
marked by urns at the eastern entry. A taller wall once separated the two buildings and created
intermediate spaces used for bike paths and bike parking. This wall has been lowered to less than
3 feet because of life-safety issues (the wall was seen as a place where would-be attackers could
hide while awaiting victims--this very real concern is an indication of predicaments faced as one
designs spaces). The space once had maple trees alternating with lampposts and benches. The
trees lining the walk were all of similar variety and height. The trees eventually grew together in
such a way as to create a horizontal canopy of green punctuated by rhythmic voids that let in
columns of light. The sense one had when walking through it and onto the Quad was one of inti-
macy as the space compressed before expanding (see figures 2.2.10 and 2.2.11).

                   Figure 2.2.10. Centennial Plaza Drawing. Courtesy of the University of Illinois
                   Archives.

63 | Part 2.2. Liberal Arts & Sciences and the Main Quad: The Symbolic Center
But these trees have been dying, and it will be impossible to recreate that former splendor until
new trees are planted and then mature. To the west of the Quad the cross axis has a wide walk
framed by urns opening up onto Daniel Street. When the University of Illinois Bookstore was con-
structed, a stout tower was placed at the corner of Wright and Daniel Streets to help mark this
cross axis. The natural extension of the cross axis may eventually be when the university estab-
lishes a gate or portal at Fourth and Daniel Streets.

       Figure 2.2.11. Centennial Plaza shortly after completion. Courtesy of the University of Illinois Archives.

Davenport Hall has one of the largest footprints on the Quad. It uses the Palladian strategy of
a large central portico with two end pavilions linked to the center mass by a connecting wall of
classrooms and offices. The unique aspect of Davenport lies in the composition of the end pavil-
ions. Instead of emphasizing the pavilions' centers with a door or ornamented window, these
centers are downplayed. This curious decentering causes the viewer's eyes to be led around the
building instead of resting at a focal point.
The last building to consider is the Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building (LCLB; previously
the Foreign Languages Building). Many think it looks out of place. Built in the late 1960s and ded-

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 64
  icated in 1970, this structure is a simple brick mass that occupies the site of the old Entomology
  Building. The style associated with the structure has the unfortunate moniker Brutalist. Though
  stylistically and decoratively much different from the other buildings on the Main Quad, the LCLB's
  massing is similar in height to its neighbors, and it supports the spatial enclosure of the Quad.
  Extending the Rockwell analogy, one might think of the LCLB as a newcomer to the family trying
  to fit in.

                      Figure 2.2.12. Aerial photograph of the University of Illinois Urbana-Champaign's
                      Main Quad. Image courtesy of Mike Bohlmann.

  Knowing that elms once lined Burrill Avenue (now sometimes referred to as the Broadwalk) and
  that honey locusts once grouped together on the Quad somewhat changes the conception of the

Part 2.3. The Engineering Campus: A
Transformation of the Public Realm

        About the Walk

        The engineering campus includes the Bardeen Quad and the North Quad. Originally a set of unorga-
        nized structures, the area once referred to as "north of Green Street" has undergone a transforma-
        tion in the past twenty-five years. Structures that were previously independent and serviced by the
        street did not use to come together to form a collective space. The structures and landscape have
        been reorganized to create a series of interwoven spaces that tie back to the overall master plan.
        The redesign of Boneyard Creek and the creation of two shearing axes--one between the Illini Union
        and Kenney Gym and the other between Grainger Library and the Beckman Institute--are major
        accomplishments that add coherence to the public realm.
        This walk begins at the Illini Union located at 1401 West Green Street in Urbana and moves north
        through the Bardeen Quad and then on to the North Quad. The walk ends at the Beckman Institute
        located at 405 North Mathews Avenue in Urbana.

                                                                                                                                       1

          The role of the architect is to create order out of chaos.
          --Jerry Smith

          Welcome: The first experience that virtually any building should give the user is the expe-
          rience of welcome. It is natural for people when welcoming friends or family that they care
          for to hold out their arms and embrace the visitor. Buildings too can provide a sense of
          welcome to the visitor. This can be done by the building reaching out to "embrace" people,
          by providing shelter to them as they arrive, by making them feel safe and cared for when

                                                                                                                                                                          2

          alighting from vehicles, or by making the entrance and approach legible.
          --Jan van Dijk

   231717635/Experiencing-Architecture.

People at the university make a clear distinction between the areas north and south of Green
Street. Departments joke about the differences between the College of Liberal Arts and Sciences
and the College of Engineering. The differences are more than topics of study and research dol-
lars--though both certainly play a role. Their physical attributes differ as well. South of Green

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 68
Street the plan of two axial quads was established by the early twentieth century. North of Green
Street is a different story. Initially the zone associated with engineering and related studies had
incremental growth and expansion without a clear vision for the creation of public space. Because
campus buildings in this area were relatively sparse, engineering administration sought to expand
both east and west and eventually north.

       Figure 2.3.1. Engineering, Liberal Arts and Sciences, and Agriculture diagram showing primary and
       secondary axes. Note the manner in which Green Street separates the Main and South Quads from
       the engineering campus. Read long description of figure.

Green Street bisects a broad swath of building front yards. This space is only a bit narrower than
the Main Quad, which has prompted some thoughts of its closure between Wright Street and Lin-
coln Avenue.
The Illini Union, with its central portico and circle drive, prepares one for entry into the Main

  campus, it started with this walkway between Everitt Lab and Engineering Hall. Frederick Law

  Quad (now the Illini Union) should terminate Burrill Avenue with a pavilion. The Union uses this

strategy on the north side. An axis is then extended to Springfield Avenue and terminates in a

Because of pedestrian safety concerns in the late 1990s, the university explored the notion that
Green Street might be redirected underneath this walkway so as to create an at-grade pedes-
trian crossing free from vehicular traffic. An advantage of this proposal was the introduction of a
500-car multilevel parking garage under the lawn to the north of the Illini Union. Two issues were
considered insurmountable obstacles: the proximity of Wright Street to the crossing would have
demanded a steep ramp west of the walkway to create a viaduct, and an early twentieth century
access tunnel under Green Street east of the proposed viaduct would have to be reworked at great
expense. Other considerations were the elimination of the bus stop in front of the Union and the
character change that putting a subterranean parking structure in front of the Union would have
on the existing trees and landscape. The Green Street corridor was subsequently reworked into
its current configuration in 2018.

Broadwalk should have a visual terminus in an Illini Union pavilion- the south-west corner of the Illini Union. This motif
would then be repeated on the north side of the Illini Union.

  Moving north along this axis one comes to several landscape features. The first is a plaza between
  Everitt Laboratory and Engineering Hall. This plaza allows axial and diagonal movement across the
  space as students travel north and south through campus and in and out of Campustown. The walk
  then narrows to allow passage over Boneyard Creek. Boneyard Creek's entry to the campus from
  the west marks its emergence from under Wright Street and its first appearance in Urbana. The
  university celebrates the creek with a series of terraces that connect the upper landscape with
  the water. As the Boneyard's flooding has become more controlled, its presence on campus has
  become less intrusive and much more pleasant. Figures 2.3.3-2.3.5 illustrate the spatial sequence
  from Green Street into the Bardeen Quad. The visual termination of the axis is the mansard roof
  of the Kenney Gym Annex and its flagpole.

  Traveling north to enter the Bardeen Quad is compositionally interesting. Grainger Library is a

  The Bardeen Quad hosts two sculptures of two different scales: Mananaan by Alexander Liberman

                              6

  of raw steel. It weighs 15 tons and is 26 feet tall. Computing the Future is a human-scale bronze.
  A painted male figure sits adjacent to Grainger Library's south entry, forever reading a book enti-

  vide a focal point between the Mechanical Engineering Laboratory entry and Grainger Library. Its
  monumental scale is juxtaposed with the size of the Bardeen Quad and the people moving nearby,
  while Computing the Future sits casually trying to humanize the enormous library whose dedica-
  tion proceedings were entitled "Gateway to a New Era" (see figure 2.3.7).

As one approaches Springfield Avenue, a major shift in axis takes place. The Kenney Gym Annex
has terminated the north-south movement and a new axis is created to the east between a pavilion
of Grainger Library and the Beckman Institute to the north. Using the same strategy as the Illini
Union used to terminate the Burrill Avenue walkway to the south, Grainger sets its west pavilion
up to recenter the north campus (see figure 2.3.8).

The portico under a very low arch allows entry to the library from the north, which then recenters
the library patron to the center of the building. Meanwhile on the outside, the portico acts like
an enormous catcher's mitt, collecting the energy of the axis from the north. This energy is then
shifted slightly to the west, down the Broadwalk (see figure 2.3.1). Perspective views play an impor-
tant part in how the individual is helped to create a mental ordering for the spaces.
The axis from Grainger Library to the Beckman Institute is relatively new by university standards.
The Beckman Institute was constructed near the site of the first university building: Old Main Uni-
versity Hall. This space had more recently been home to a baseball field, and many adjacent sites
were vacant when the institute was completed in 1986. With the establishment of this axis it was
only a matter of time and money before the area would be further developed.

The Beckman Institute is a massive construction housing offices and labs: "The Arnold O. and
Mabel M. Beckman Institute for Advanced Science and Technology at the University of Illinois was
conceived early in 1983, when campus administrators decided to assemble a proposal for a unique

new venture in multidisciplinary research at the university."

The building forms an edge to the campus along University Avenue. On its south face, centrally
located and fixing the north-south axis is a five-story tower that houses conference rooms over
the main entry. The parti of the building is simple. Laboratories located on the north side are

7. Beckman Institute, "History."

                                                                                       Kevin J. Hinders | 78
linked to offices across a four-story atrium by eleven bridges. These bridges are both useful and
symbolic. Not only do they facilitate movement but they represent the very idea of the building:
that by separating these uses individuals might interface with their peers to create new and inno-
vative research opportunities. The atrium becomes like a main street and the bridges have fur-
nished lounges to encourage discussion and interaction.

For all its good intentions and laudable research the building has a fundamental flaw at the campus
planning level. It internalizes the campus instead of opening the university to its neighbors. The
extensive wall along University Avenue is devoid of public portals. Rather, the building turns its
back to University Avenue and the people to the north and even extends a metal fence to the west

79 | Part 2.3. The Engineering Campus: A Transformation of the Public Realm

  that runs to Wright Street. This offensive act has not gone unnoticed by its neighbors. Loss of the
  larger context also occurs inside the building. The axis does not terminate in a substantial flour-

  2.3.11).
  Though the tapestry is beautiful it is not at a scale appropriate with the axis of the Main Quad to
  the south. Behind the wall on which the tapestry hangs are fire stairs (see figure 2.3.12).
  These fundamental design oversights might be overcome should the university choose to address
  the problem. It has been suggested that a large-scale portal could be incorporated into the north
  facade, which would allow passage through the laboratory wing into the atrium. This gesture of
  welcome would be a more fitting representation of the idea of a university as a gathering place for
  all those interested in study, research, and service (see figure 2.3.13).

In the future, a plaza/gateway that incorporates the sculpture entitled Diss-Connections by John

One of America's greatest contributions to the design of the built environment is its university
campuses and their public spaces. It has been shown that campuses are forever a work in progress,
changing and adapting to meet the needs of the day and the hopes for the future. The engineering
campus provides insight into the transformation by incremental growth using strategies for the
creation of functional structures in support of attractive public spaces.

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 82
PART 3. THE UNIVERSITY CONTEXT

             Overview

             Perhaps unknown to many Illinois residents is a small town in Ohio named Urbana, which is
             the county seat of Champaign County. Years ago, as a reflection of the nation's attitude
             toward Manifest Destiny, a group of settlers left their home in west-central Ohio long
             before Horace Greeley's exhortation "Go west, young man." One of these settlers eventually
             saw fit to name Champaign County after his place of origin. Later, as a major railroad was
             being planned, an area of what was called West Urbana--now Champaign--was established.
             A discussion of territorial expansion based on the one-mile grid established as law under
             Thomas Jefferson (then secretary of state) and the Land Ordinance of 1785, which led to the
             Public Land Survey System, will contextualize the placement of both Urbana and Champaign
             and form the background for part 3.

The single greatest influence on the built environment in the United States was determined over
200 years ago shortly after the founding of the new country. Eager to provide a system for the
sale of lands that could supply the new nation with much needed funding, the Land Ordinance of
1785 laid the policy for land subdivision and subsequent sale. Thomas Jefferson, as a member of
the Continental Congress, wrote the Ordinance of 1784, which later became the Land Ordinance of
1785, passed by Congress. Jefferson's influence can readily be seen throughout Champaign County,
the Midwest, and the lands west of the Allegheny Mountains.

       The American Continental grid was originally established by the Continental Congress of
       1785. It provided a pattern of six-mile square townships subdivided into one-mile square
       sections. This pattern of land survey was conceived to bring about the orderly settlement
       of the expansive Northwest Territory, those western colonial lands won from the British in
       the Revolutionary War and ceded by the colonial states to their new national government.
       It was subsequently extended into all of the new territories that would one day make up
       the 48 continental United States.

       This grid constitutes the most pervasive urban-rural form in the United States. The trace
       of a giant-order Cartesian geometry on the landscape, it extends inexorably from the
       western slopes of the Allegheny Mountains north, south, and west to our nation's borders.

83 | Part 3. The University Context
         A matrix for both agricultural and urban development, the grid disappears only where the

                                                                                     1

         land is uncultivated or unsettled.

       Figure 3.1. Champaign County Township map 1875. Image from the United States General Land Office.
1. Hurtt, "American Continental Grid, 32." In this essay Hurtt elaborates on the inherent connection of Americans with the

  continental grid and its ties with Manifest Destiny and the expansion of township communities.
                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 84
  George Washington, in his first address to Congress on January 2, 1790, stressed the need for a
  uniform system of weights and measures. There were such discrepancies that measurements in
  one state were dramatically different from those in another. Without a uniform national system,
  trade was greatly inhibited and quarreling among states was common. Congress charged the first
  secretary of state, Thomas Jefferson (who had the role from 1789 to 1793), with the creation of a

                                                                                                                    2

  uniform system of currency, weights, and measures. Jefferson, recently returned from Paris, had
  spent five years in France, where he interacted with members of the Académie des Sciences. Jef-
  ferson was well suited to the task, which he undertook with enthusiasm.

                      Figure 3.2. Township Diagram.

  During the first half of 1790 Jefferson worked out a decimal system for weights and measures that
  predated the French adoption of its metric system in 1793. His system was plain and elegant as it
  tied distance, weight, and currency together. Both Jefferson and Alexander Hamilton recognized

                                                                                                                                                                                                     3

  that the units used to measure western lands would become the standard for the country.

2. Linklater, Measuring America, 101.
3. Ibid., 108-15.

   85 | Part 3. The University Context
For approximately ten years the process for measuring land for public sale was held up. The cause

of this delay was twofold: first, the Native populations banded together to create the Western Con-

federacy (composed of at least a dozen nations including the Miami, Wyandot, and Delaware). By

1790 the Western Confederacy had begun to inhibit settlers from moving into the area. Second,

                                                                             4

Congressional inaction was an issue. Without an agreed-on means, the plotting and measurement

                                                                                   5

of land based on a grid was impossible.

                Figure 3.3. Township Subdivision Diagram.

Three treaties signed in 1794-95 addressed one issue. After the defeat of the Western Confederacy

at the Battle of Fallen Timbers, one treaty forced "the Confederacy to acknowledge U.S. sover-

eignty over its land and to allow settlement up to a line from the mouth of the Kentucky River to

                      6

Lake Erie." The Jay Treaty with Britain (1794) gave the US the opportunity to occupy Detroit and

the northern frontier territory, while the Treaty of San Lorenzo gave the US freedom of navigation

4. Ibid., 118.
5. Ibid., 141.
6. Ibid.

                                                           Kevin J. Hinders | 86
  on the Mississippi River and access to New Orleans as a port. These three agreements allowed new
  settlers to move into western lands at a scale previously not possible.

  Meanwhile Congress had dithered with Jefferson's proposal and no new system of weights and
  measures had been approved. Though the French government had attempted to reach out to the
  US to share its new metric system in 1794, its emissary was not able to present the materials to
  Congress. Thus, the US missed the opportunity to become the second country in the world to

                                                           7

  adopt the metric system. Instead, Congress quickly passed "an Act for the sale of land of the
  United States in the territory northwest of the River Ohio, and above the mouth of the Kentucky

                 8

  River."
  With this legislation Congress opened up western expansion and determined the grid would be

7. Joseph Dombey was the emissary from France charged with introducing the metric system to the United States in early
   1794. A storm drove Dombey's ship, the Soon, off course and instead of arriving in Philadelphia Dombey was prevented
   from reaching America at all. He instead died in prison in the Caribbean in Plymouth Montserrat. Thus the message
   from Maximilien Robespierre and the correspondence from the Committee of Public Instruction along with a ceremo­
   nial copper metric rod and a kilogram weight were never formally presented to the US (see Linklater, Measuring Amer­
   ica, 133-42).

8. Ibid., 141.
   87 | Part 3. The University Context
measured in neither Jefferson's decimal system nor the French metric system. Instead it was mea-
sured using Gunter's chain, which was based on feet, inches, yards, and miles. Starting with Ohio,
the lands were surveyed with an unprecedented regularity that stretched a grid across the United
States.
To facilitate the measurement of the land the territories were divided into survey townships that
were 6 miles by 6 miles square. These townships were then subdivided by a 1-mile grid, which
created a 36-square-mile area with thirty-six subdivisions. The parallel east-west dividing lines
are called township lines, while the parallel north-south dividing lines are called range lines. This
measuring technique has left an indelible mark on the built landscape in America--and particularly
the prairie (see figures 3.2-3.5).

                                          Figure 3.5. Map of Champaign County townships.
                                          The range lines established on the 1-mile grid are
                                          the major north-south streets while the township
                                          lines are the major east-west streets. Once beyond
                                          the city these lines typically become county roads,
                                          such as County Road 1800 North at the top of the
                                          map. Augmented image from Google maps.

Champaign and Urbana are just two communities that owe their form to the ideals and pragmatics
of the continental grid. With the exception of precolonial and colonial settlements and the unique
instances of topography found in mountain ranges and dramatic floodplains, the grid has influ-
enced the selling of property and consistently forms the single most significant impact on the built
environment across the United States.

                                                                                                                                                               Kevin J. Hinders | 88
Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town

     About the Walk

     Champaign is a railroad town shifted off the US continental grid to align with railroad tracks planned
     in 1851. This grid shift occupies 80 acres (a quarter mile from Neil Street to First Street and a half
     mile from Washington Street to Springfield Avenue) of downtown Champaign. The only aberration in
     this shifted grid is University Avenue, which extends the continental grid through Champaign and
     divides the 80 acres in half. The downtown has seen three different train stations where this walk
     starts. This walk clarifies the rationale for the placement of Main Street, city hall, industry and the
     major public institutions including parks, churches, libraries, theaters, and the commercial center.
     This walk begins at Illinois Terminal, the Champaign Intermodal Transportation Center located at 45
     East University Avenue in Champaign. The walk encircles downtown Champaign and terminates on
     Main Street.

         Part 3.1. Orientation Map.

89 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
Champaign (originally West Urbana) is the product of the US continental grid, land speculation,
and the construction of the Illinois Central Railroad (ICR). Today, the continental grid envelops the
80 acres that originally formed the town (see figure 3.1.1).

 Figure 3.1.1. Champaign map in 2020 with the original 80 acres called out. The 80 acres are bounded to the
 north by Washington Street, to the east by First Street, to the south by Springfield Avenue, and to the west by
 Neil Street.

University Avenue runs parallel with Springfield Avenue--one of the mile-by-mile roads that marks
this grid and mentally ties Champaign back to the nation's capital in the east. This grid extends
south with Florida/Kirby Avenue, Windsor Road, Church Street, etc. and west with Prospect
Avenue, Mattis Avenue, Duncan Road, Staley Road, etc. until the grid is simply marked by a numeric
system of county roads in the rural countryside (as shown in figure 3.5). University Avenue was
originally known as East and West Main Street (confusingly, there was also a Main Street located

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 90
  two blocks north, which today is the two-block street still named Main Street). This grid line phe-
  nomenally connects the citizen back to Washington, DC, but not before passing Urbana, Ohio,
  which is the seat of that state's Champaign County. In fact, the naming of Urbana in Illinois traces
  back to this Ohio area through State Senator John Vance, who named it after his hometown in
  Ohio. Thus, one can trace the westward expansion of the US through Urbana-Champaign as the
  continental grid marches westward from Ohio to the Pacific Ocean.

  The town of Champaign is part of a system of railroad towns created by the ICR that were laid out
  12 to 15 miles apart so farms would be within a half day's journey.

          In 1850 Illinois politicians, including Abraham Lincoln and Stephen A. Douglas, pressured
          the federal government to agree to give Federal lands to the State of Illinois. These lands
          would be used as a financial incentive, through state-issued charters to companies, to
          build railroads. Financiers would put up the cash to build a railroad, and in turn, they got
          vast amounts of land to sell along their railroad's right-of-way. President Fillmore signed in
          1850 legislation so that states could issue charters of federal lands, and the State of Illinois
          chartered the Illinois Central in 1851. It gave the proposed owners 4,060 square miles of

                                                                                      1

          land, nearly 10 percent of Illinois.

  The ICR originally planned to connect Cairo in southern Illinois with LaSalle and then to Galena
  in the northwestern part of the state. Senator Stephen A. Douglas owned land south of Chicago
  and got a change in the original charter to construct a branch to connect Chicago via east central

                                                                                                                   2

  Illinois to the main line at Centralia (see figure 3.1.2).

  Because of the topography, cost, or land acquisition it was decided to locate the railroad several
  miles west of Urbana. At first, this town was called West Urbana and was little more than a muddy
  slough, but with the building of the railroad came speculation and growth. In 1861 Champaign
  incorporated as a separate local government.

  New towns along the ICR tracks share remarkable characteristics. Because of the need for the rail-
  road to connect and move through the landscape with the greatest ease, the tracks do not coin-
  cide with the continental grid. As a result there exists an interesting juxtaposition of the grid of
  the town with the tracks and how this grid interfaces with the continental grid. The purchase of
  land for the railroad typically postdated the sale of land to settlers in Illinois. Speculators often
  purchased farmland with the hope that the railroad would choose a specific path and then would
  purchase their land at an increased cost while surrounding land would also increase in value. As

1. Chagnon, History of Railroads, 1. In this way the influence of two of America's great presidents can be seen in the creation
   of the built environment in Champaign. Jefferson's grid is ubiquitous while Abraham Lincoln assisted the ICR in it legal
   endeavors to negotiate the creation of railroad lines across the state.

2. McCollum, "Champaign."

   91 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
  an example, Colonel M. W. Busey purchased 80 acres for an estimated $100 in 1848 that were sold
  to the ICR for $1600 in 1852. This purchase was bounded by Washington Street to the north, First
  Street to the east, Springfield Avenue (Springfield and First Street denote the continental grid) to
  the south, and Neil Street to the west. The tract is bifurcated into two 40-acre tracts by University
  Avenue. With the exception of University Avenue, downtown Champaign does not adhere to the
  continental grid. The railroad, after purchasing this property, created four streets to run parallel
  to the tracks: Oak Street, Locust Street (east of the tracks), Chestnut Street, and Walnut Street
  (west of the tracks). In Champaign, Oak Street is no longer present in downtown but can be seen
  both to the north and to the south. In the city center Oak Street was absorbed by the railway when
  its right-of-way was expanded to the east. Up and down the ICR route this naming pattern for
  streets is repeated with few exceptions. Sometimes the railroad chose to develop only one side
  of the tracks, and so only two streets are named for trees in Buckley, Illinois, but the pattern is

                                                                                            3

  remarkable (see figures 3.1.3A and 3.1.3B).

                                                    Chicago branch.

Finally, before beginning a tour of Champaign it is appropriate to address the manner in which a
railroad divides a community. A quick search of the Internet reveals a myriad of songs addressing
the presence of two sides of town. In the song "The Leader of the Pack" the Shangri-Las lament
the social divide:

       My folks were always putting him down

                                                                                                                  4

       They said he came from the wrong side of town.

James Brown, Guy Clark, Motörhead, Iggy and the Stooges, Mary Chapin Carpenter, and Sting are
just a few of the musicians (and their represented genres) who have used "the wrong side of the
tracks" to describe this social and physical divide like the Kinks do:

4. "The Leader of the Pack," lyrics by Jeff Barry, Ellie Greenwich, and George Morton.

                                                                                        Kevin J. Hinders | 94
          We're just slum kids, and we know it,

          And we never stood a chance.

          We were dragged up from the gutter,

          From the wrong side of the tracks.

          So how dare you criticize,

          When you don't know what it's like

          To be dragged up from the gutter,

                                                                                         5

          From the wrong side of the tracks.

  This divide is both real and symbolic. It derives from a very simple, practical condition. In any
  town, physical aspects determine the layout. Given that railroad towns are relatively flat where the
  tracks come through, the topographical influence in Illinois is minimal, so the major determinate
  to a desirable side of the tracks is most often determined by the direction of prevailing winds. In
  Champaign County the prevailing winds move west to east. It was common practice for the more
  affluent to live upwind of the tracks to avoid the smoke and pollution produced by the trains and
  industry. This condition is repeated throughout the US. Thus the west side of the ICR tracks are
  where the commercial and more affluent residences are located, while the factories and less afflu-
  ent are on the east side. Amusingly for Champaign residents, this pollution is sent east to the peo-
  ples of Urbana.

  We begin at the flagpole outside the Illinois Terminal, a multimodal transportation hub that links
  train, bus, pedestrian, and automobile traffic. From this spot one can see three Champaign train
  stations. They are like the three bears from the Goldilocks story. The Baby Bear is the oldest exist-
  ing station. It was built in 1899 (the architect of this station was Francis Bacon, supervising archi-
  tect of the ICR from the mid-1890s to 1907) following a fire that destroyed its predecessor. This
  station is now located at 320 North Chestnut Street and was moved using log rollers from its pre-
  vious spot so that a new station could be dedicated in 1924. This small station was renovated in
  2015 and is now a popular restaurant. The Momma Bear is a 1924 neoclassical station located at 116

                                                     6

  North Chestnut Street. This served as the station until 1999 when the Papa Bear, the new Illinois

  Moving west along University Avenue, the first structure on the south side is the Traction Building,
  constructed in 1909. It operated as the Illinois headquarters of the Illinois Traction System, later
  the Illinois Terminal Railroad Company (ITC), which was an interurban electric railroad with both

                                                                                                               7

  passenger and freight business in central Illinois. Champaign lines ran to Danville from 1902 to
  1953 and to Decatur from 1907 to 1955. These lines linked to rail services that extended to Peoria
  and all the way to St. Louis. The service also had an affiliated set of electric streetcars that pro-
  vided local service in many cities. With the growth of the automobile and highway systems the
  passenger traffic on the ITC greatly declined, and the ITC was eventually bought out and absorbed
  into larger rail freight providers. Its successor is the Norfolk and Western Railway.

   In 2025, the city boasted the number of locally owned bars and restaurants in downtown Cham-
   paign numbered 35 and the number is growing as the city continues to recover from Covid 19 dif-
   ficulties.

   Champaign owes its resurgence to a calculated investment in bars and restaurants that reversed
   the negative connotations of downtown. The city now balances a "work by day, play by night"

                                12

   atmosphere.

   One block north of University is Park Avenue. Park is the most common nonnumerical street

                                           13

   name in America. From Neil moving west on Park Avenue one crosses Randolph (northbound)
   and State (southbound) Streets. These streets were made one way to facilitate movement past the
   city. Intended to speed vehicular traffic, this street couplet allows people traveling by car to bypass
   downtown. Together with the pedestrian mall, they aided in the deterioration of the downtown
   built environment. Traffic engineering may sometimes consider the speed of movement while not
   taking into account the repercussions to the quality of affected places they affect (see part 3.3).

   Between Randolph and State Streets lies the beloved Virginia Theatre at 203 West Avenue. The

                                                   14

   Virginia, built in 1921, has operated as a vaudeville house, movie house, and theater and concert

                   15

   venue. The 1800-seat structure has a stylistically elaborate Italian Renaissance exterior and
   Spanish Renaissance interior like many of the historic Fox Theatres across the US that feature
   elaborate fantasy retreats for movie patrons to enjoy. These theaters, built in the 1920s, appealed
   to the public as they created a backdrop where the adage "the show starts on the sidewalk" could
   be realized. Many of these theaters came on hard times during the Great Depression in the 1930s
   and even more found themselves the victims of urban blight and urban renewal in the 1960s and

14. The Virginia Theatre was designed by C. Howard Crane and Kenneth Franzheim.
15. Virginia Theatre, "History."

    101 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
1970s. Adding to these problems was the calculated business move of suburban theaters to build
multiplexes capable of showing movies on multiple screens and sized to accommodate varying
numbers of patrons. These suburban competitors often outbid downtown theaters for the right to
show premier movies, which left downtown theaters without movie draws to fill their large spaces.
Today, many of these surviving theaters have been restored by communities seeking to preserve
these palaces of entertainment.

                                      Figure 3.1.10. West Side Park Statue: Prayer for Rain by
                                      Edward Kennys.

Now owned by the Champaign Park District and fortified with grants and donations, the Virginia
offers movies, plays, and concerts. The decaying 1940s marquee was replaced in 2011 with a replica
of the 1920s original. Nearby sits a statue of movie critic Roger Ebert, thumbs up, commemorating
his commitment to his hometown (he was born and raised in Urbana and is a University of Illinois
alumnus). Every April the Virginia hosts his Ebertfest film festival (see figure 3.1.9).

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 102
   Park Avenue leads from the Virginia Theatre to a four-city-block, 12.7-acre park simply named
   West Side Park. Had the county courthouse been moved to Champaign--a plot thwarted in 1897--it
   presumably would have been located in this space. At the park's center is a large sculpture foun-
   tain featuring a Native American. Prayer for Rain by artist Edward Kemeys was dedicated in 1899

                                          16

   (see figure 3.1.10).

   Other notable features in the park include the Lincoln Megalith, several statues and plaques com-
   memorating the Black Hawk War, a memorial to police and firefighters, and the sculpture Double

                                                                                                17

   Dutch-A Jump for Joy by artist Gary Bibbs. The park serves as a place of both passive and active
   recreation. Students from nearby Central High School still use it for physical recreation classes
   on occasion. A playground located in the northeast quadrant is often used by preschoolers from
   nearby day care centers. Today West Side Park is bordered by structures with numerous uses.
   Originally two types were prominent: residences and churches.

   The residences surrounding West Side Park were grand affairs. Mansions lined University Avenue
   as it stretched westward and two notable homes still sit along Elm Street on the park's west edge,
   although both have been turned into funeral homes. The large, Queen Anne style, white clapboard
   Ross Mattis House at 201 North Elm Street is now used by Heath and Vaughn Funeral Home, while
   the Owens Funeral Home at 101 North Elm Street occupies the classical, brick and stone Trevett
   residence (Trevett was a business partner in the Trevett-Mattis Bank whose facade remains at 112
   West Church Street). At one time, large homes with grand interior living spaces on the first floor
   and numerous rooms above were popular for renovation into funeral homes in many Midwestern
   cities. One can speculate that although a person may not have been able to afford to live in such a
   grandiose home when they were alive, they could have the opportunity to have a temporary stay
   when deceased.

   The appropriately named Church Street on the park's north side has two prominent structures at
   the intersection with State Street (see figure 3.1.11).

   On the northeast corner is the First United Methodist Church and to the west is the First Presby-
   terian Church. Farther west, a block past the park is the First Church of Christ, Scientist. On the
   southern side of the park is the Emmanuel Memorial Episcopal Church and one block south of the
   park is Holy Cross Roman Catholic Church. In addition, the First Baptist Church of Champaign was
   located at the northeast corner of Randolph Street and West University. The First Baptist congre-

   gation moved to Savoy in 1981.

Churches in the Midwest are often strategically located adjacent to commercial areas. Six-days-
a-week cities thrived on business but Sundays in the Christian tradition were reserved for rest.
Businesses were closed and "blue laws," which restricted certain activities, often limited sales of

sons. First is the deterioration of downtown. Second is the need for automobile access. Third is the desire to locate
closer to congregation members as people moved from downtown. Last is the need for expanded facilities such as
schools and day care centers, which require more land than what was available in a downtown. In some cases, the down­
town facility would remain and a new offshoot would be created to serve the community in a new location. In Cham­
paign, for example, St. Matthew Roman Catholic Church was formed by members of Holy Cross Church in the 1960s
on 10 acres of farmland donated for that purpose. St. Matthew's school was constructed by 1964, followed by a church
sanctuary in 1978.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 104
    alcohol. On Sundays church attendees could rely on the parking spaces used the other six days a
    week for commerce. A synergy existed between religion and commerce.

    Two other building types once held a very important presence on West Side Park. The Burnham
    Athenaeum at 306 West Church Street was built in the classical style with two-story Ionic columns
    at the entry in 1896 from a donation by Albert C. Burnham. The building was used by the Cham-
    paign Public Library until 1977 when it moved to a larger facility at 505 South Randolph Street
    (designed by Hammond Beeby and Associates) and then moved again in 2008 when a new library
    (designed by Ross Barney Architects) approximately three times larger than its predecessor was
    opened near the same location. The Burnham Atheneum was converted by the law office of Meyer

                                                                                                                    19

    Capel, which has occupied the building since 1980. At the northwest corner of Elm and Church
    was a mansion that in 1938 was converted to the McKinley YMCA. Though replaced as the commu-
    nity YMCA in 2010, the YMCA's presence added to the community for 72 years. The new Stephens
    Family YMCA, located in suburban Champaign, replaced the aging structure that had been out-
    grown. Significantly, the Stephens Family YMCA building is not located near downtown Cham-
    paign.

    The demolition of the McKinley YMCA building in 2019 was the result of a Champaign Central High
    School renovation and expansion project. The choice to "keep Central central" was a rallying cry
    that helped the Champaign Unit 4 School District get financial backing to purchase properties that

                                                                                          20

    doubled Central High School's acreage.

    Libraries, churches, social organizations, and parks form a significant community aspect of a city.
    Together they represent the ideals of providing for a healthy mind, healthy body, and healthy spirit
    adjacent to the downtown business district. This set of community values was reflected in the built
    environment created by Champaign's founders and its philanthropists. Each edifice symbolized a
    community's commitment to its citizens through both private and public gestures.

    With the passage of time, a community's values can change and so can its needs. Changes along
    Church Street/Main Street in Champaign reflect the city's physical adaptations to societal shifts.
    Returning toward the train station along Church Street one sees a large brick and stone building
    sited at 301 North Randolph Street at the Church Street intersection. Owned by the Champaign
    Park District since 1991, this building is the Springer Cultural Center, where cultural, recreational,
    and educational programs for all ages as well as workshops, lectures, exhibits, and performances
    are offered (see figure 3.1.12).

19. After Meyer Capel began converting the structure an arsonist set a fire that damaged the interior and roof in October

20. The McKinley YMCA property was not the only noteworthy structure to be razed. Community outcries to "save the
     Burnham" along with two other homes were not enough to keep the Burnham mansion at 603 West Church Street from
     demolition. Preservationists were unable to secure financing to move the structure to a nearby vacant lot.

     105 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
           Figure 3.1.12. Old Post Office, now the Springer Building. Architect: James Knox Taylor.

   This structure was formerly the US Post Office. Constructed in 1905 in the Beaux-Arts style, this
   building has seen numerous additions and alterations. Its main floor sits almost a full story above
   the street and is accessed by a prominent staircase to the marble lobby. As the US Post Office, this
   building was created for handling mail and to provide a symbolic presence of the federal govern-
   ment. Changes in mail distribution, including shipping accessibility, led to the need for new facili-
   ties. In 1966, after it was no longer used as a post office it was converted for alternative functions
   and took on the name the Federal Building, reflecting its historic use and symbolic presence. The
   solidity of the structure, built of brick and stone and raised above street level, was a conscious

                                                                                                                                                                                21

   effort by the federal government to pronounce its solid presence in Champaign.

   To the direct south of the Springer Cultural Center is a large parking lot. This site was once the
   home of a multistory garage that had a bridge connection over Randolph Street to Champaign's
   largest department store: Robeson's. Signs of the bridge can still be discerned in the brick work
   on the building's west facade. Robeson's served Champaign for 116 years and was the last family-

21. Federal presence was important to the citizens of a community. Stories exist of towns in Texas where the federal gov­
    ernment built oversized courthouses to intentionally bring peace to the West. Within a short time, the wild character
    was replaced with civility as ruffians were forced to either abide or move on.
                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 106
   owned department store in Illinois. Robeson's closed in 1990 because of the competition from big

                                                   22

   box stores and malls. The building is now leased to a variety of businesses on the ground floor
   with office space above.

   On the north side across from the former Robeson's location is the Art Theater, originally known
   as the Park Theater. Built in 1913 specifically for cinema, it originally seated 600 people. With alter-
   ations and remodeling it shrank to seat 250 people. The Art has had an interesting history as it
   has adapted to the times. From 1971 to 1986 it was an adults-only movie house. In 1987 it reopened
   as the New Art Theater and began showing art and foreign films to a devoted audience. In 2012 it
   became a co-op and in 2017 it merged with the Art Film Foundation to become a recognized non-

                                               23

   profit organization. The Art Theater regularly played independent, foreign, and short films until
   October 2019 when it closed. Although the owner tried to locate another theater operator to lease
   the building, after ten months and with the COVID-19 pandemic still requiring social distancing
   he determined there was no market for a theater in the structure and came to the conclusion to

              24

   sell. In 2025 it remains vacant and for sale.

   Farther east across the parking lot is the facade of the Trevett-Mattis Bank, which became the
   Bank of Illinois in 1963. This facade was removed when the building was demolished and then was
   incorporated into the design of the M2 Building when it was constructed at 301 North Neil Street
   from 2007 to 2009.

   The intersection at North Neil Street, Church Street, and Main Street has undergone a dramatic
   transformation that reflects the resurgence of downtown Champaign since the early 1990s. This
   five-corners intersection was home to the Flatiron Building, also known as the Gazette Building,
   between Neil and Hickory Streets from 1909 to 1969. The Gazette Building was torn down to pro-
   vide parking for nearby businesses such as Sears and Roebuck (which soon moved out to the new
   Market Place Mall by Interstate 74 in 1975). Eventually Hickory Street was removed and the current
   park/parking lot is the result. One Main Plaza and M2, created and managed by One Main Devel-
   opment, occupy the two northern sites. The mixed-use One Main Plaza, completed in 2004, was
   designed with ground-level retail, second- and third-story offices, and twenty-three condomini-
   ums above, while the nine-story mixed-use M2 Building has commercial and office activities on
   floors one through four with living units above. M2 has a connection to the 123 West Hill Street
   parking deck, which was built by the city to consolidate parking away from surface lots to allow
   for continued downtown growth.
