     107 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
    The newest addition to this intersection is the Hyatt Place Hotel, which opened in 2014. The hotel
    is constructed on a lot that had been vacant since a cold November day in 2008 when a fire
    destroyed the handsome Italianate Metropolitan Building (built in 1871), which was just finishing a
    substantial renovation.

    Between Neil Street and Walnut Street are shop fronts and former sites of two notable businesses.
    The News-Gazette and the First National Bank had provided services to the Champaign community

                                                                      25

    from this block for decades. Originally the Urbana Union, founded in 1852, the newspaper
    became the News-Gazette when David W. Stevick merged his Champaign Daily News with the
    Champaign Daily Gazette in 1919. The News-Gazette's Stevick Building was completed in 1984 and
    today contains offices and communication systems. The paper was printed in nearby buildings
    between Walnut and Chestnut Streets across from the 1924 (Momma Bear) train station for almost

                           26

    100 years. Before the trucking industry replaced railroad usage, materials for the News-Gazette
    and other industries were brought into Champaign on railroad spur lines. Though the spur lines
    are mostly gone, their presence can be seen through placement of the buildings that ran paral-
    lel to these tracks east of Walnut Street and north of Main Street. Most of these structures were
    warehouses. The buildings' orientations are an anomaly. They hold neither to the railroad grid nor
    to the continental grid. Further evidence of the past steam locomotives is the base of the water
    tower near the termination of North Chestnut Street. Water stored in the tower supplied the rail-
    road's steam engines.

    Today, newspapers are no longer a primary source for news for many. With the rise in digital
    media and the ability to obtain news on demand, the News-Gazette is changing. Less advertising
    and fewer classifieds have affected the manner in which the paper is produced. The Stevick News-
    Gazette building was auctioned in the summer of 2020 after the offices relocated to Fox Drive in
    Champaign.

    The First National Bank Building, located at 30 Main Street, was until 2019 occupied by a suc-
    cession of banks. When built in 1910, it boasted a 25-ton steel safety deposit vault. This vault
    is encased by a five-story building with a solid rusticated base topped by four-story Corinthian
    columns. Its solid appearance was a vital aspect of the bank's intention to communicate that the
    institution was sound and therefore money invested with the bank was secure. For many patrons
    the idea was simple: This building looked like a bank (see figure 3.1.13).

25. News-Gazette, "History and Origin."
26. The News-Gazette was printed in Champaign at 48 West Main Street for almost 100 years. The last printing was Sunday,

    June 25, 2017. The paper is now printed in Peoria and trucked to Champaign (Kacich, "Century of Printing").

                                                                                                                                                                   Kevin J. Hinders | 108
          Figure 3.1.13. Former First National Bank Building. Architect: Jenney and Mundie.

It was built to last. Its enormous vault displays and represents that security. Over the years, the
look of banks has changed dramatically. The stock market crash in 1929 led to the Great Depres-
sion and failure of over 9000 banks by 1933. The Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation was cre-
ated to give the banking industry greater stability. Over time, the sense of security for a bank came
not so much from its appearance as from its depositor's assets being insured. Thus freed from the
need for the structure to symbolically represent stability to the community, banks have evolved.
Following World War II a typical interior became much more modern in style. Today, the architect
Louis Sullivan's famous credo "Form ever follows function" is reflected in the utilitarian nature of

109 | Part 3.1. Champaign: Railroad Town
                                                                    27

many post-World War II banks. Thus a fast food restaurant, a post office, and a bank all have a
similar-looking front counter and the architecture may not distinguish the use. Through acquisi-
tions and mergers, the occupant of this building eventually came to be PNC Bank in 2008. In 2019
PNC Bank relocated, and it seems the image presented by the 1910 facade no longer fit with PNC's
branding as a friendly financial institution. The building is currently home to a restaurant.

Today one may walk down Main Street and imagine the bustle of activity as passengers would
come off the trains on their way to the Inman Hotel or visit Kuhn's Clothing Store (now Jos. Kuhn
and Co.). Moving past remaining cast-iron storefronts from the late nineteenth century, such as
at 34 Main Street, one sees a rehabilitated area that still shows signs of a time not so long ago
when there were multiple pawn shops in what was a rough part of town. This is underscored by
the police station's viaduct that leads from its new station directly to the east end of Main Street.

New shops, restaurants, and bars have replaced the vacant storefronts so prevalent before the
concerted effort to encourage visitors. Outside seating is enjoyed during pleasant weather. The
optimism and growth of the early twentieth century is being replicated a century later as down-
town Champaign experiences a renaissance and structures either adapt or are replaced to accom-
modate the needs and desires of its inhabitants.

27. Sullivan and Athey, Kindergarten Chats, 201.

                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 110
Part 3.2. Urbana: County Seat

       About the Walk

       Though located just under 2 miles from downtown Champaign and the senior city of the two,
       Urbana (38,336) is less than half the population of Champaign (88,302). As the Champaign County
       seat, Urbana has the county courthouse and a newer federal courthouse. Unfortunately, the city
       made a series of decisions that center around Lincoln Square Mall that have caused a general lack of
       cohesion in the downtown environment. Though public amenities such as Main Street, the court-
       houses, fire and police stations, the farmers market, churches, and a post office exist, the superim-
       position of an enclosed urban mall and its vast parking lots has disassociated the institutions from
       the neighborhoods and reveals the delicate balance Midwestern towns maintain between their
       neighborhoods, their landscape, and their institutions.
       This walk begins at the Champaign County Courthouse at 101 East Main Street in Urbana. The walk
       encircles downtown Urbana before returning to Main Street.

         During the first fifteen years after Illinois became a state in 1818, forty-five new counties
         were created through the grassroots efforts of proponents of local government. The pio-
         neer spirit was marked by self-reliance, independence, and the desire to control one's
         destiny; these characteristics were reflected in the establishment of county governmental
         seats. The settlers' enlightened self-interest led them to recognize opportunities to

                                                                                                                                                            1

         advance their own ambitions in concert with community benefits.

         The nonhierarchical growth and expansion of township communities can be seen to derive
         from an insistence on a direct relationship between the single individual and supreme
         powers. In Puritan and Quaker ideology, there is an insistence on a direct relationship
         between man and God without a mediating hierarchical structure. This ideal religious
         order was a new model for an ideally nonhierarchical governmental order. Therefore, when
         a community or township grew to the point that a new settlement needed to be formed,
         the new community split off entirely from the older one and became whole and indepen-
         dent in and of itself. As a mode of self-government derived from English experience in

1. Baker and Miller, Commemorative History, 13.
  111 | Part 3.2. Urbana: County Seat
which the monarchy modified a hierarchic structure to suit itself, this pattern of cellular

subdivision and self-government resisted the institution of a formal structure between the

                                                                             2

individual and the highest authority.

  Part 3.2. Orientation Map.
2. Hurtt, "American Continental Grid," 35.

                                            Kevin J. Hinders | 112
  To understand Urbana one must understand it as a product of two activities. The first was the
  exploration of the territory from the Mississippi River and the second is pioneer movement from
  the east, originally along the Ohio River. The settlement pattern in central Illinois was consistent:
  settlers chose to live near groves of trees. These groves provided resources for building homes and
  were located near water sources yet had sufficient elevation to avoid most flooding. In Champaign
  County there existed Sadorus Grove (Sadorus), Mink Grove (Rantoul), Bowes Grove later known
  as Shaeffer's Grove (near Philo), and Linn Grove (Sydney). The towns of Middleton (now Mahomet)
  and St. Joseph also owe their origins to their stands of trees and the local waterways (see figure
  3.2.2).

  Figure 3.2.1. Illinois County boundaries diagram. 1816, 1824, and 1835. 1816: The eleven counties with primary
  settlements along the Wabash, Ohio, and Mississippi Rivers. The waterways were the original transportation
  routes in Illinois. 1824: Growth of counties in the southern portion of the state. 1835: Champaign County and the
  expansion into the northern half of Illinois.

                                                                                                                                                                                                                    3

  The largest of all these groves had a simple and apropos name: Big Grove (originally 13,000 acres),
  and it was located near the geographic center of the county (a requisite for a county seat). Early
  white settlers found the area attractive and by 1833 they had established Urbana as the county
  seat of Champaign County, which was formed from land previously associated with Vermilion

                                                                                                        4

  County and was the sixtieth county in Illinois. Senator John Vance had proposed that this new
  county be split off from Vermilion County and named it after his home in Ohio, which had as

3. History of Champaign County, 14.
4. When Illinois became a state only 15 counties existed. Twelve were in the southern portion of the state. Crawford

   County, which accounted for approximately one-third of the total acreage of the state, eventually contributed to part or
   all of over thirty-five counties.
   113 | Part 3.2. Urbana: County Seat
                                                                                 5

  its county seat the town of Urbana. The precise siting of the new county seat was not without
  intrigue. At the time of its formation, the largest population in the county was located at Big Grove.
  The north side of Big Grove offered numerous reasons for making it the logical location for the
  county seat: it had the only governmental building in the county, the Van Buren Post Office, and it
  was near Fort Clark Road, the only public road in the county.

   "level, open country" (Peterson, "Glacier Runs through It").

          Accounts indicate that the Locating Commissioners, having met at the home of Phillip
          Stanford, left there giving assurances that the north side site met all requirements for the
          county seat. However, at the invitation of Isaac Busey, the Commissioners accepted his
          hospitality overnight. The next morning, a stake identifying the site of the county seat was
          placed on the south side of Big Grove, near Isaac Busey's house. On June 21, the Locating

                                                                                                                                                                                6

          Commissioners met and filed a report official fixing the location of Urbana.
  The Busey family (brothers Isaac and Matthew, along with their nephew Colonel Matthew W.
  Busey) was one of several landholders who sought to gain by the placement of the town of Urbana
  closer to their property. Though the population was sparse, the growth provided by a county seat
  was attractive to landowners. The history of western expansion into the Ohio Territories and later

                                                                                                                              7

  the Louisiana Purchase is a history of land speculation. Whatever occurred that night at Isaac
  Busey's home, it enriched the Busey family's holdings. Another example of the Busey family's spec-
  ulation is property purchased with an eye on capitalizing when the railroads began to make their

  prairie appearance.

   We start this walk at the Champaign County Courthouse located at 101 East Main Street. The

                                                                                                                                                                                         9

   stately structure is built of red sandstone in the Romanesque style (see figure 3.2.3).

           Figure 3.2.4. Federal Courthouse on South Vine Street.

   This stone and steel structure was built in 1901--that is, it was added onto in 1901. By the turn of
   the century the ICR had significantly increased the size, affluence and population of Champaign,

                                                                                                           10

   population 9098. Urbana's population was 5728. The County Board wanted a new courthouse but
   wanted to avoid a county vote on the idea--which almost certainly would have shifted the loca-
   tion of a new courthouse to Champaign. The board conspired to keep the courthouse in Urbana.
   In 1898 they devised a simple scheme to make certain a vote would be avoided. The board initiated
   a plan to create a building repair to the 1849 brick and wood structure, which would not require
   public approval. Joseph Royer, an 1895 graduate of the University of Illinois School of Architecture,

 9. Henry Hobson Richardson (1838-86), one of America's greatest architects, employed this style that came to be known as
    Richardson Romanesque. It remained popular into the early twentieth century.

   was commissioned to "repair" the structure and his design called for the incorporation of one wall

   from the existing building.

                          Figure 3.2.5. The Federal Courthouse in Urbana Illinois sculpture Sky Cathedral
                          by Linda Howard.
11. Adams, Joseph William Royer, 18.

    117 | Part 3.2. Urbana: County Seat
   The 1901 courthouse originally cost the county $150,000 and had a 135-foot-tall clock/bell tower
   that was repeatedly struck by lightning. Eventually the tower was lowered to 85 feet. In 2006, after

                                                                                                                                                                   12

   fundraising of $1.54 million, the tower was rebuilt to its original height. The clock/bell tower
   marks the symbolic center of the county and can be seen from miles away. It also symbolically
   links the courts with the celestial heavens--though given the lightning strikes, perhaps too much
   so.

   Today the building is entered at 101 Main Street through an addition to the 1901 structure built
   in 2003. The addition is a functional facility that incorporates both offices and courtrooms. The
   original entry has been abandoned because of security and accessibility concerns. The new entry
   meets basic requirements of the Americans with Disabilities Act and has metal detectors to pro-
   tect access. In 1998, Judge George Miller was attacked in his courtroom by an irate individual hurl-

                                        13

   ing a fire bomb. Within days the sheriff had a metal detector installed. Very soon after it was
   realized that up until that time probably a good number of weapons had entered the courthouse.
   Whether it was these incidents or the domestic terrorist attack in 1995 on the Alfred P. Murrah
   Building in Oklahoma City (known as the Oklahoma City bombing) that prompted the change in
   the entrance, the courthouse no longer displays the open sense of a place to gather that repre-
   sents the people of the community. In this, the Champaign County Courthouse reflects a national
   shift due to antiterrorism protections.

   Next door to the Champaign County Courthouse is the US Courthouse, located at 201 South Vine
   Street. This brick and stone veneer building with a steel and concrete masonry structure was con-
   structed in 1994 and is stylistically postmodern. This courthouse is home to the US district, bank-
   ruptcy, and magistrate courts. Office space is also provided for court-related agencies, including
   the US attorneys, marshals, probation officers, and public defenders. Out front is the necessary
   flagpole and a public statue by Linda Howard entitled Sky Cathedral (see figures 3.2.4 and 3.2.5).

   In 1962 the Kennedy administration instituted a policy of including fine art in public buildings.
   Since then the General Services Administration, the agency responsible for federal public build-

                                                                                                                                                                      14

   ings, has taken over the role of overseeing the Art in Architecture Program. Funding for each Art
   in Architecture project is at least .5 percent of the building project's total estimated construction
   cost. This funding goes toward the designer's services and costs of fabrication and installation.
   Sometimes the art may be incorporated directly into the design of the architecture, such as a floor

                                                                                              15

   or ceiling or a plaza or landscape scheme. Sky Cathedral is part of this program.

12. Huang, Labrosse, and McCormick, "Champaign County Courthouse."
13. Schenk, "Much Has Changed."
14. General Services Administration, "Art in Architecture Program"; General Services Administration, "GSA Art in Architec­

    ture Policies and Procedures," 2.
15. General Services Administration, "GSA Art in Architecture Policies and Procedures," 6-7.

                                                                                                                                                                   Kevin J. Hinders | 118
Adjacent to the US Courthouse is an expanse of parking. This lot is used on Saturdays for the
Urbana farmers market, which operates every Saturday from May through October. Though the
farmer's market is very popular with venders and the public, this is a large area of the city given
over to a parking lot used only once a week, part of the year. The parking lot was originally created
to serve the adjacent Lincoln Square Mall, which was under construction from 1961 to 1964.

Lincoln Square Mall was built in response to a perceived stagnation in the Urbana economy. Res-
idents had mixed feelings about the project. It promised economic growth but also required the
closure of three public streets: Green Street and High Street between Race and Vine Streets
and South Broadway Avenue between Elm and Illinois Streets. With these closings the city and
developer were able to establish a 20-acre parcel for a new mall. A few structures on the site
were allowed to remain. These include Howard Apartments at the intersection of East High and
South Race Streets and the First United Methodist Church. Significantly, the Urbana Lincoln Hotel,
designed by Joseph Royer and opened in 1921, was integrated into the new construction. This
Tudor revival hotel faced the intersection of Green and Broadway. In 1927 a statue of Abraham Lin-
coln by Lorado Taft was placed adjacent to the hotel entrance. This statue was moved to Carle
Park on completion of the mall, and the hotel's main entrance was reworked to the perimeter of
Race Street.

The mall was planned by a nationally recognized developer named Victor Gruen for Carson Pirie
Scott and Company, which also provided the anchor store for the three-story complex. Offices and
twenty commercial firms were also provided. The mall was touted as the first weather-enclosed
shopping mall in Illinois outside of Chicago. Green Street and Broadway Avenue literally extend
into the mall as its main axis and cross axis. For two decades the mall was a commercial success
but by the mid-1980s competition was drawing customers away, and in 1988 Carson Pirie Scott
sold the mall. Since then it has had both Bergner's and Herberger's (closed in 2002) as the main
anchors, but after remodeling the tenants have included the county government and health care
providers. Numerous owners have come and gone.

So what went wrong? Retail patterns shift as consumers change their shopping habits. Compe-
tition from other strip malls and big box stores contributed to the decline in business. The mall
was essentially a suburban prototype placed in a city environment. It lacks the interstate highway
traffic that Champaign's Market Place Shopping Center provides. As shopper numbers declined
the sense of vacancy within the space increased, which led to greater store vacancies and a trou-
blesome spiral. Just as in many small towns affected by peripheral developments, the mall's "main
streets" with anchor stores were reduced to slots for niche shops with discounted lease rates. The
old Urbana Lincoln Hotel, which was incorporated into the mall's design, continues to struggle and
the city struggled for years to find an owner who can keep the hotel operational (see figures 3.2.6
and 3.2.7). In 2025, the hotel reopened as the Royer Hotel, a part of the Hilton Tapestry Collection.
It features a restaurant called The Writer's Room, and an indoor pool.

Figure 3.2.6. Urbana Figure-Ground circa 1955. The     Figure 3.2.7. Urbana figure-ground circa 2017. The

On a formal front the mall and its street closings did Urbana's present downtown few favors. The
area is now an eight-city-block zone adjacent to the Main Street corridor, which effectively cuts
off the neighborhoods to the south, east, and west. Just as Champaign reversed its exterior mall in
1988 it is perhaps a matter of time before Urbana reintroduces Green Street and Broadway Avenue
as through streets to reconnect Urbana's residents back with their city.

On the southwest corner of West Elm Street and Race Street is the beloved Urbana Free Library
(see figure 3.2.8).

As with many important buildings in Urbana, the structure was designed by Joseph Royer, and
it was constructed in 1918 in the neoclassical style (contrasting with the Tudor revival Lincoln
Hotel he designed across the street five years later). The library expanded in 1975 and again in
2005 (with design by Isaksen Matzdorff Architects). The first expansion was a three-story inter-

                                                                                                                                                                                                16

national style addition while the 2005 renovation incorporated a classical revival style. These
choices reflect the periods in which the additions were undertaken. The original 1918 structure
built during World War I uses a neoclassical style commonly associated with public buildings at
that time while the modern, international style addition executed in 1975 reflects the US culture's

16. Ma, Stotler, and Priester, "Urbana Free Library."

                                                       Kevin J. Hinders | 120
move toward modernism after World War II. Classical revival reflects the eclectic nature of archi-
tecture in the beginning of the twenty-first century.

       Figure 3.2.8. Urbana Free Library. Architect: Joseph Royer.

Moving north on Race Street takes one to the intersection with Main Street. Looking west, Spring-
field Avenue and Main Street split to form a triangular space formerly occupied by the Flatiron
Building. This structure acted like a western bookend to the city with the Champaign County
Courthouse anchoring the east. It had a small replica of the Statue of Liberty at the divide. It is
interesting to imagine that Urbana's Main Street moved off the continental grid to connect to
its Main Street "cousin" in Champaign. Springfield Avenue remains the township grid street that
extends, figuratively speaking, all the way to the State Capitol Building in Springfield.
From this intersection it becomes clear that Urbana is sited on high ground adjacent to the Bone-
yard Creek. Looking north and west, the topography drops off and the east-west Norfolk Southern
Railway line lies several blocks north where it crosses over Boneyard Creek. This line has its east-
ern terminus in Urbana.
Across the street from the former Flatiron site is a large Busey Bank branch. Sitting back from its
Race Street edge to provide customer parking, this structure does little to help Main Street cre-
ate the needed spatial closure to reinforce Main Street as a place. Busey Bank, now a part of First
Busey Corporation, is descended from a financial institution founded by some of the earliest set-

121 | Part 3.2. Urbana: County Seat
tlers of Urbana. It is now headquartered in Champaign and operates in Illinois, Missouri, Florida,
and Indiana.
The one block of Main Street Urbana, between Race Street and North Broadway Avenue is an
eclectic collection of commercial activities and styles. Care has been taken to provide a well-land-
scaped area for pedestrians and patrons of the cafes and restaurants. This is the heart of old
downtown Urbana. The Princess Theater once occupied a prominent location between Race and
Broadway. In the 1930s it was owned by Alger Brothers, who also owned and operated the Art The-
ater in Champaign at the same time. Built by the Busey family in the 1870s and remodeled on many
occasions, the building has been home to a bank, an opera house, a theater, and offices. Currently
the structure is used as a gallery. Many of the buildings here were once shop houses that had com-
mercial activity at the street level and families living above. Over time these businesses shifted and
changed while the upper stories often become office space. An example of this is the architect's
office occupying the space above a shoe store at 102 West Main Street.

         Figure 3.2.9. Old Urbana Post Office.

Near the courthouse exist two noteworthy structures: the old Urbana Post Office and a parking
garage. The 1914 structure is a classical, two-story building raised above street level. The entry
is framed by two-story Corinthian columns and pilasters (five bays) to create a presence on the

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 122
   prominent north-south axis, Broadway Avenue. The structure now pairs with the Hotel Royer to
   form an entrance to Lincoln Square Mall (see figures 3.2.9 and 3.2.10).
   Most of the post office's functions have been removed and the building now serves as office

                  17

   space. Just west of the Champaign County Courthouse on Broadway is a two-story parking
   garage. Urban legend has it that this garage's structure was designed with extra supports so as to
   accommodate vertical expansion. Because of an incredible amount of surface parking in the area,
   the garage remains a one-story edifice with parking on the ground floor and the roof. Fortunately,
   it supports a pedestrian environment by filling out the block and providing a street edge that helps
   define Main Street. This spatial enclosure is critical to the pedestrian experience. Unfortunately
   for Urbana and many other towns in America, the concessions given to the automobile to attract
   people to downtowns have created environments less desirable for pedestrians.

           Figure 3.2.10. Urbana Post Office and the hotel forming the north entry to Lincoln Square Mall.

   This walk ends where it began, near the Champaign County Courthouse. Looking around Main
   Street one sees additional support needed by a county seat. Numerous law firms occupy first- or
   second-floor offices. Inevitably banks are located near the courthouse. This was, and is, practical

   as people coming to a county seat to purchase land and record deeds once needed local finan-

   County, almost always have banks on nearby corners. In Urbana, what is now an office building
   named County Plaza was once one of the two largest banks in Urbana: Champaign County Bank

   and Trust. This five-story building at 102 East Main Street replaced a number of smaller struc-

   nearby Montgomery Ward building. The bank had a large parking area to the south, designed more

   Courthouse provided bookends to Urbana so did Busey Bank and the Champaign County Bank and
   Trust in both form and function.
   In Urbana one sees the struggle between the automobile and the pedestrian. Like most Midwest-
   ern towns created in the early to mid-nineteenth century, the automobile had no presence and
   the residential area was an integral extension of the downtown. Today Urbana exemplifies how the
   form of the city continues to evolve.

Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place

     About the Walk

     In 1995 Chancellor Michael Aiken stated that he was embarrassed to bring guests of the university to
     Campustown. In response came a series of committees and projects known as Campustown 2000.
     Politics can be seen as having a major role in the creation of place. This walk will trace the
     approaches to Campustown 2000 by the three major groups involved: the cities, the university, and
     the shop and property owners. Long perceived as being in a standoff, these three groups had their
     own fears and agendas for Campustown. The development of a strategy for Campustown improve-
     ment will be examined and the Campustown master plan and its subsequent changes to the area will
     be illustrated. The chapter will also discuss the role of drainage and the Boneyard Creek and its
     retention and channelization.
     This walk begins at the Alma Mater statue located near the southeast corner of the Wright Street
     and Green Street intersection. The walk continues west on Green Street to explore the area adjacent
     to the campus.

Part 3.3. Orientation Map.

125 | Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place
                                                                                                                                                                                                                    1

  In 1966, film director Sergio Leone released his third spaghetti western starring Clint Eastwood.
  The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly had each character defined in the opening credits. Toward the
  end of the film the protagonist, a "Man with No Name" played by Clint Eastwood (the Good), has
  a memorable duel with two other characters, Tuco Ramirez (the Bad), played by Eli Wallach, and
  Angel Eyes (the Ugly), played by Lee Van Cleef. The duel begins as each character desires access to
  a gravesite where $200,000 in gold is buried. Since each has a gun, there is a standoff. Presumably,
  whoever is the first to draw will be the next to be shot, which would leave the patient person as
  the winner. No one can be certain they will not be the first one shot, but they assume that the first
  to fire will certainly be the second target. The question of patience is a question of greed as well.
  Significantly, in this standoff, the viewer identifies with the `good' Man with No Name.

  By the mid-1990s a similar standoff had occurred in Champaign's Campustown area. The area had
  been long neglected because of the positions of the three main characters: the University of Illi-
  nois, the City of Champaign, and the Campustown property owners and merchants. Here though,
  the protagonist is not obvious.

  In 1996 David Dressel, Director of Facilities, Planning, and Management, explained to the author
  the Campustown standoff: "The University and the City of Champaign are often at odds given
  the University's need for property and its tax-exempt status. If the University acquires property,
  that property goes off the city's tax roll. The city is understandably wary of making investments
  in an area where the tax base could continue to erode. The City of Champaign likewise may be at
  odds with the local merchants and (perhaps) the larger population of the Campustown area. The
  city having finite resources often chose to support upgrades in other parts of the city rather than
  invest in Campustown improvements. Some suspect this is due to the number of non-registered
  (in Champaign) voters compared to other parts of the city. Thus, other areas of Champaign receive
  preferential status as a symptom of the politics of government. The Campustown property own-
  ers and merchants had long endured a perceived secondary status to other areas of the city. The
  Campustown area produced a substantial tax base for the city while few finances were directed to
  the perpetual flooding and infrastructure needs of the area."

  The university may have had the perception that local merchants and property owners were
  exploiting students. Rents in the Campustown area (within ten blocks from the Illini Union) seem
  to support this view as the cost of student apartments in the area are often two to three times
  greater than comparative size units elsewhere in the cities of Urbana and Champaign. This brings
  to mind the realtors' adage exhorting "location, location, location." Unlike Leone's The Good, the

1. The first film of the trilogy was A Fistful of Dollars (1964) and the second was For a Few More Dollars (1965). Although the
  movies were marketed as a trilogy by United Artists, the stories are independent of one another. The main character in
  all three was rarely called by name so United Artists decided to package the films as the "Man with No Name movies."

                                                                                                                                                                Kevin J. Hinders | 126
  Bad, and the Ugly, there is no protagonist in Campustown planning. It is more about having differ-
  ent points of view.

  In 1996, however, the standoff ended when Chancellor Michael Aiken participated in a panel dis-

  owners, 50 property owners, 12,700 University employees and 37,000 University students, will
  meet in the near future to consider how to raise money and complete the talking stage before pro-

  ject momentum dies."

  Working together they seized the opportunity to form the Campustown 2000 Task Force: With
  representatives from the university, city officials, and area business and property owners four
  subcommittees were formed: Transportation and Traffic; Safety; Financing; and Drainage, Public
  Improvements, and Appearance Subcommittees. These subcommittees reported to the Campus-
  town 2000 Task Force. The city took responsibility for a traffic study and hired Bucher, Willis, and

                                                                                                 4

  Ratliff Corporation for a six-month survey. The university took responsibility for the Drainage,
  Public Improvements, and Appearance Subcommittee, which resolved to try to create a set of
  design guidelines.

  "The mission of the Campustown 2000 Task Force is to facilitate the improvement of the Campus-
  town area to make it more conducive, accessible, and desirable for attracting students, visitors,
  and local residents as a quality environment for conducting commerce and residential develop-

  ment."

  The Drainage, Public Improvements and Appearance Subcommittee concluded that "a new image
  for Campustown should be developed. In addition, a procedure to maintain this image should be
  created. Recognizing the resources of the University's School of Architecture, a proposal for a
  multi-disciplinary team was suggested. A faculty member and four graduate students from the
  disciplines of architecture, landscape architecture and urban and regional planning were selected

Dressel explained to the author: "It was a good solution for us. A faculty led group of students
working on the project could be `completely deniable.' If the group produced good results the Uni-
versity would look good. If the group produced results contrary to what the University wanted the
administration could discern that this was an academic exercise."

The group was hired for two months and worked in the Facilities and Planning Offices above the
Illini Union Bookstore on Wright Street. An early task of the design team was to determine the
area of study. Some wanted the Campustown area to be widely defined, stretching from Univer-
sity Avenue to Windsor Road and from Neil Street to Lincoln Avenue. For political reasons, it was
determined that this study would concentrate on Champaign but that a Campustown area would
also be created in Urbana in the future near Krannert Center for the Performing Arts to serve the
east side of campus (this has become the Gregory Street development between West Oregon and
West Nevada Streets). Eventually the design team chose to concentrate on a smaller area. In its
plan, the team wrote:

       The area of the Campustown Commercial Core and Green Street Corridor as outlined
       by the Campustown 2000 Drainage, Public Improvements and Appearance Subcommittee
       extends from Neil Street to Wright Street and includes parts of Daniel Street (between
       Sixth and Wright), John Street and Healey Street (between Fifth and Wright). This design
       team has chosen to concentrate on the Green Street Corridor and the north-south streets
       that intersect the corridor.

       Through observation and a survey, the design team learned that the visual and aesthetic
       quality of the Green Street Corridor decreases as one moves from Wright Street to the
       west. In order of preference, people surveyed chose the area between Sixth and Wright
       Streets as having the highest visual quality, and their opinion is that the aesthetic quality
       decreases as one progresses westward.

       There are numerous reasons for this recognizable shift in aesthetic quality. Historically the
       Green Street Campustown area was one block in length located between Wright and Sixth
       Streets. A shift occurred in the fifties and sixties as this commercial strip began to expand
       westward. In response to this expansion zoning changes were made, and in the early 1960s
       parking was removed from Green Street. At this same time Green Street zoning west of
       Fourth Street was changed from B-4 (requiring no parking) to B-3 (requiring a percentage
       of on-site parking). This action lowered the burden of providing public parking for busi-
       nesses to the west of Fourth Street but has had detrimental effects on the visual and aes-
       thetic quality of the area.

tecture), Jason Gustaveson (urban and regional planning), Kenneth McCown (architecture), and Laura McKane (archi­
tecture) led by Kevin Hinders.

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 128
         The buildings built since this zoning change are seen as objects, free standing buildings,
         typically unattached to surrounding structures, often set back from the property line.
         Buildings to the east, in contrast, create an enclosing system by using party wall construc-
         tion and limiting building setbacks to a uniform distance. This system allows the viewer to
         experience the street/sidewalk area as an outdoor room. As one progresses to the west,
         the spatial enclosure decreases, thus lowering the quality of the corridor as "a place."
         The University of Illinois provides a clear border for the east end of the Campustown,
         but no clear boundary exists to the west. (The Illinois Central Railroad does not act as a
         boundary because it is too far away.) Without the benefit of a clear demarcation, the Green
         Street Corridor loses definition as it moves to the west. Thus, the Green Street Corridor
         has become a variety of individual destinations without an overall identity. The challenge
         to the design work team is to create a vision of Campustown which facilitates both vehicu-
         lar and pedestrian movement while fostering a sense of place for residents, merchants and

                                7

         visitors.

Campustown's Green Street was then broken into three sections, each with distinct characteris-
tics. From Wright Street to Fourth Street was the commercial hub, the densest area. From Fourth
Street to First Street was an area with a mixture of freestanding commercial buildings with their
own parking and apartments built typically to their lot lines. From First Street to the railroad
tracks was an area of mixed single-family homes, a drive-up commercial strip, and a corner gas
station. It was determined that these areas were distinct enough that a gateway at each would be
appropriate. The train viaduct east of Neil Street would be the first marker.

                                     Figure 3.3.2. Campustown 2000 Sketch of First and Green.
                                     Top: 1996. Middle: Campustown 2000 proposed massing
                                     to create a First Street gateway. Bottom: As seen in 2020.

At First Street from the west would be another appropriate spot to differentiate Campustown and
give the area an identity. Pedestrian markers would begin here with distinct banners and a clear
threshold.
The desire to stretch Campustown's identity from Wright Street to First Street helped define the

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 130
project. It was decided that there were too many vacant lots and not enough edge definition to
give Campustown a cohesiveness. Thus, the area was perceived as incoherent and lacking identity.

The design team was challenged to accomplish three goals by the Drainage, Public Improvements,
and Appearance Committee: to develop a functional and aesthetic vision for the area, to generate
street and facade guidelines based on that vision, and to recommend a procedure for the estab-
lishment and work function of an architectural review board. In reality, many area merchants were
concerned with a recent development on Green Street: Businesses were in an arms race to put up
larger and larger signage, in particular backlit awnings that almost everyone agreed were eyesores.
Many property owners and businesses wanted some kind of regulation to control these imposi-
tions into the streetscape. Most preferred to have this regulation after their own "improvement"
had been made, however.

Having defined the area of investigation the design team created a survey that elicited 177
responses. The survey was essential. First, it provided useful input to help prioritize action and
verify the members' observations. Second, and very important, it was undertaken with the intent
to provide much sought after verification that Green Street in this area should be a destination
rather than a vehicular corridor connecting Urbana and Champaign. The traffic study that had
been scheduled to take at least six months took over eighteen months. This timing gave the design
team the opportunity to be proactive with information and insights they supplied to the Trans-
portation and Traffic Subcommittee. By providing details to the Campustown 2000 Task Force
and its subcommittees the team was able to preempt the possible exploration by traffic studies to
use Green Street as an automobile arterial. The survey also assisted the designers in prioritizing
responses to a number of identified safety issues. The survey was able to both guide the team's
investigation and justify its approach.

One other issue had been affecting Campustown for years: flooding. One-half block north of
Green Street flows the Boneyard Creek. More often than not the Boneyard is a shallow trickle.
Most of the watershed begins in north Champaign, heads to the Salt Fork, and then travels to the
Vermilion, Wabash, Ohio, and Mississippi Rivers before emptying into the Gulf of Mexico. In 1996
it was still considered a navigable waterway by the Army Corp of Engineers. However, time and
progress meant there were big impacts when heavy rains occurred:

       More impermeable infrastructure combined with growth in the campus area and C-U at
       large created a perfect storm for more frequent, more damaging flooding. The flood of
       1979, which damaged millions of dollars of property, was one of C-U's loudest wakeup calls.
       Intense flooding regularly overloaded the drainage system in the coming decades. Even
       when we tried to forget the Boneyard, it forced us to remember. In 1993, a flood halted the

131 | Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place
          construction of UI's Grainger Library and effectively shut down the University for some

                                                                                                            8

          time, causing millions of dollars of damage.
  In addition to the water from north Champaign moving through Campustown, the area's water-
  shed was increased. An area known as "Lake Neil," located west of the Illinois Central Railroad on
  South Neil Street, often flooded. This water would eventually percolate into the soil, but as devel-
  opment along Neil Street continued to produce impermeable pavement the problem grew. The
  city reengineered the area in the mid-1980s to drain into Boneyard Creek. This additional water
  exacerbated the flooding in Campustown. With a heavy rain the intersection of Fourth and Green
  Streets was susceptible to substantial inundation (see figure 3.3.3).

  Figure 3.3.3. Green Street Flooding circa 1980. Image courtesy of the University of Illinois Archives.

  The design team explored the problem with flooding but were encouraged to separate aesthetics
  and flooding into two separate problems. The team proposed a Boneyard walkway/bikeway where
  some portions of the creek were placed in a culvert and others were improved and made into

   cleaning and repair. The Alma Mater is situated to greet people approaching from the intersection

   On leaving the Alma Mater and progressing to the Green and Wright intersection, it is helpful to
   refer to a brief history of Green Street:

           Green Street was laid out in 1868 dividing the north and south campuses of the University
           of Illinois. The street was lined with large, beautiful homes and elm trees providing a
           canopy of shade. The area near the intersection of Wright and Green Street was recog-
           nized as the point where Champaign, Urbana, and the University of Illinois came together.

           A number of forces precipitated the development of Campustown. Prior to 1905 the char-
           acter of Green Street was residential with some small businesses operating out of homes.
           With an increasing enrollment at the University of Illinois, an economic demand for stu-
           dent goods and services became apparent. The central location of Green Street spurred
           new development shortly after 1910 with the block between Wright Street and Sixth Street
           becoming fully developed by the end of the decade. The residential character of Green
           Street west of Sixth Street remained well into the 1930s.

           Residences along the remainder of the street began to attract more interest from entre-
           preneurs. Many houses began to transform into storefronts, some of which can still be
           seen today. This also led to a diverse blend of businesses including: tailors, barbers, ice
           cream parlors, movie theaters, jewelry stores, bookstores, bowling alleys, billiard parlors,
           and restaurants. A majority of these types of businesses are still present today in some for-
           mat or combination.

           By the 1970s the character of Green Street had been fully transformed into a commercial
           district. The automobile contributed dramatically to this transformation. On-street park-
           ing was removed in the late 1960s to increase traffic flow. This displacement of parking
           spaces and increasing development along Green Street resulted in the City of Champaign
           amending its zoning ordinance to require businesses to provide parking with new con-
           struction. In addition to increased parking, the amount of automobile traffic that circulates
           through the area has had a significant effect on the character of the Campustown area.
           In contrast to these influences the area has been historically dependent upon pedestrian

                                                                                                                                          12

           traffic with businesses primarily orientated for students.

   This synopsis, taken from the 1996 Campustown Revitalization Plan, went on to describe the area
   as seen in 1996:

12. Hinders, Campustown Revitalization Plan, 11.

    135 | Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place
Today, the commercial Campustown area is concentrated between Wright and Fourth
Streets, extending as far west as Neil Street and including several north-south streets.
Champaign-Urbana has witnessed a shift of commercial activity to other areas of the
region, specifically the North Prospect and Market Place areas in recent years. This shift
in commercial activity is hardly responsible for the problems that currently face Campus-
town. Increasing neglect, automobile traffic and changing political climate has generated

                                                                                                                                   13

an unattractive and unsafe pedestrian-oriented environment.

In 2008 the City of Champaign described how the planning of Campustown was evolving:

       The original University District Action Plan (known in the past as the Campustown Action
       Plan) was first created through a joint venture between Campustown businesses, the Uni-
       versity of Illinois and the City of Champaign. . . . This report became the basis for the 1999
       Campustown Action Plan. This document set forth a wide range of action items targeted at
       implementing the Task Force recommendations. These action items contained recommen-

13. Ibid., 13.

                Kevin J. Hinders | 136
           dations on priority, funding and responsibility. Now nine years later, this update contin-
           ues to build on the successes of that original document. Its chapters are molded after the
           original task force report. However, it is important to bear in mind that since the original
           plan adoption in 1999 several key changes have occurred. In 2002, Campustown 2000 was
           disbanded and in its place the University District Advisory Board was created. This board
           serves as an official City of Champaign board and is comprised of specific key leaders in
           the University District (Property Owners, University Representatives, Student Represen-
           tatives, Real Estate Development, and Business Owners). This group, along with City staff,

           will be responsible for overseeing the implementation of this document.

   Green Street continues to be the heart of Campustown. In 1996 the design team determined that
   changing the roadway from four lanes to three, increasing sidewalk widths, and introducing ded-
   icated turn lanes throughout Campustown would enhance safety and pedestrian activity while
   encouraging bus use (see figures 3.3.6 and 3.3.7).

   The design team first sought to establish Green Street as a special location, unique in the city.
   Picking low-hanging fruit was essential for success. Choosing low-cost options that could be
   implemented in a timely manner encouraged a sense of place for Campustown. Prior to this time
   very few trees existed in the area (there was only one tree between Sixth and Wright Streets, for
   example). Introducing trees in grated wells along with consistent street lighting suited to both
   pedestrian and vehicular traffic was an easy way to introduce unifying elements while increasing
   safety. These streetlights were also outfitted with banners and landscape amenities such as hang-
   ing plants.

   An issue that needed to be addressed was the servicing problems for the businesses located on
   the south side of Green Street east of Fifth Street. A connecting alley did not exist and delivery
   and refuse removal was problematic. In an effort to resolve this problem the planning team pro-
   posed changes to parking and delivery traffic along Sixth Street. A pilot study was undertaken to
   explore design implications and opportunities at the intersection of John Street and Sixth Street.
   The intersection was chosen as it also assisted the university in an attempt to alleviate life-safety
   problems associated with people using wheelchairs along John Street. Diagonal parking combined
   with dedicated loading zones provided a reasonable compromise in this area. The streetscaping
   exploration at the John and Sixth intersection served as a later model for the Green Street corri-
   dor.

   To initiate investment in the area, a fund overseen by a review committee was created to pay up to
   one-fifth of development costs if the property owner chose to follow guidelines established by the
   task force (up to a maximum of $200,000). This carrot incentivized investment in Campustown and

14. City of Champaign, University District Action Plan.

    137 | Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place
began the steady growth readily seen today. Improvements that could earn funds included making
properties more accessible, updating building facades, and increasing safety through design.

                           Figure 3.3.7. Green Street perspective, Campustown 2000. Note the
                           reduced roadway, expanded sidewalk, traffic calming paving, facade
                           improvements, banners, lighting, and other design amenities. Drawing
                           by Esmeraldo Formantes.

Some of the first new construction in the area occurred just west of Wright Street. Even before
the task force had completed its recommendations, Follett's Bookstore began replacing its struc-
ture at the northwest corner of Green and Wright Streets. Today Follett's Bookstore has closed
but the building has been adapted to house both retail and restaurant activities. At 616 East Green
Street, a pair of towers were built to take advantage of the proximity to campus. These towers
replaced several older one- and two-story structures including a movie theater and a McDonald's.
It is important to note that the university assisted McDonald's in deciding to surrender a prime
Green Street location by leasing it space in a new food court in the basement of the Illini Union

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 138
   and creating a space for another McDonald's near the student dormitories on Gregory Avenue and

                                                                                                                                                                                                 15

   Euclid Street in a facility with the Illini Orange snack bar, better known as "the Orange."

   These new structures followed the suggestion made by the design team to set back the facade of
   a Green Street frontage at least 10 feet if the building was 20-40 feet high. The design team rea-
   soned that this would help make a more consistent, pedestrian-scaled space while still allowing
   development at a higher density. The City of Champaign never adopted the setback requirement
   and many structures in Campustown do not have Green Street setbacks.

   Perhaps the most notable change in the area was the attention given to the existing facades.
   Numerous owners took advantage of available incentives to restore their facades to a more origi-
   nal appearance. At one time in the 1990s a two-story almost blank facade with a huge Buffalo Wild
   Wings logo was adjacent to the wood-clad exterior of Murphy's Pub. With the new guidelines and
   incentives, the building's billboard-like elevation of the logo was removed to reveal a facade that
   better matched its neighbors. Eventually Murphy's Pub expanded into the space.

   Progressing west one encounters a phenomenon that can be seen at numerous Campustown loca-
   tions. Houses on both Green Street and Sixth Street were adapted to provide commercial opportu-
   nities. Many two-story homes that sat back from the street had a storefront attached that filled in
   the space between the house and the sidewalk. Establishments such as the bars formerly located
   at 706 and 708 South Sixth Street readily displayed this structure (see figures 3.3.8 and 3.3.9).

   These structures were demolished in 2018 to make way for a new residential mixed-use project

                                           16

   (see figure 3.3.10). Today, however, several examples of a storefront tacked onto a house remain
   such as the structures located at 406 Green Street.

   Even more interesting is the case of a house located near the intersection of Sixth and Green. This
   building is reminiscent of the one in the children's story The Little House by Virginia Lee Burton. In
   this tale, a little house built in the country wonders what it would be like to live in the distant city.
   Soon the city expands to surround the little house. Tall buildings block out the sun and the city
   brings pollution and noise. In Burton's story the city engulfs the little house. The house grows sad
   and can only be happy when it is restored to the country. Though the story is excruciatingly anti-
   urban it accurately depicts the urban expansion around this house-turned-apartment-building in

Across the street, on the northwest corner of Green and Sixth Streets a large development
stretches between Green and Healey over the Boneyard Creek. For many years this land had been
a parking lot owned by the City of Champaign known as Lot J. In the 1980s a request for propos-
als to develop the site was issued by the City of Champaign. Unable to reach an agreement with a
developer, the city continued to lease the lot. Wendy's chain restaurant leased a small one-story
building at the corner, but by the early 1990s that building was vacant. JSM Apartments bought the
property and developed it with a two-story commercial building accommodating two popular fast
food restaurants. The strategic construction was intended to position JSM for selection to develop
the Lot J parcel. JSM's project became a six-story mix of parking garage, hotel, and commercial
activities with a landscaped corridor over the buried Boneyard Creek culverts.
Green Street has seen considerable development since the mid-1990s. Newer, taller apartment
buildings continue to be built as Campustown expands westward. Prior to the 1990s, however, the
character of Campustown had a marked shift at Fourth Street. When the city removed parking
from Green Street it began requiring property owners and businesses to have on-site parking.
This change in zoning west of Fourth Street dramatically changed the character and quality of
the built environment. Structures west of Fourth Street began to take on suburban characteristics
that supported automobile traffic at the pedestrian's expense. Burger King, International House of
Pancakes, convenience stores, and other businesses constructed roadside buildings more appro-
priate for strip mall locations. Many even had drive-through windows. These businesses posed
serious safety problems as cars and pedestrians were placed at odds with one another. A few rem-

141 | Part 3.3. Campustown: Politics of Place
nants from this period survive. The Pizza Hut at 411 East Green was a suburban prototype and was
remodeled to reflect Pizza Hut's rebranding in the twenty-first century, and the US Post Office
at 302 East Green Street is a suburban transplant that appears very out of place. At the south-
east corner of Fourth and Green stood a bank with drive-through service. Though the bank has
given way to a series of businesses, it shows how the densification of the area is encouraging more
pedestrian activities as the drive-through has now been transformed with an urban infill building.
This new structure helps provide a continuous building edge to the street and supports Campus-
town's pedestrian life (see figure 3.3.13).

Figure 3.3.13. Campustown Infill. The densification of Green Street at the southeast corner of the Fourth Street
intersection.

On the former site of the Burger King at 309 East Green is a building sometimes referred to as
"the Whopper." This play on the hamburger chain's popular sandwich references the former Burger
King location and the size of the new construction. Where once stood houses converted to com-
mercial businesses and a one-story suburban hamburger chain transplant now stands a twenty-
story apartment building with a multistory parking deck adjacent to an odd green patch waiting
for another tower. The cost of the project, according to the City of Champaign, was approximately

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 142
   $34 million and provides 110 apartments. Since the Campustown 2000 investment, the growth of
   Campustown has been significant. From 2004 to 2016, over 700 apartments were added to the
   Green Street corridor from the railroad tracks to Wright Street at an estimated cost of $163 mil-
   lion. During that same period the City of Champaign recorded the construction of over 1900 units
   at a cost of over $375 million in the Campustown area. Between 20016 and 2024, over 60 projects
   exceeding one million dollars have been undertaken in the Campustown area at a conservatively

                                                                                                                                                                               17

   estimated cost of over $700,000,000 thus adding over 3500 units to the area. New structures
   continue to be added as the Green Street corridor density expands to the west. The area is as
   vibrant and lively as it has ever been. Now safe from flooding, development continues to include
   mixed-use structures that cater to the campus community.

Part 3.4. Small Towns and Rural
Landscapes: Philo

       About the Walk

       "Philo: Center of the Universe" is proudly displayed on the town's water tower. In actuality, for its
       inhabitants, Philo has been an axis mundi just as its water tower states. The idea of creating a central
       and cohesive place in the agrarian landscape is important for an understanding of central Illinois.
       This walk is actually a drive in the country. Beginning with the ubiquitous Casey's General Store
       found in almost every small Illinois town, this ride discusses the role of commerce, the grain eleva-
       tor, churches, the post office, the cemetery, the evolution of the schools, and even the local tavern
       as integral aspects of small town living.
       This walk/drive begins on Route 130 after leaving Urbana and heading south to Philo. The tour goes
       through the various sections of Philo, the cemetery, the downtown, and the residential areas.

                                                                                1

  Traveling east from Decatur, Illinois, on the Norfolk Southern Railway one passes a series of towns
  with tall grain elevators: Cerro Gordo, Milmine, Bement, Ivesdale, Sadorus, Tolono, and Philo,
  then Sydney, Homer, Fairmount, Catlin, and Tilton before reaching Danville, about 80 miles from
  Decatur. On average, just over 6 miles separate each town. These towns are the centers for farm-
  ing communities located on the Toledo, Wabash, and Western Railroad line (now a Norfolk and
  Southern line) to facilitate the movement of crops to processing centers (see figure 3.4.1).
  Philo is located near Bowes Grove and is named after Philo Hale, the land speculator who bought
  up acreage in the area anticipating the construction of a railroad. His foresight was rewarded when
  the railroad was built in 1856. Eventually the "Wabash Cannonball" began running between Detroit

1. Decatur has been an important grain-processing city for over a century. The A. E. Staley Company began processing
  corn in Decatur in 1906. A. E. Staley became Staley Continental in 1985 and was taken over by Tate and Lyle--a British
  corporation (see Tate and Lyle, "Augustus Eugene Staley"). The Archer Daniels Midland Company, long a major agricul­
  ture-based corporation, was headquartered in Decatur from 1969 to 2014 (see ADM, "History").
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 144
  and St. Louis and traveled through Philo (Detroit to Fort Wayne, Indiana, to Decatur, and then to

                        2

  St. Louis). The main use for the rails, though, was the linkage of farmers to their markets.
  The continental grid that crisscrosses the area has long allowed farmers to bring crops to town,
  where the grain elevators facilitate exchange. These prairie towers set at a regular increment can
  be seen for miles across the flat, east central Illinois landscape. The elevators identify the commu-
  nity's presence on the skyline above the fields of corn and soybeans (see figure 3.4.2).

  Part 3.4. Orientation Map.

2. From "Wabash Cannonball":
          Listen to the jingle, the rumble, and the roar, As she glides along the woodland, through the hills, and by the
          shore. Hear the mighty rush of her engine; hear that lonesome hobo's call. You're travelling through the jungles
          on the Wabash Cannonball. (Loewen, "Wabash Cannonball").

                            Figure 3.4.1. Map of the Illinois Great Western Railroad.

The skyline of these towns tells a story. The dominant horizon on the prairie is broken by few ver-
tical attenuations. The groves of the area can be seen from miles away, as can the built environ-
ment. Church steeples and bell towers serve to call their congregations. These vertical extensions
from the horizontal plane strive to connect heaven with Earth. Similarly Philo's water tower with
its proud inscription "Philo: Center of the Universe" locates Philo, not just locally, but in the cos-
mos (see figure 3.4.3).

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 146
       Figure 3.4.2. Philo Skyline. Image of Philo across the prairie.

It serves as a centering device--an axis mundi. Philo has experienced numerous fires throughout
its existence. The water tower is a symbol of people coming together. It is a governmental struc-
ture that underscores the importance of a community, especially when the town or village is not
large enough to support a county seat or a grand city hall. The skyline of the small Midwestern
town is punctuated by these built edifices that break the monotony of the fields. Spiritual commu-
nity, civic community, and economic community form the backdrop for the life of the town.

147 | Part 3.4. Small Towns and Rural Landscapes: Philo
Figure 3.4.3. Philo Water Tower.

Driving south down Route 130 from Urbana one approaches Philo (population 1461) through a

                                                                                  3

series of cautionary speed limit signs. At one time roads coming into Philo were paved on only

one side while the other side remained compressed dirt. The farmers needed paved roads when

3. Data USA, "Philo, IL."

                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 148
  they brought heavy harvests into town. Returning home, their vehicles were much lighter and did

                                                                             4

  not need the expensive pavement.

  On the northern outskirts of Philo lies a Casey's General Store. Casey's business plan has been to

                                                                                                                                                                                                                    5

  locate its combination of convenient stores and gas stations in small towns across the Midwest.
  This strategy makes great business sense for Philo. It no longer supports a grocery store and there
  is only one other gas station in town (Phillips 66). Though the town still has major agricultural ties
  it has also become a bedroom community to Urbana-Champaign. Many residents work at the Uni-
  versity of Illinois, Parkland College, or local health care facilities. Instead of forcing residents to
  drive 3 miles to a southern Urbana strip mall or big box store, Casey's services the community for
  its smaller-scale needs.

  On entering town there is a distinct separation of the east and west. To the west lies the town
  cemetery, immaculately kept and slowly growing. The cemetery overlooks the prairie from a high
  point. Philo was referred to by railroad engineers as "Summit" because it could take two engines to
  push railcars up the incline to Philo from the west. On a clear day one can easily count a half dozen
  grain elevators in the distance. The cemetery bears the signs of persons having visited. Tombs are
  cared for and flags are placed on Veteran's Day. One gets the sense of the connection between
  those living to the east and those buried here.

  Crossing Route 130 on Jefferson one can go six city blocks before coming to what was once the
  edge of town. Philo's land pattern is easy to discern. "Old" Philo rests between County Road 1650
  East, which becomes Cleveland Street on the east, and Route 130 (or County Road 1600 East),
  which is called Lincoln Street. It is between County Roads 900 and 800 North (Monroe Street and
  Roosevelt Road, respectively). Incorporated in 1864 by E. B. Hale, the son of the town's namesake,
  the town originally comprised 80 acres. Now Philo occupies approximately 320 acres inside this
  boundary of streets paying homage to our past presidents. A closer look indicates that the down-
  town and the oldest area were located on the north half of the town. One hundred and sixty acres
  lie north of Van Buren Street. This is still the heart of Philo (see figure 3.4.4).

  South of Van Buren lie residences and newer construction, where the streets begin to change and
  become less dense. To the east lie the "suburbs." This area, east of Cleveland Road, is, surprisingly,
  sprawl. Sprawl occurs when developers purchase agricultural land and make improvements, typ-
  ically providing electricity, water and sewer lines, and paved roads, and then subdividing the area
  into lots for sale to future homeowners and contractors building on speculation. These tracts are

   or fewer." (Casey's, "Corporate Profile").

are sold the governance is turned over to a homeowner's association. Philo has two such tracts:
Jefferson extends east to take on a meandering path with cul-de-sacs to the north and south..
To the south, Valley View Drive and Country View Drive are named to attract those wishing to
take advantage of sight lines afforded of the agricultural land surrounding the city. They also have
adjoining cul-de-sacs.

                                                                       Kevin J. Hinders | 150
  Returning to Philo's older quarter along Madison Street just west of Cleveland, one sees the Roman
  Catholic Church and a school named St. Thomas. The parish was established in 1894 and the cur-
  rent church was dedicated in 1980. The parochial school was established in 1905 and the current
  school was built in 1966 with an addition dedicated in 2020. Philo supports two other churches
  that are over a century old, the Zion Lutheran Church at Van Buren and Harrison Streets and the
  Philo Presbyterian Church at 105 East Jefferson Street. In addition to these village churches is
  Countryside United Methodist Church, which moved to its current location in the 1970s approxi-
  mately 3 miles from Philo on 1807 East County Road 1000 North. This congregation has roots dat-
  ing back to the 1830s and includes former churches from Philo and Sydney including Alice Chapel,

                                                                   6

  Lost Grove, and Pleasant Hill.

  Years ago, Philo had an old two-plus-story schoolhouse on Madison Street. For over five decades

                                                                                                                                      7

  the stone and brick school served as the Philo High School, but in the 1950s the schools in the
  southern part of Champaign County created a consolidated high school in Tolono, 6 miles west of
  Philo. The Champaign County Unit Seven School District now controls a vast area, serving towns
  in the southern part of Champaign County including Tolono, Philo, Sadorus, Pesotum, and Sydney

                                                                                                8

  set among 100,000 acres of rural farmland. The high school is known as the Unity Rockets: Unity
  to symbolize the uniting of numerous communities into one larger school and Rockets to serve as
  an apropos nickname for a school emerging after World War II.

  The Philo school building on Madison Street continued to serve the town until 2002, when the
  Unity East Elementary School (pre-K through fifth grade) was constructed at 1638 County Road
  1000 North just north of Philo. Unity West Elementary School was built that same year in Tolono.
  The designs for the schools were very similar to conserve costs and avoid any hard feelings or
  favoritism in the district. These schools feed into the junior high and high school located in Tolono.
  The Philo school site was sold to the village and the old school building was torn down. Today the
  R. E. Franks Center occupies the part of the grade school addressed toward Washington Street,
  which is connected to the Thelma Melohn Gymnasium on Madison Street. This community center
  is used for sporting events, receptions, and reunions.

  Washington Street at the Route 130 intersection makes a concession to the adjacent railroad
  tracks before realigning with the continental grid. Washington is the commercial street in town. Its
  entrance from the west is marked by the Phillips 66 gas station. At Jackson Street, one block east
  is a commemorative place dedicated to those who have served in the armed services. This marks
  the pride of local inhabitants linked to the federal government. The flagpole and bronze plaques
  remind the population and visitors of the sacrifices made by service members from the Philo area.

   One-half block farther on the north side of the street is a small brick federal post office from the

   The primary intersection in Philo occurs at Washington and Harrison Streets. Harrison is the only
   street that crosses over the railroad tracks to link the two sides of town, and it is here that the
   grain elevator is also located. The grain elevator is now a co-op, a common phenomenon in central
   Illinois as the Premier Cooperative now operates the elevator.

   in 1883, this bank is noteworthy for being one of the oldest and most substantial banks in Philo by

   tower was located at this site, overseeing the town and truly marking its center. That tower was

   Today the heart of Philo is the Philo Tavern, located on the southwest corner of Washington and

10. This water tower was replaced in 2009 with a 250,000-gallon tank (Walsh, "New 250,000-Gallon Water Tower").

Harrison Streets. The Philo Tavern is where farmers gather for coffee in the morning and other
patrons come for lunch and dinner. Most towns of this size have their own version of the Philo
Tavern: a place where people congregate to hear local news and visit with one another. The Philo
Tavern also serves as a local hub. People from other communities travel to Philo for their Friday
fish fries and their Saturday night prime rib dinners.
Many towns and villages across the Midwest are similar to Philo. Yet each has unique, distinguish-
ing characteristics that reflect its own history. It is the remarkable similarities in their built envi-
ronments that echo across the country and continue to evolve to support local people as they go
about their lives in rural America.
