Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape:
Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form

     About the Walk

     The University of Illinois Urbana-Champaign has an incredibly rich set of exterior spaces, including
     Illini Grove, the quadrangles, and many smaller intimate areas. In America, some of the most beauti-
     ful landscapes are cemeteries and public gardens. This part discusses both the formation of the
     campus topography and how American cultural preferences of the landscape have shifted over the
     centuries.
     Mount Hope Cemetery, directly to the south of the Stock Pavilion, predates the university. This area
     was used for interment by the 1850's. Currently Mount Hope Cemetery has approximately 14,000
     graves. The cemetery features a large mausoleum and chapel, individual and family mausoleums, a
     columbarium, family plots, in-ground plots, a Jewish section, and areas reserved for military veter-
     ans. A tour through the cemetery reflects the values and concerns of the living even in death. Strate-
     gies and design layout with trees and plantings reinforce the values of our society and the changes
     that continue to take place in the world of the living that affect the world of the dead.
     This walk begins at the entrance to Mount Hope Cemetery at 611 East Pennsylvania Avenue in Cham-
     paign. The walk moves in a wide counterclockwise circle from the main mausoleum and then west-
     ward before returning to the entrance gate.

       God created man in His own image, in the image of God He created him; male and female
       He created them. God blessed them; and God said to them, "Be fruitful and multiply, and
       fill the earth, and subdue it; and rule over the fish of the sea and over the birds of the sky
       and over every living thing that moves on the earth."
       --Genesis 1:27-28

With the Fall of Man (Genesis ch. 3), God sends Adam and Eve away:

       "Cursed is the ground because of you!
       In toil shall you eat its yield

       All the days of your life.
       Thorns and thistles it shall bring forth for you,

       By the sweat of your face shall you get bread to eat
       Until you return to the ground,
       From which you were taken;
       For you are dirt,
       And to dirt you shall return."
       . . . the Lord God sent him out from the garden of Eden, to cultivate the ground from which

       the cherubim and the flaming sword which turned every direction to guard the way to the
       tree of life.

Part 3.5. Orientation Map.

In these chapters from Genesis describing the origin story of humanity from the Christian per-
spective, two important ideas in this tradition are relayed that shall be discussed here: first, that
humankind has dominion over nature; second, that in the beginning humans lived in a garden in
which harmony existed between people and animals, and humankind's fall from grace and banish-

155 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
  ment from the Garden of Eden created a clearly antagonistic relationship between humans and
  nature.

  The history of human civilization traces back to a nomadic existence with a seasonal movement of
  clans. Only with the advent of agricultural farming were groups able to settle in one place. These
  settlements reflected a fundamental understanding of the seasons and allowed for greater manip-
  ulation of the built environment. Stonehenge is an example of a neolithic desire to fix oneself in
  both time and place.

  As human societies evolved so did the understanding of the universe and nature. Since antiquity,
  however, some cultures have had a core belief that though humans benefit greatly from an
  enhanced understanding of nature, there still is a fundamentally antagonistic relationship between
  them. Even today, structures are constructed to keep weather out and provide safety within. At the
  urban scale, cities in previous eras were typically encircled by walls and fortified for protection.
  Those not "inside" the walls (exemplifying one's own society) were "outsiders"--barbarians--and

                                           1

  often antagonistic.

  During early settlement of what would become the United States and the later westward move-
  ment by Europeans and their descendants, the notion of outside versus inside became important
  in their psychology. The indigenous peoples may or may not have been thought by Europeans to
  be like Rousseau's romanticized "Noble Savage" but they were certainly considered by many Euro-
  peans to be "uncivilized." Similarly, the Native Americans were wary of the "uncivilized" strangers.
  Benjamin Franklin wrote in his essay "Remarks concerning the Savages of North America" (1784)
  explaining each society:

1. The philosopher John Locke, in his Two Treatises, proposed the idea that humans were essentially free beings:

          According to him humans were initially in a state of nature. The state of nature was apolitical in the sense that
          there were no governments and each individual retained all of his or her natural rights. People possessed these
          natural rights (including the right to attempt to preserve one's life to seize unclaimed valuables and so forth)
          because they were given by God to all of his people. The state of nature was inherently unstable. Individuals
          would be under constant threat of physical harm. And they would be unable to pursue any goals that required
          stability and widespread cooperation with other humans. Locke's claim is that government arose in this con-
          text. Individuals seeing the benefits which could be gained decided to relinquish some of their rights to a central
          authority while retaining other rights. This took the form of a contract. In agreement for relinquishing certain
          rights individuals would receive protection from physical harm security for their possessions and the ability to
          interact and cooperate with other humans in a stable environment ("John Locke (1632-1704)," Internet Encyclo-
          pedia of Philosophy, accessed June 11, 2025, https://iep.utm.edu/locke/#SH4a).

  Thus the citizen was affiliated with a particular government. All others were a part of another society that was often
  viewed with suspicion and mistrust.

                                                                                                                                                                Kevin J. Hinders | 156
       Savages we call them, because their manners differ from ours, which we think the perfec-
       tion of civility; they think the same of theirs.

       Perhaps, if we could examine the manners of different nations with impartiality, we should
       find no people so rude as to be without any rules of politeness; nor any so polite as not to

                                                                                2

       have some remains of rudeness.

This bias toward self as civilized and outsider as barbarian can be illustrated by the children's
movie Pocahontas, released by Disney in 1995, as the English settlers exchange lyrics with the
Powhatan tribe:

   157 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
  Whether it was forts built by the Pilgrims or Conestoga wagons crossing the West and circling the

                                               4

  wagons for defense, there is a consistency in the inside good/outside bad and civilized/uncivi-
  lized dichotomy in American culture.

  This relationship is reflected in the manner in which the landscape is treated. Since ancient times
  in Western cultures, the walled city has typically included gardens, which often reflect a desire to
  create an earthly recreation of the "paradise lost." These paradise gardens can be seen in a vari-
  ety of places and cultures. Christian versions often replace the biblical Tree of Knowledge of Good
  and Evil with a central fountain. This may be taken as a symbol of the role that baptism plays in
  washing away the sins of Adam and Eve. In these gardens, and the evolution of gardens in Western
  culture in general, one can trace a marked sense that humankind is being placed in a dominant
  position over nature. Trees are planted in orderly rows. Bushes and shrubs are trimmed to create
  geometric patterns and forms. Water is channelized and appears in fountains along with sculp-
  tures and buildings.

  The gardens of Versailles are a superb example of a design suited to exemplify Louis XIV's com-
  plete control. Topiary is trimmed into elaborate cones and arabesques in an effort to create an
  exacting order. The display of both power and control is both aesthetic and political.

  With the rise of the Enlightenment in the West a dramatic shift took place that is still reverberating
  today. As humans' understanding of the natural world expanded, the sciences developed special-
  ized fields--including physics, biology, and chemistry--that produced additional insights into how
  the universe works. With this new knowledge, humans' interactions with nature gradually changed
  from one of antagonism and a new aesthetic developed. This shift is readily seen in the English
  landscape garden. In the United States, the work of Calvert Vaux and Frederick Law Olmsted illus-
  trate this change in the preference for an undulating landscape that simulates a "natural" setting.
  In truth, these landscapes are incredibly contrived to look natural but, like New York's Central Park,
  require large amounts of soil redistribution and removal of the existing natural landscape to create
  the desired effect. Therefore as the antagonistic relationship with nature changed in the West, so
  did the design of the built environment.

  The Enlightenment begat the Industrial Revolution, which provided new materials (notably steel
  and float glass) and means of production. These coinciding factors have had a substantial influence

4. Nathaniel Philbrick describes the Pilgrims' fort built in 1622 out of fear of an attack from the Narragansetts: "By March,
   it was complete: a massive, sap-dripping, bark-peeling wall between them and the surrounding forest. A new sense of
   order and control had been willed upon the wilderness. Plymouth was now an entity, a circumscribed place, its streets
   and houses darkened by the shadows of its margins. It was quite shiplike in many ways. Plymouth was like a huge
   Mayflower run aground on the shores of New England, her bow resting atop Fort Hill, her stern edging toward the har­
   bor and the outflow of Town Brook. By impaling the town, the Pilgrims had made it clear that they intended to remain
   there for a very long time" (Mayflower, 130).

                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 158
on the way we now design our world. Frank Lloyd Wright's homes illustrate the desire to place the
structure in nature. Often these homes inserted rooms into the landscape with windows on three
sides. The ability to blur the distinction between inside and outside is not just a matter of using
technology but is a use of technology in support of a shift in values about the natural environment.
Today, a homeowner may still run lawn mowers and create highly artificial landscapes, but these
alterations are often done with the sincere desire to make things look natural.

In the preindustrial age, the aspiration to reaching the heavens is seen throughout history. From
the cosmic mountains and pyramids in the Middle East, Egypt, and Mexico to the boldly tower-
ing medieval cathedrals of Europe, the use of bearing wall construction--brick on brick, stone on
stone--created monuments aspiring to link heaven and Earth. Since the invention of the Besse-
mer process, which allows for the production of steel, new construction technology has allowed
for greater verticality and horizontality and much longer spans. A new preference for the nat-
ural landscape occurred alongside these technological advancements. The search for a god not in
heaven but in the world around us has coincided with the advancements in knowledge of the nat-
ural world. Alongside this search has been a growing preference for the natural over the human
built. Thus, new construction technology has helped people create a more horizontally framed
relationship with nature. The Prairie houses of Frank Lloyd Wright and the glass and steel designs
of Ludwig Mies van der Rohe illustrate the idea of a structure that uses horizontal planes to
frame the landscape and an attempt is made to blur the distinction between inside and outside. In
Wright's case, he often created rooms with dramatic roof overhangs and windows on three sides
with the fourth edge anchored by a hearth. This symbolic idea of the hearth providing a verti-
cal centering device for the family that then extends out into nature is extremely poetic and is
repeated throughout Wright's Prairie-style houses. In Mies van der Rohe's early houses the inside
walls extended beyond the interior into a garden and the horizontal floors/ceilings extended
beyond the glass enclosure walls to create a similar extension of the inside into the natural world.
In his later work, most notably the Farnsworth House, the horizontal planes of floors and roof
hover above the ground with the occupants occupying the space in between (see figures 3.5.1 and
3.5.2).

Before exploring the landscape of campus it is essential to understand the form and formation of
the prairie in which the campus rests. In Annals of the Former World, John McPhee writes about
the Earth as it was long before human habitation. Through a series of trips with geological experts,
McPhee discusses how the Earth's forms have been created. In the passage below he is traveling in
the Midwest with geologist Anita Harris:

       We had come into the continent's province of supreme tectonic calm, the Stable Interior
       Craton, where a thin veneer of sediment lies flat upon the stolid fundament, where the
       geology--even by geological standards--is exceptionally slow. "This is the most conserva-

159 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
       tive part of the U.S.," Anita said. "I've often thought about it. The wildest, craziest people
       are in the most tectonically active places."

       And yet the craton stirs. There is no part of the face of the earth that vertically and laterally
       does not move. The bedding planes in Midwestern rock, which appear to be absolutely
       level, do in fact dip. They will descend across a great many miles and then rise, arching
       over the far rim of a vast and shallow bowl, and then subtly dip again to form a similar bowl:
       the Findlay Arch, the Michigan Basin, the Kankakee Arch, the Illinois Basin. Anita called
       the arches "basement highs." . . . They are products of the creaking motions of the craton,
       in response perhaps to plays from deep within the mantle--a process that, in the general
       phrase, is "not well understood." They represent a degree of tectonic activity about as lively

                                                                               5

       as the setting in of rigor mortis.

Illinois, as most schoolchildren learn, has incurred repeated invasions. Long before humans settled
in this area, however, there were invasions of another sort. Over the past 2.5 million years, approx-
imately twenty-five huge continental glaciers have encroached and receded across the plains.
Sometimes the glaciers measured over 2 miles thick. These glaciers moved tons of dirt and
stone at their leading edge. As temperatures warmed, the glaciers would retreat, leaving behind
moraines. Two such moraines run through Champaign County: the Bloomington Moraine system
and the Champaign Moraine system are both part of the Upper Sangamon Basin. These moraines
were formed between 10,000 and 25,000 years ago during what is called the late Wisconsin glacia-
tion. This Wisconsin invasion left a moraine that is a part of the University of Illinois campus. A
long ridge runs south of Memorial Stadium, State Farm Center, and the intramural activity fields.
The top of this ridge is crowned by the round barns along St. Mary's Road. To the south, waters
drain to the 195-mile-long Embarras River before emptying into the Wabash just above the Ohio
River. To the north, the waters drain to the Boneyard Creek (see figure 3.5.3).

At first, the waters of the glaciers were held by the earthen dams they had left behind. Eventually
the increasing water pressure on the moraines caused breakthroughs, such as the Sangamon
River, which cuts through the moraine near Mahomet. These breakthroughs typically feature sand
and gravel deposits at their outflow. Because of the broad, flat areas left behind by previous, more
southerly encroaching glaciers, the water on the south side of the Champaign Moraine spread and
settled. South of Champaign remained a vast, swampy area for millennia before ingenious meth-
ods were used by settlers in the 1800s to produce some of the richest farmlands in the world. Even
today, the areas to the south of the moraines have a very high water table.

This is the natural landscape surrounding the university: Broad prairie occupying approximately
eighty percent of the land with groves of trees near streams and rivers accounting for the remain-

5. McPhee, Annals of the Former World, 252.

ing twenty percent of acreage. Mount Hope and Roselawn Cemeteries sit upon a moraine sur-
rounded by the University of Illinois (see figure 3.5.4).

  The cemeteries predate the university. As the school has grown it has come to envelop the parcel
  that once defined its southern edge.

  Mount Hope Cemetery was founded in 1855 on acreage donated by the Burt family and was incor-

                                                     6

  porated one year later. The Burts recognized the need for an outdoor community space. In the
  mid-nineteenth century, before public parks, the cemetery was a place to walk, picnic, and be

                                                                                 7

  outdoors in a manicured landscape. Paths and roads provided vehicular and pedestrian access to
  open green space. The Mount Hope Cemetery site has an appealing undulation of the ground. Pre-
  viously the largest cemetery space in Urbana was located at what is now Leal Park until the Native
  American and pioneer remains buried there were relocated to other cemeteries. Though Mount
  Hope is perceived as a single cemetery, there are two separately named cemeteries. Because of
  upkeep costs, an area in township section 18 (the westernmost area, alongside Fourth Street) was
  sold in 1906 for $50,000 to a group that named its area the New Mount Hope Cemetery. This later
  became Roselawn Cemetery (see figure 3.5.5).

  Although the two cemeteries are contiguous, they remain separate in name and retain separate
  pricing for grave spaces and burial services. The cemeteries are now the resting place of approxi-

   paigncountyhistory.org/single-post/a-garden-for-the-dead-in-the-big-grove.

mately 14,000 persons and are nondenominational though there are dedicated sections, including
the Champaign and Urbana Hebrew Congregation Addition.

Figure 3.5.5. Mount Hope Cemetery and Roselawn Cemetery.

Both Mount Hope Cemetery and Mausoleum and Roselawn Cemetery are addressed at 611 East
Pennsylvania Avenue in Champaign. On entering the main gate, the mausoleum along with its non-
denominational meeting space is located to the east. A low stone building, the mausoleum pro-
vides aboveground burial locations for both embalmed and cremated remains. A gathering space
with amber glass to either side of a vaulted, central hall greets the visitor. Bench pews for approx-
imately 100 people allow for viewings and ceremonies. Tombs are stacked six high and run in adja-
cent corridors lined with small urns for flowers and mementos. Many couples and families are
grouped together. Uniform cream-colored marble slabs with bronze letters announce the names
of the people interred. Benches provide resting space for the bereaved and those visiting. Shelves
with glass doors hold vessels with cremated remains.
Outside are four primary materials used as grave markers. Early stone memorials and monuments
were often carved out of limestone and marble. Because of the weathering effects on these mate-
rials, granite has now become the stone of choice. Most granites are gray, red, or black. Bronze
metal is also often used in plaques in the military sections. Bronze is chosen as it conveys a sense
of dignity, resists effects of weathering, and can be cast to include information about the deceased.
As one moves through the cemetery these materials will convey their own attributes to the visitor.

163 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
  To the west of the mausoleum are two distinctly different sets of graves. The first is an area cre-
  ated for those who participated in the American Civil War. The Grand Army of the Republic cre-

                                                                                                                                                                                                   8

  ated the memorial statue and soon after placed the cannon and flagpole (see figure 3.5.6).

  This area provides tombs for war veterans dating back to the Revolutionary War. The grave mark-
  ers in this area are made predominantly of white marble. The thin markers have rounded tops and
  stand in rows at attention. Weather has eroded the engravings but the information is still legible.
  To the north of the Civil War monument lies the Jewish section. In 1899 the Jewish cemetery in

8. "The cannon is a 32-pounder seacoast defense coat model. It was built in 1851 at the West Point Foundry. The two [Grand
   Army of the Republic] posts of Champaign and Urbana came together to operate this section of the cemetery for Civil
   War veterans. Over the years, veterans of other wars have been buried in this section. [The Grand Army of the Republic]
   continues to decorate the veteran graves in this section and to maintain the cannon" (Miller, "Mount Hope Cemetery").
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 164
                                                                                                                                                                                                                     9

   Urbana was closed and those interred there were moved to Mount Hope Cemetery by April 1900.
   Today many of these grave markers have stones placed on top in the Jewish tradition. According to

                                                                                                                                                                                                                    10

   Rabbi David Wolpe, the reasons for this practice range from the "superstitious to the poignant."
   Superstition has it that the stones help keep the soul down as the grave is believed to retain some
   aspect of the departed soul. The stones also serve as a more permanent reminder of one's visit
   than flowers, which are rarely seen in Jewish cemeteries. Stones have a permanence of memory
   while flowers provide a sense of passing. Another explanation dates to the days of shepherds:

           A beautiful answer takes its cue from the inscription on many gravestones. The Hebrew
           abbreviation taf, nun, tsadi, bet, hey stands for "teheye nishmato tsrurah b'tsror ha-
           chayyim," a phrase usually translated "May his soul be bound up in the bonds of eternal
           life."

           Yet tsror in Hebrew means a pebble. In ancient times, shepherds needed a system to keep
           track of their flocks. On some days, they would go out to pasture with a flock of 30; on
           others, a flock of 10. Memory was an unreliable way of keeping tabs on the number of the
           flock. As a result, the shepherd would carry a sling over his shoulder, and in it he would
           keep the number of pebbles that corresponded to the number in his flock. That way he
           could at all times have an accurate daily count.

           When we place stones on the grave and inscribe the motto above on the stone, we are
           asking God to keep the departed's soul in His sling. Among all the souls whom God has to

                                                                                                                                                                                             11

           watch over, we wish to add the name--the "pebble"--of the soul of our departed.

   In addition to stones one may also find touching reminders from loved ones who have left behind
   objects such as a harmonica, an action figure, or a hockey puck (see figure 3.5.7).

   Though entire books have been written on symbolism in the cemetery it is worthwhile to touch

   on why certain trees and shrubs are found in this cemetery. Moving west from the Jewish area
   one comes to an alee of very old cedar trees (genus Cedrus). These trees are coniferous, which
   gives them longevity and an evergreen character, and this makes them and cypress trees (genus
   Cupressus) common in cemeteries. The cypress has been associated with death since the ancient

                                                                                                                                                                                                          12

   Greeks and Romans, who believed the tree was sacred to the keepers of the underworld. It is
   thought that the wood used to make the cross for Jesus's crucifixion came from the wood of a
   cypress or oak tree. Thus, the oak (genus Quercus) is one deciduous tree often associated with
   graveyards. Early American settlers were known to have buried children under or near the shade
   of oak trees. The weeping willow (genus Salix) is another tree often associated with cemeteries
   and typically these are located near ponds and streams. Because of the topography at Mount Hope
   and Roselawn willows are not prevalent, but graveside peony bushes are very common here. These
   perennial flowers need very little attention and are known to survive generations, which makes
   them an ideal planting for spring and early summer flowering. Also found in Mount Hope Ceme-

                                                                                                                                                                                                                   13

   tery are English yews (Taxus baccata). Yews such as these have been known to live for centuries.

At the western edge of the alee is a meadow. Graves bound the north side of the gravel road. To the

Farther to the west is a second area dedicated to veterans. This section was originally a rectangu-
lar area demarcated from other spaces by four corner plantings of yew shrubs. Over time, these
shrubs overgrew the adjacent gravesites and all but those in one corner have been removed. This
unfortunate need for removal has left the area poorly defined. The graves here do not have head-
stones of granite or marble and instead are marked with bronze plaques that lie flat along the

167 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
ground in rows. Each plaque designates the name, rank, date of birth, and date of death and some-
times include a religious symbol (for example, a cross or Star of David) along with the war in which
the veteran served. A monument at the north end of the section is dedicated to those who gave
their lives in combat.

                           Figure 3.5.9. Huff and Zuppke Headstones on the `fifty yard line.'

The corner of Pennsylvania Avenue and Fourth Street has a parking lot that forms the northern
boundary of Roselawn Cemetery. Extending the eastern boundary of the parking area directly
south determines the border between Mount Hope and Roselawn. Roselawn became a popular
burial location as its founders promised better upkeep than at the neighbor to the east.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 168
Though widespread in other countries such as Italy, family mausoleums are not common in Mid-
western cemeteries. Though they certainly exist (there are numerous mausoleums in Mount Hope
Cemetery) only a few have been built since 1945 here, and a major one is in Roselawn. This notable
structure is the mausoleum for a family of newspaper publishers at the northwest corner of the
cemetery near a secondary entrance off Fourth Street. The Stevick-Chinigo mausoleum is an ele-
gant granite, cubic volume with adjacent urns on each side and two asymmetrically placed bronze
doors. David Stevick's ownership of the News-Gazette newspaper was taken over by his wife and
later his daughter, Marajen Stevick Chinigo, until death in 2002. The mausoleum is sited at a
45-degree angle with Fourth Street and has a paved forecourt with semicircular benches This is
one of the last family mausoleum constructed in the two cemeteries. See figure 3.5.8).

                        Figure 3.5.10. Headstone of Thomas D. "Dike" and Teddy G. Eddleman.

Roselawn also has the graves of a number of well-known persons from the University of Illinois.
Looking toward Memorial Stadium looming up across Fourth Street, it is possible to pick out
where the 50-yard line is located in relationship to the eastern facade. The field on which the Illini
play is known as Zuppke Field in honor of Robert Zuppke (1879-1957), the Illinois football coach
from 1913 to 1941. Zuppke's grave sits on the projected 50-yard line in Roselawn. One would think
he has the perfect seat except his boss, George Huff (1872-1936), sits one row closer to the field.

   Huff, for whom Huff Hall is named, was the athletic director for Illinois from 1901 to 1935. These
   family graves are marked on both sides and can be seen by passersby from Fourth Street (see figure
   3.5.9).

   Also noteworthy is the grave location of Thomas D. "Dike" Eddleman and his wife, Teddy. Eddle-
   man's headstone has images of the three sports he played as an Illini (basketball, football, and
   track). It is located at the extension of the south end zone. Presumably Eddleman's penchant for
   finding the end zone continues in the afterlife (see figure 3.5.10).
   Often plots were purchased for families and contain room for two or three generations. In the
   older area of Mount Hope Cemetery families such as the Burnhams (headed by Albert C. Burnham,
   1839-97) would establish a precinct of their own with stone stairs, border markers, and a large
   family monument--in the case of the Burnhams, a tall, light gray, granite vertical obelisk resting on

   a base --surrounded by individual grave markers. Today two relatively new family plots are on the

Along the way back to the Mount Hope Mausoleum one passes a variety of headstones, monu-

most recognizable obelisk in the United States, though not made of a single piece of stone, is the Washington Monu­
ment in Washington, DC.
171 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
   ments, and memorials. As in most Midwestern cemeteries there are a considerable number of
   obelisks from different eras in different colors and types of stone. The obelisk is a type of axis
   mundi. In the cemetery setting, it attempts to link the grave with the heavens while marking a
   particular place on Earth. It is a means to establish a miniature center of the universe for the
   deceased. It is also a form associated with Freemasonry and obelisks are commonly used to iden-
   tify the graves of masons.

   Another often seen marker is the "Woodmen's column" (see figure 3.5.13). Modern Woodmen of
   America was formed in 1883 as a financial fraternity by Joseph Cullen Root, who sought to "bind in
   one association the Jew and Gentile, the Catholic and the Protestant, the agnostic and the athe-

            15

   ist." The group's name was inspired by the pioneers who "cleared forests to build homes and

                                                                                                                       16

   communities and provide security for their families." Root later created Woodmen of the World
   (WOW) to be an organization that would "clear away problems of financial security for its mem-

                 17

   bers." A women's auxiliary of WOW, the Supreme Forest Woodmen Circle, was formed in 1892.
   From 1890 to 1900, WOW life insurance policies included free grave markers and from 1900 to the
   mid-1920s members needed to purchase a $100 rider to cover the cost of a monument. The prac-
   tice was finally discontinued because of the costs. To create uniformity but have local flexibility
   WOW would send templates to local stone carvers who might embellish the models with axes,
   mauls, and other woodworking instruments. These limestone markers are often found in family
   plots.

   On the backside of the multifamily mausoleum is a screened area for excavating equipment. Here
   the symbolic and poetic aspects of the cemetery meet the realities of the pragmatic digging and
   maintenance issues of the funerary business. This juxtaposition is readily seen as the equipment is
   stored adjacent to the chapel wall.

   Nearby is an exterior, aboveground burial wall located close to Pennsylvania Avenue. Similar to
   the interior of the Mount Hope Mausoleum, black granite slabs announce the presence of those
   interred behind. This is a columbarium. By European standards, where above ground burial is more
   common, this is a quite small. European cemeteries commonly have long "streets" in their necrop-
   olises ("cities of the dead"), see figure 3.5.14.

   In the Midwest, the cemetery has never been seen as a city, but rather is a rural landscape with a
   sense of peace. From here, one can freely wander through this landscape and enjoy a predecessor
   of the Midwest's public park.

The university has a beautiful relationship with the landscape, which is discussed in different parts
of this book. It is worth noting three other landscapes in brief: Illini Grove, the Arboretum with
Japan House, and the rain gardens.

Illini Grove was established in 1871 on the more than 13 acres originally called the Forestry. It was
planted with over 15 different tree species to educate farmers about which ones to use for fire-

173 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form

   wood, fence posts, and shelter. More tree species were added as the area was enhanced, but the
   construction of Lincoln Avenue Residence Hall and McKinley Health Center trimmed it to only 9
   acres. A resolution was passed by the Illinois State Legislature to ask the university to keep this
   space wooded and open in honor of Senator Stanley Weaver and Mary Smith Weaver because of

                                                              19

   their dedication to nature.

           Figure 3.5.14. Mount Hope Cemetery Columbarium.

   The current Arboretum was developed in the late 1980s and early 1990s with the stated goal "to
   discover, interpret and disseminate knowledge in plant sciences and applied arts by providing an

    aesthetically enriched setting for education, research, conservation and enjoyment." The Arbore-
    tum occupies 160 acres and continues to be developed. It has a formal allusion as being an exten-
    sion of the President's House. The main formal garden is located on an axis with the house to
    create a perceptual, but not connected, axis. This is similar to what a Georgian/English house
    would have: a formal front with a parterre garden behind and an open Olmsted-style garden in the
    distance.

    Japan House is near this area but lies in a more naturalistic-appearing garden. Near the structure
    small theme gardens associated with traditional Japanese ceremonies change the scale of the
    landscape to match the intimacy of the building.
    Rain gardens are now used to help alleviate flooding on campus and downstream. These gardens
    are planted with native species and use swales to capture water that might otherwise move to
    storm sewers. The swales temporarily contain runoff and allow the ground to absorb the rainwa-
    ter as it percolates through the soil. A goal of these gardens and swales is to slow the flow of water
    from the watershed and allow it to be absorbed by the earth rather than flow across impermeable

     175 | Part 3.5. Making a Built Landscape: Cemeteries and Other Notes on Form
parking lots and building sites. By slowing the water and allowing it to be absorbed, these rain gar-
dens help recharge the aquifer beneath.

Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger
House and Its Environments

     About the Walk

     No matter how many different types of urban and rural environments a person may have experi-
     enced, people can recall a meaningful personal living condition--be it an apartment in a large com-
     plex or a single-family house. This part begins by discussing the development and values of the
     American Midwestern street and its typical landscape and homes. The discussion then moves on to a
     small home constructed in the early 1960s for Margaret Erlanger, a member of the Dance Depart-
     ment faculty. This home was designed by Jack S. Baker, a professor in the Illinois School of Architec-
     ture. The structure inverts many qualities of the typical neighboring houses while maintaining many
     aspects of them to make it an integral part of its neighborhood. The relationship between the user
     and structure to create a place suited to the owner's desires is emphasized. This unique home was
     bequeathed by Erlanger to the Illinois School of Architecture and now acts as both a home for visit-
     ing scholars and a retreat center for faculty colloquia.
     This walk begins at the intersection of West Indiana Avenue and South Lincoln Avenue in Urbana.
     Erlanger House is located at 303 West Indiana Avenue.

          Part 3.6. Orientation Map.

177 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
  In the 16th century the architect Baldassare Peruzzi and his protégé Sebastiano Serlio reasoned
  that there were two primary set designs for theatrical works that took place in the city: the scena
  tragica (tragic scene) and the scena comica (comic scene). The tragic scene was conveyed through
  heroic classical architecture and served as models for architects of the Renaissance. Structures
  included triumphal arches, obelisks, round arches, and palaces (see figure 3.6.1).

         Figure 3.6.1. Serlio's Tragic Scene from Tutte l'opere d'architettura et prospetiva.

  The perspective is laid out to reveal a space with buildings respectfully creating order. It is easy
  to imagine Shakespeare's tragedies in these heroic settings as one ponders the concepts of life,

                                          1

  mercy, and greed. The comic scene is populated with mostly medieval architecture, a ruin, and

1. Though many of Shakespeare's plays are set in the Middle Ages, the idea of a well-ordered and noble space supports the
  more serious subjects of plays such as Hamlet and contrasts with the more haphazard, unplanned spaces of happen­
  stance in the comic scene--the setting for plays like The Taming of the Shrew.
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 178
a church with greenery growing out of its dilapidated bell tower. Buildings undulate in and out,
which leaves a space less clearly defined than what's found in the tragic scene (see figure 3.6.2).

                                          Figure 3.6.2. Serlio's Comic Scene from Tutte
                                          l'opere d'architettura et prospetiva.

These served the Renaissance well. But the United States is the product of the Enlightenment. A
move toward the sciences--both natural and political--was at the foundation of the nation. As this
country grew the pioneer spirit and the preference for living with, and in, nature was a part of the
national psyche. Here, a different model was essential. Serlio had published one other scene, the
scena satyrica (satyric scene), that seems to embody the Midwestern small town. The satyric scene
might be imagined as a setting for A Midsummers Night's Dream with structures under a canopy
of trees lining a path (see figure 3.6.3).

       The American small town is a dream town. . . . It is the Enlightenment garden city rendered
       in infinite variations. . . 237. But Elm Street--Elm Street elevates it all, occasionally to the
       level of poetry. With Elm Street, European hegemony in the development of urban streets
       is challenged, and in the process Serlio's "Satyric Scene," long ignored by Europe, is resur-
       rected, civilized, and brought center stage. Elm Street is the quintessential tree-lined res-
       idential street. . . .
       The house sits free in continuous Arcadia, discreetly varying but always addressing the
       great colonnade of trees that structure the public realm. Here public and private are both
       accommodated in a respectful dialogue; and adherence to the conventions of street, side-

179 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
       walk, front yard, porch, and public rooms is precisely what allows invention and private

                               2

       variation.

A pre-World War II street with brick pavers and a clear sense of both public and private zones,
Indiana Avenue, between Orchard Street and Lincoln Avenue, is a version of this Elm Street, USA
(see figure 3.6.4).

Figure 3.6.3. Serlio's Satyric Scene from Tutte l'opere d'architettura
et prospetiva.

There is a beautiful balance created by the orderly adherence to simple patterns. On both sides of
the street is a green band before the presence of a sidewalk. This band is part of the city's right-
of-way but care for this zone is administered by the homeowners, who have planted turf grass,
ground cover, and flowers. The south side of the street also contains streetlights in this right-
of-way zone. The space between street and sidewalk is planted with shade trees, which provide
a primary ordering to the public realm. Combined with the driveways and streetlights a pleas-
ant rhythm is established. The lots on Indiana Avenue are typically a modest 60 feet wide and 130
feet deep. Most houses have detached garages located in the rear yards. The houses lining Indi-
ana Avenue are 1½ to 2½ stories tall. Though the styles of the houses vary, their masses are similar

2. Dennis, Court and Garden, 237, 240.

                                                                        Kevin J. Hinders | 180
as they create a public face to the street. Almost all the houses have pitched roofs and sit back
approximately 50 feet from the street curb. The syncopation of house, side yard, side yard, house
repeats down the street and is accented by the driveways to the garages in the rear. A wall of res-
idences is raised slightly above grade, which allows for basement light and ventilation and places
the houses above street level. A hierarchy of space overlooking the street creates an extended set
of front yards often overseen by front porches, also raised above street level. These porches once
served an important social function. Not only did they announce the front door and entry into the
home, but they provided a place to overlook the street and passersby. On warm summer evenings
they provided a place for residents to take in cooler evening breezes. The romanticized picture
is of young couples who would promenade their baby carriages or take evening walks with chil-
dren while neighbors discussed news of the day on the porches--overseeing the scene is a strong
aspect of the American dream. The very idea of community is fostered by this setting.

        Figure 3.6.4. Indiana Street, Urbana Illinois.

The public and private aspects of life are further reflected in the Indiana Avenue houses built prior
to World War II. The homes typically have a porch that serves the entry. Rooms such as offices/
studies and living rooms are placed at the front of the house to overlook the street. Kitchens and
informal rooms, such as family areas, are located to the rear. Kitchens typically are entered off the
back or side of the house. Though side yards occasionally have gardens, most gardens--especially

181 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
  the victory gardens created during the war to grow vegetables--are reserved for the backyard. The
  backyard is for the family and invited guests. It is a private realm, often fenced off from neighbors.
  After World War II a shift began to take place in the American house. Technological factors had a
  tremendous influence on the relationship between house and street. Three technological changes
  shall be discussed here: the growing use of the private automobile, the creation and proliferation

                                                                                                                3

  of air conditioning, and the invention of television.

                      Figure 3.6.5. Single Family Housing Lot Diagram. Read long description of figure.

  The automobile is credited with helping create the American suburb. The dream of owning one's
  own home was achievable for more people as getting to and from work became easier. After World
  War II the federal government, under President Dwight Eisenhower, created the Interstate High-
  way System. The highways made getting to workplaces and leisure activities faster and made peo-
  ple less dependent on commuter trains. As the arterial streets and highways began to crisscross
  the nation the countryside began to be consumed by suburbs. As the speed of the automobile
  increased so did the size of the suburban lot. The typical single-family house went from occupy-
  ing a narrow, deep lot to residing on wider, and then even wider, lots. Alleys were no longer built.

3. These are not stand-alone phenomena as they are dependent on fuel and advancements in energy delivery.
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 182
Homes began to incorporate attached garages. Initially these were one-car garages and discreet
but eventually the designs grew to two-car garages or even larger structures with large, electron-
ically operated doors prominently displayed to the street. The house that was once deeper than it
was wide is now often wider than it is deep (see figure 3.6.5).

                           Figure 3.6.6. Plans of Erlanger House. Image courtesy of Jeff Poss. Read
                           long description of figure.

Today the issue of sprawl is being questioned as people are becoming more aware of the depletion
of the Earth's resources and the burdens that fossil fuel use is placing on the environment.
The second technological factor dramatically affecting the house is the advent of air conditioning.
In the past, it was preferable to spend time in the summer evenings outdoors and wait for the
house to lose some of the heat gained through the course of a hot, sunny day. After the advent of
air conditioning, porches, especially covered porches, were often turned into weather-enclosed
spaces. These enclosures created additional interior living space. This internalization of the porch
tended to change the social quality of the street while also often changing the previous public-pri-
vate relationship. Today the back patio is the place reserved for outdoor activity. A simple privati-

183 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
zation has occurred. In many suburbs the porch is no longer a relevant feature and in some cases
is a decorative element at best.

                              Figure 3.6.7. Erlanger House vertical slot as seen from Indiana
                              Avenue. Image Courtesy of Ken McCown.

The third technological factor is television. Though radio held an important place in America's cul-
ture, the rise of television began to create an internalization to the American home. Social dia-
logue often became a discussion of the previous night's TV program. Milton Berle became "Mr.
Television" and "Uncle Miltie" was like a family member in your family room. Today, the legacy of
television, including aspects of computers and the Internet, continue to contribute to the inter-
nalization of the American household.
The comforts of the internal space, made greater by the ability to have more square footage, have
changed the character of residential streets in the US. Today, many suburbs do not incorporate
sidewalks. If they do, it is often only on one side of the street. As homes have become retreats
the grid has given way to the curved street and the cul-de-sac. The automobile often dictates the
distance the house sits back from the street, so when a garage begins to become a repository for
material no longer capable of being stored inside the house proper, there is at least a space pro-
vided to accommodate the displaced automobile.

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 184
  Figure 3.6.8. Erlanger House Interior view of the main space looking toward the front courtyard. Image courtesy
  of Ken McCown.

  The beauty of Indiana Avenue--Urbana's version of Elm Street--is fragile. Its success depends on
  the landscape and structures working in harmony. Any disruption in either leads to a greater
  destruction of the whole:

          Unfortunately, the very characteristics that give the small American town its positive qual-
          ities are also those that make it extremely vulnerable. Endless nuance and interpreta-
          tion are possible as long as the delicate balance of solids and voids is maintained. But the
          Neoclassical system is inherently biased in favor of the private icon, so balance is dou-
          bly dependent on maintenance of the public realm, the underpinnings of which are still,
          however faint, those of the classical structure of space--of street and square. What is not
          defined by the buildings must be completed by the trees, and slight weakening of either

                                                                                                                              4

          element can result in serious erosion of the system.

  The typical structure along Indiana Avenue has a prominent door and large windows overlooking
  the street. There is however an exception that demonstrates the rule. This house is an interesting
  insertion into the existing 1920s and 1930s street fabric. The house at 303 West Indiana Avenue

                                        5

  was built in 1964: a late addition to the neighborhood. It was designed by Professor Jack Baker of

4. Dennis, Court and Garden, 240.
5. Margaret Erlanger bequeathed the house to the University of Illinois School of Architecture in 1975.

   185 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
the Illinois School of Architecture for Margaret Erlanger, a leader and instructor of dance. Stylis-
tically the house is considered a work of modern architecture in the international style (see figure
3.6.6).

Figure 3.6.9. Erlanger House view across the discussion area to the rear yard. Image courtesy of Ken McCown.

Baker sought to create a unique building that met the needs and personality of its owner while
still fitting into the existing neighborhood. He was able to create a structure that accepts the basic
materials, massing, and setbacks of the adjacent homes while changing just about everything else.
The house is a two-story brick building with one bedroom and 1½ baths. It has a flat roof. Designed
for a single woman, it guarded her privacy by turning inward and limiting the house's exposure to
the street. Instead the house, which sits on Carle Park, has only two tall, transparent slot windows
that allow limited views to one of Urbana's most beautiful parks (see figure 3.6.7).
Instead Baker placed the house between two exterior courtyards to the north and south. He then
opened the house up with two-story-tall walls set into the thick brick frames. The mass of the
building is symmetrical but the interior layout is not. Entering in the east slot one encounters a
foyer with a wooden staircase to the left and a coat/storage closet to the right. This slot of space
moves through the building to provide a view of the backyard. The floor is sandblasted concrete.
Moving into the main space from the foyer one encounters a lowered discussion area, a floating

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 186
fireplace, and a large, two-story dance space/living room. This is the central volume of the house
(see figures 3.6.8-3.6.11).

Figure 3.6.10. Erlanger House interior view of the dance floor/living area. Image courtesy of Ken McCown.

To the north is a gravel-floored courtyard connected to the discussion area with large, sliding
glass doors with glass above. To the south is the rear yard, also connected to the interior with
large sliding glass doors and glass above. The effect is a large space that blurs interior and exterior.
The spaces are defined more by the horizontal floor and ceiling than by the adjacent walls, which
have been designed to float between the entry and the kitchen. Materials are kept to a very limited
palette. Floors are concrete and wood on the first floor. Baker used three-wythe-thick masonry
bearing wall construction for the exterior walls and left the brick exposed on the interior. Glass
is inserted with limited framing to accentuate the interior/exterior connections. When not brick,
interior walls are limited to painted white drywall and painted black louvers for the mechanical
systems. The building is detailed to bring the gravel on the outside north court into the interior
space. The concrete aggregate in the floors matches the gravel in the courtyards. On the south
side a concrete pad moves from inside to outside, one step above the wooden floor of the dance
space.
The stairs have no outer handrail. Baker was well aware that the owner's movements were an inte-
gral aspect of the house. Ascending the stairs one moves across a bridge to the bedroom (see fig-
ures 3.6.12-3.6.14).

187 | Part 3.6. The Home and the Street: Erlanger House and Its Environments
                         Figure 3.6.11. View of Erlanger House Exterior Courtyard at the sliding doors
                         separating the exterior and interior. Image courtesy of Ken McCown.

This room floats above the discussion space with a bathroom beyond. Baker minimized the layout
to a simple east-west floor suspended above the main north-south axis. This space is detailed to
accentuate the suspended, hovering sense. The bedroom has no walls to the north and south. No
one can look into the bedroom. The floors are notched so as to not meet the exterior walls. Baker
was so concerned with minimizing the design and accommodating his client that he placed the
light switches under the carpeting to be activated by a tap of the foot.
Even the exterior landscaping is kept minimal. The yard is raised several steps above the sidewalk

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 188
and filled with gravel and ground cover. No garage was provided. Maintenance was kept as low as
possible.

                                       Figures 3.6.12. Erlanger House entry slot with stairs.
                                       Image courtesy of Ken McCown.

Erlanger House is perceived by some as an eyesore and others as one of the finest pieces of
modern architecture in Champaign. It is historically contextual. In the late 1950s and early 1960s
numerous architects began creating symmetrical buildings--long disdained by the modern move-
ment. Today visitors with permission are able to experience the space and determine for them-
selves the appropriateness of the house.

PART 4. TECHNICAL ASPECTS OF
ARCHITECTURE

       Overview

       This part will discuss the professional responsibility of the designer (architect or designer) to the
       client (the owner) and the general public, beginning with an outline of general welfare and the need
       to adhere to codes. The text then progresses to an outline of typical services provided by the archi-
       tect/designer. This part uses three walks to illustrate technical responses to design requirements.
       Parts on mechanical systems (supply and demand) and structures give examples of how designers
       use codes and technical principles to inform the creation of design solutions.

         The Congress shall have Power To lay and collect Taxes, Duties, Imposts and Excises, to pay
         the Debts and provide for the common Defense and general Welfare of the United States;

                                                                                                                                                                                               1

         but all Duties, Imposts and Excises shall be uniform throughout the United States; . . .
         To make all Laws which shall be necessary and proper for carrying into Execution the fore-
         going Powers, and all other Powers vested by this Constitution in the Government of the
         United States, or in any Department or Officer thereof.
         --US Constitution, Article 1, Section 8

         We, the People of the State of Illinois--grateful to Almighty God for the civil, political and
         religious liberty which He has permitted us to enjoy and seeking His blessing upon our
         endeavors--in order to provide for the health, safety and welfare of the people; maintain
         a representative and orderly government; eliminate poverty and inequality; assure legal,
         social and economic justice; provide opportunity for the fullest development of the indi-
         vidual; insure domestic tranquility; provide for the common defense; and secure the bless-
         ings of freedom and liberty to ourselves and our posterity--do ordain and establish this
         Constitution for the State of Illinois.

1. The article goes on to describe the powers included, such as establishing a post office, coining money, and raising an
  army and a navy.
  191 | Part 4. Technical Aspects of Architecture
          --Preamble, Constitution of the State of Illinois

  The US Constitution, along with most state constitutions, has a statement about general welfare:

                                                                                                                                                                                                          2

  the concern of the government for the health, peace, freedom, and safety of its citizens. The
  design of the built environment is greatly affected by these beliefs. Laws and codes have been
  adopted to protect health, safety, and welfare (of the public and the owner and future owners of
  a structure). Whereas in some cultures the architect is considered a purveyor of culture, in the
  United States a pragmatic approach is taken. In the United States architects and those partici-
  pating in the design of buildings are legally responsible to two primary groups: their clients and
  the general public. The architect is bound by law to deliver contractually agreed upon services
  for the owner and must also meet the requirements of codes adopted by law by the local com-
  munity, state, and federal governments. These codes (and sometimes covenants) are expected to
  protect the health, safety, and welfare of the community--thus fulfilling an obligation expressly
  stated in the US and Illinois Constitutions. Hence the design should fulfill the needs of the client
  while also meeting base standards to protect the owner and the public. Codes are created by orga-
  nizations that set standards. These organizations (for example, the International Code Council)
  operate independently from the jurisdictions responsible for adopting and enforcing codes. If a
  community does not specify the codes that will govern structures in their area, then the designer
  is expected to act "reasonably and responsibly," which typically means the designer will rely on
  best practices and/or codes they would normally use elsewhere. Codes also prescribe aspects of
  design that the community has declared valuable. For example, a local municipality may decide
  that there will be a minimum setback between buildings. For whatever reason, be it fire safety, pri-
  vacy, or servicing, this setback becomes the design criteria for that community.

  Adherence to codes is important because it assures a base level of quality control. It also gives
  future owners a level of assurance as to what they might expect from a specific structure built at a
  specific time under specific code restrictions. Since ownership of constructed environments often
  change hands, it is imperative that buildings have an acceptable, documented standard to ensure
  fairness. Laws and codes are enacted to protect not only the owner constructing the project but
  also future owners and occupants.

  The State of Illinois does not have a required building code per se. Instead, units of local govern-
  ment have authority to adopt and enforce building codes, zoning ordinances, and other instru-
  ments related to construction. If a local municipality does not have a code, only then does the
  State of Illinois have authority to impose a code compliance. In this way, the local authority deter-
  mines the laws for its constituents. It is important to note, however, that there is one federal code

2. "Alexander Hamilton maintained that the clause granted Congress the power to spend without limitation for the general
   welfare of the nation" (see "General Welfare," Law Library--American Law and Legal Information, accessed June 12, 2025,
   https://law.jrank.org/pages/7116/General-Welfare.html).

                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 192
  that supersedes local authorities: the Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA). First created in 1990
  and administered by the US Department of Justice Civil Rights Division, this code creates guide-
  lines for inclusive design. The design standards are to assist those with disabilities including sight
  and hearing impairment and those with mobility issues--such as those who use a wheelchair. ADA
  adherence took a moral issue and raised it to a legal one.

  To gain some insight into codes, this text will look at the City of Urbana. The city has adopted the

                                                                                                                   3

  International Code Council's 2021 ICC Model Codes.

     · Updated Standards:
        The 2021 edition incorporates changes and updates based on advancements in technology,
        construction practices, and lessons learned from previous editions.

     · Structural Provisions:
        The 2021 IBC includes new construction types for tall wood or mass timber buildings (Type
        4A, 4B, and 4C) and provisions for hillside settings in the IRC.

     · Fire Safety Enhancements:
        The 2021 IFC includes provisions for emergency responder radio communication coverage in
        new buildings and other fire safety measures.

     · Energy Conservation:
        The 2021 IECC focuses on improving energy efficiency in buildings, contributing to sustain-
        ability and reducing energy consumption.

     · Residential Building:
        The 2021 IRC includes specific requirements for one- and two-family dwellings, including
        braced wall lines, fire separation, and emergency escape and rescue openings.

  In addition to these codes the city has zoning acts that determine what kind of building uses may
  be allowed in specific areas and parcels. Zoning guides the development of areas for specific uses
  such as industrial parks, residential neighborhoods, and mixed-use developments, to name a few.
  Zoning can also determine the sizes and heights of allowable structures. It cannot be overstated
  that adherence to prescribed zoning and codes has an enormous effect on the design of the built
  environment.

  Other great determinants of the form of the built environment are the needs and desires of prop-
  erty owners. Whether the owner is an individual, a company, or a governmental entity, the owner's
  goals dramatically shape the built outcome. As has been previously stated, buildings reflect con-
  text, ideals, and aesthetics but they also meet a specific owner's needs. It is good to understand the
  process that leads to the creation of a structure or a built landscape. The following text attempts

3. Other types of delivery methods include design/build and turnkey.

   193 | Part 4. Technical Aspects of Architecture
to clarify one process that leads to a design from the architect/designer's point of view in relation
to a legal responsibility.

An agreement between three entities is the most common method for the creation of a built
design. The prevalent process to having a building constructed (though certainly not the only one)

                                                                                                                                                                                           4

is when contracts are made between an owner, an architect (designer), and a contractor. This cre-
ates an owner-architect-contractor relationship. The owner provides the site and capital for the
project, the architect provides the design expertise, and the contractor is responsible for the con-
struction of the project. There are many variations on this model.

The owner-architect agreement usually outlines the scope of work and sets the expectations and
duties of each party for the project. A typical agreement has the following design services (though
contracts may be created that include greater or fewer services, this is a guideline for understand-
ing the general services provided by the design professional): schematic design, design develop-
ment, construction documents, bidding, and construction administration (includes payment). The
American Institute of Architects (AIA) has created standard contracts for different agreements.
The AIA contracts clarify each of these services. Below is a synopsis of these basic services.

Predesign is typically an additional service and is not included in a basic owner-architect agree-
ment. Predesign refers to efforts made to assist the owner in clarifying the project. This may
include programming, which is when the project's needs are clarified. Spaces and sizes are deter-
mined and project needs are worked out before design is commenced.

Schematic design involves the creation of early designs for the owner's review. The designer pre-
pares a preliminary evaluation of the owner's program, schedule, budget, site, and other aspects
of the project to generate at least one schematic design. During this phase, multiple approaches to
the problems are typically considered and explored, such as material choices, sustainability com-
ponents, building siting and massing, and cost. The designer's ideas are presented to the owner,
often in drawing and study model form, to determine the solution with the owner.

After a schematic solution is accepted the process moves to the design development stage. Here
the project continues to change and more explorations are undertaken in relation to technical
aspects of the project. The work often involves consultants to the architect specializing in areas
the project may require (these often include experts in heating, ventilation, and air conditioning
[HVAC]; plumbing; electrical systems; structural engineering; civil engineering; soils; and others,
depending on the project). These consultants may have contracts with the architect or may have
contracts directly with the owner. In some cases, the design firm has these services in-house,

4. See Ramos, "Bid like a Winner."

                                    Kevin J. Hinders | 194
  which means the firm supplies both design and engineering services. The estimate of the cost of
  work is updated and the owner's approval is again requested.

  Once the designed project is determined to meet the needs expressed by the owner within an
  allocated estimate of probable cost, it can advance to the construction documents phase. Here the
  project has drawings and specifications refined further to describe the expectations of the struc-
  ture to be built and outline these expectations for the contractor. Specifications are written doc-
  uments that describe the scope of work, the materials to be used, and the methods of installation
  along with the quality of work required. Specifications are combined with construction drawings,
  which illustrate the project. These documents are meant to communicate the owner's intentions
  for the building so that the contractor can determine how it will be constructed.

  On completion of the construction drawings a project may be sent to the procurement phase. This
  involves either a bid process or a negotiated proposal. "A construction bid is the process of pro-
  viding a potential customer with a proposal to build or manage the building of a structure. It's
  also the method through which subcontractors make their proposals for their services to general

                               5

  contractors." During this phase the owner may choose to select specific contractors to provide
  a proposal for the construction of the project or may open the bidding up to interested, qualified
  contractors. During the procurement phase the designer assists by distributing documents, orga-
  nizing information for potential contractors, and guiding the bidding or negotiation process. The
  architect supplies the bidders with instructions and construction documents and clarifies ques-
  tions prior to bid submittal.

  After the successful selection of the contractor and an agreement between the owner and the
  contractor has been reached, the project enters the permitting and building stages. The designer
  enters into the construction administration phase. The designer provides for the administration
  of the contract between the owner and the contractor. In this phase the designer acts on behalf
  of the owner by evaluating the work of the contractor and advising the owner on the quality and
  progress of the project. The designer may reject work on behalf of the owner if he/she deter-
  mines it does not conform to the construction documents. The architect also acts for the owner by
  observing and verifying the completion of work so that the contractor can be paid. The architect
  issues certificates of payment after different agreed-on completions. The architect also reviews
  submittals from the contractor that assist in the completion of the project and answers questions
  that arise from the contractor as the project progresses. After passing final inspections and the
  transfer of warranties and documents, the project may be considered complete.

  There are numerous additional services and variations on these basic arrangements. Some firms
  offer design/build services, which is when a designer and a construction company have a contract

5. This information is taken from the US National CAD Standard.

   195 | Part 4. Technical Aspects of Architecture
  for both services. At other times a designer may be selected for only the first phases of the project
  and a second architect of record assumes the legal responsibility for the construction documents
  and the execution of the design. This is especially common when a designer is working in a foreign
  country and the legal situation calls for a local presence to complete the construction documents
  and oversee construction administration.
  The design of buildings and their sites may require an extensive team of contributors with special-
  izations, especially on larger projects. A typical project may have numerous considerations. The
  following is a list of drawings often used on a project, the contributors, and the letter designation

                                                                                                                                                                                                                    6

  for the drawing sets they provide in parentheses in accordance with the National CAD Standard:
  general (G), which includes sheet lists, symbols, abbreviations, and the code summary--this is usu-
  ally created by the architect; architecture (A), which includes plans, sections, elevations, large-
  scale views, details, schedules and diagrams, and 3D representations; civil engineering (C), which
  includes parking, roads, and related infrastructure; landscape design (L), which includes the con-
  trol of water runoff and plantings; mechanical engineering (M); plumbing engineering (P); process
  (D); electrical engineering (E); structural engineering (S); interiors (I); fire protection (F); equip-
  ment (Q); telecommunications (T); distributed energy (W); and other disciplines (X).
  The walks in this part will address the building mechanical systems (supply and demand) and
  structural systems to introduce the technical aspects of creating the built environment and will
  provide examples of how engineering is integral in the design of the built environment. Though
  not comprehensive, these examples illustrate the complexities of constructing in today's environ-
  ment.

   code. In July 2025, Urbana operated under the 2021 ICC Model Codes.
