Part 4.1. Mechanical Systems: Supply

     About the Walk

     The university has an elaborate infrastructure to service its buildings and their occupants. The uni-
     versity maintains several facilities that produce heat, cooling, and electricity for the campus. These
     facilities include miles of steam service tunnels, shallow trench steam lines and direct buried steam
     line. There are other underground utilities such as direct buried chill water pipes, high voltage elec-
     trical conduits, natural gas pipelines, domestic water lines, sanitary sewage piping and storm sewer
     piping. The Abbott Power Plant produces the steam to over 300 customers. The steam is used for
     heating and domestic hot water usage. It is enough thermal energy for a city of over 45,000 people
     plus research facilities. Electricity and some steam is used to produce chilled water which serves
     most of the larger buildings on campus. There is also a large chilled water storage tank to reduce the
     total cost of cooling by reducing peak electric demand costs. This part focuses on this infrastructure
     that supplies heating, cooling, and electricity to the campus.
     This walk begins at the Abbott Power Plant located at 117 South Oak Street in Champaign. The walk
     also includes a tour of the Oak Street Chiller Plant located at 1305 South Oak Street in Champaign.

The university maintains a substantial infrastructure that services the buildings and grounds of the
campus. This infrastructure includes provisions for heating, cooling, electricity, telecommunica-
tions and computer access, water supply for both domestic use and fire safety, sanitary drainage,
stormwater drainage, and waste removal. These can be simplified into two areas: supply and post
consumption. In earlier times, structures on the prairie were stand-alone constructions with fire-
places, wells, and septic fields serving the needs of the inhabitants. Supplies came from nearby
forests, streams, and wells constructed for the inhabitants. Waste was either recycled, burned,
buried, or sent downstream. Today, elaborate systems have been created to service buildings and
their occupants. Often these systems are not even visible as more and more infrastructure is being
placed underground.

Supply of heating, electricity, and cooling is a substantial aspect of the university's budget and
is virtually unseen in day-to-day operations. As time has progressed these needs have expanded
dramatically The university has had four power plants to date. The first was built in 1881 and
was replaced in 1898. Both were located near Gregory Hall. In 1910 the Mathews Avenue Heating,
Lighting, and Power Plant was brought online. The central location on Mathews Avenue, north of

197 | Part 4.1. Mechanical Systems: Supply
Green Street, was preferred as the tunnels and supply lines could be kept short. As the university
expanded south, however, it became clear that a larger plant would be necessary. A compromise
was struck when locating the new Abbott Power Plant. Ideally the plant would be in the center of
the campus to limit the distances of supply runs but two factors contributed to selecting the cur-
rent location. First, it was preferable to put the plant at the edge of campus for aesthetic reasons.
Second, the plant would be near the Illinois Central Railroad tracks to facilitate train cars filled

                                                                                                                             1

with the coal needed to fuel boilers and power generators.

      Part 4.1. Orientation Map.
1. Michael Keefe Brewer, email message to the author, August 8, 2025.

                                                                       Kevin J. Hinders | 198
  Since the Abbott Power Plant's construction in 1941, it has increased capacity to keep up with the
  demands of the expanding university.

          When Abbott was constructed the university's primary need was for steam to heat build-
          ings and for domestic water use (and sometimes for cooling). The peak electric load was
          very small on a per square foot of building space compared the buildings today. In the
          1940's the electric load was for lighting and some fans. There was not air conditioning. Now
          the buildings are almost all air conditioned. Additionally, some research spaces have very

                                                                                                                                        2

          strict indoor air temperature and humidity requirement.

  Located at 1117 South Oak Street, Abbott Power Plant is a cogeneration facility, which means that
  it simultaneously produces both steam and electricity and therefore is extremely efficient. With
  congeneration, a byproduct of the steam creation is the opportunity to produce electricity, which

                                                                                                                                                                                                               3

  makes it a two-for-one service that burns fuel to provide both heat and electricity to buildings.

  The university's cogeneration plant is over 80 percent efficient, which means less than 20 percent

                                                                                                                                                        4

  energy loss in the transformation of energy from one form to another. A typical power plant's pur-
  pose is to produce electricity, the most useful form of energy. Since the typical power plant does
  not have a large heat sink to absorb its heat by-product (like university buildings, for example) it
  loses a large amount of energy, which makes it less efficient. According to Bright Hub Engineering,
  most coal, natural gas, and fuel oil power plants run at efficiencies of between 30 and 45 percent
  (see "The Efficiency of Power Plants").

          Two gas turbines, which can operate on natural gas (or in an emergency fuel oil), are uti-
          lized to generate electricity. Each gas turbine is connected to a heat recovery steam gen-
          erator (HRSG) that captures the hot exhaust gases from the turbine and uses that heat
          to generate steam. Three gas-fired boilers (natural gas and/or fuel oil) and two coal-fired
          boilers convert water to steam.

          The steam generated in the HRSGs and boilers is used to operate steam turbine generators
          (STGs). The STGs use the high pressure steam from the boilers/HRSGs to spin a steam tur-
          bine that drives a generator to produce electricity. The low pressure exhaust steam from

2. Ibid.
3. "A large power plant away from a thermal use has to reject heat to condense steam back to water to complete the cycle.

   Cogeneration system harvests the latent heat of steam condensing back to water by sending the relatively lower pres­
   sure turbine exhaust steam to heat a building or heat exchanger. This almost doubles the economic thermal efficiency
   as compare to a remote power plant. The key is that the thermal and electrical loads have to be geographically close and
   comparable in size." Ibid.
4. Michael Larson, email message to the author, August 11, 2025.

   199 | Part 4.1. Mechanical Systems: Supply
the turbines is sent to campus and used to serve campus energy needs including but not

                                                                                                                             5

limited to space heating, water heating, and space cooling.

Figure 4.1.1. Power Plant Diagram. Water is heated by a boiler which burns coal, fuel oil or natural gas. The steam
produced is sent to power turbines which produce electricity. This steam is then sent to the campus. Some steam
is sent to condensers which then is sent back to the feedwater pumps. Once steam is used by the campus the
remaining steam/condensed water is looped back to the power plant.

The power plant supplies low-pressure steam to over 250 buildings on campus while providing 88

                                                                                                                                                                                                  6

percent of the university's electrical needs by producing 85 megawatts (MW) of electricity.

The university chooses to remain flexible with its choice of fuel. Of the seven boilers in Abbott
three are duel fuel (natural gas or fuel oil), two are coal fired, and two are HRSGs. Having its own
plant, the university is able to negotiate preferred rates for fuels and even has its own natural gas
line, which runs from Monticello. This flexibility helps the university economize and be able to
change fuel when prices fluctuate. For example, should one fuel become scarce or have a price
spike, the power plant has the ability to shift to another fuel source to economize and deliver unin-
terrupted service. More recently with Illinois decommissioning large coal power plants with some
being converted to natural gas there have been natural gas shortages in the winter. There have
also been electrical shortages in the summer driving up the demand prices enormously. Having
onsite generation and fuel redundancy has helped manage that energy supply risk.

5. Facilities and Services, "Abbott Power Plant."
6. Larson, email message to the author, August 11, 2025.

                                                          Kevin J. Hinders | 200
   The Abbott Power Plant is also environmentally compliant. A scrubber was installed in 1986 to
   clean flue gases and help protect the environment:

           Using the best available air pollution control technology, Abbott Power Plant meets or
           exceeds all Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) emission standards. Electrostatic pre-
           cipitators (ESP) and a flue gas desulfurization unit (scrubber), supported by a Continuous
           Emission Monitoring System (CEMS) in the stack, remove90% of air pollutants, providing
           significant environmental benefits compared to conventional electric generation and heat-
           only systems. Efficient cogeneration coupled with emission reduction equipment have
           reduced carbon dioxide emissions by 101,000 tons per year, the equivalent of removing

                                                                                                                                                                                                7

           18,000 automobiles off Illinois highways or the reforesting of 21,000 acres of land.

   Steam is distributed through three routes: walkable steam tunnels; shallow steam tunnels large
   enough for only two pipes (about 9 miles in total across campus); and direct-buried piping sys-
   tems, which utilize no tunnels (approximately 11 miles). All the tunnels are strictly off-limits to
   unauthorized individuals. An unauthorized person might become lost in the labyrinth of occupi-
   able, below-ground tunnels, where temperatures can reach 140 degrees Fahrenheit and lead to
   death. The university locks and monitors the entire system to safeguard against such possibilities.
   Tours of the tunnels are no longer available. Once steam is delivered to buildings it is distributed
   through radiators, hot water supplies, heating systems, and heat exchangers. The steam is also
   used to heat tap water, showers, and pools.

   The electricity produced by Abbott is shipped to the Main Campus Substation and accounts for

                                                                                                                                                                                                  8

   the majority of the electricity used on campus--approximately 65 percent in 2023-2025.

   Renewable energy does fulfill a portion of the university's electric needs. In 2015, the university
   created Solar Farm 1.0, located south of Windsor Road near Neil Street on almost 21 acres. In its
   first year of operation it produced 2 percent of the university's electrical needs. Solar Farm 2.0 is a
   10 MW facility that occupies approximately 54 acres between First Street and Dunlap Avenue just
   north of Curtis Road. Solar Farms 1.0 and 2.0 provide approximately 6 percent of the campus elec-

                            9

   tric needs. There are discussions for a future Solar Farm 3.0.

                                                                                                                                                                                                                10

   In December 2016 the university signed a power purchase agreement with a nearby wind farm. It
   is expected that the university's renewable sources will account for approximately 9 percent of its
   usage between 2017 and 2027. The university is aiming for carbon neutrality by the year 2050.

Following World War II the United States began to use air conditioning on a large scale. At that

constructed on campus it would have an accompanying cooling tower installed. Older buildings
were retrofitted with rooftop units when possible and window units were placed for specified
spaces.

Part 4.2. Mechanical Systems and Codes:
Demand

     About the Walk

     Buildings consume large amounts of energy and water. To provide comfortable, productive spaces
     buildings have the single greatest demand for energy on campus. A tour through Temple Hoyne
     Buell Hall provides an example of how building systems deliver a comfortable environment to its end
     users after receiving supply from the infrastructure, including Abbott Power Plant. Beginning with
     the two mechanical rooms, which house the main pumps and blowers, the principles of heating and
     cooling using a combination of radiant and forced air are explained. As one progresses through the
     building, explanations of water supply for domestic use, sprinklers, and electrical supply are pro-
     vided. Storm drainage, life-safety issues, and general code requirements are also discussed.
     This walk begins at Temple Hoyne Buell Hall located at 611 East Lorado Taft Drive in Champaign.
     Note: due to security issues, access to the mechanical rooms requires university permission.

Temple Hoyne Buell Hall (TBH), designed by Perkins Will, was conceived by its benefactor, Temple
Hoyne Buell, to be a place where the Departments of Architecture, Urban and Regional Planning,
and Landscape Architecture (the three concentrations within the College of Fine and Applied Arts
that are involved with the design of the built environment) could come together. Through the
sharing of a single building, interdisciplinary teaching and research might be undertaken. With
this vision in mind the parti is clear: The first floor of the main bar along the South Quad houses
the administrative offices of each department. Above this bar are the architecture studios. In the
bar along the military axis is the Plym Auditorium with the studios and classrooms dedicated
to urban planning and landscape architecture above. The third mass--a long, sweeping, curved
bar--contains faculty offices. The faculty are not segregated by discipline. Instead one finds a
mixed neighborhood of faculty from all three areas on the second floor, second mezzanine, and
third floor. These three bars form an interior space: the Blicharski Atrium. The atrium is a multi-
purpose space where planned and spontaneous functions can occur. Because the building leaves
much of its plumbing, heating and cooling ductwork, conduits, and other infrastructure exposed,
it lends itself to teaching students about mechanical systems (see figure 4.2.1).

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 204
            Part 4.2. Orientation Map.

Underlying and supporting this functional/conceptual layout of TBH are two factors that go hand
in hand with the designer's ideas of spatial layout: mechanical systems and how the structure
meets building codes. Most systems in other structures are not readily seen, but in TBH, however,
the systems are mostly exposed, labeled, and comprehensible.
This TBH tour begins in the lowest levels of the building--the mechanical rooms (these are
accessed via stairs and there has been no update for wheelchair access since they are not a part of
the public areas). The mechanical rooms announce their presence to the visitor. Cylindrical con-
crete forms located near the south and west entrances provide fresh air intake to the mechanical
systems. These spaces are grated to prevent unauthorized access and accidents while allowing air

  ment.

  Since the water table in Urbana and Cham-
  paign is such that when digging down 7 or 8

  is important to provide pumps that can move
  water away from the building. Without this

  possible structural damage. Pumps in the
  North Mechanical Room remove groundwater
  to alleviate hydrostatic pressure on the walls
  and floors of the structure. Groundwater is
  gravity fed to large cisterns below the floors in
  the basements. Building roof drains also feed
  into these cisterns. Once the cisterns reach
  capacity the water is pumped to storm drains.
  The principle is the same as in residential

  The North Mechanical Room also has electrical panels and monitoring equipment, but the majority
  of the space is set up for heating, ventilation, and air conditioning (HVAC) equipment. Large
  amounts of ductwork, blower motors, and heating and cooling coils help manage temperatures in
  the 85,400 square feet of TBH. Two large rectangular metal boxes fed by pipes with ductwork at
  each end are used to heat and cool different zones. Providing zoned heating and cooling allows for
  flexibility and greater control and is an effective way to economize. For example, when the Plym

1. The water table is the local upper surface where groundwater saturation begins. This zone varies across the planet. For
  those who have visited a beach and dug down to make a sandcastle one can readily understand the water table as the
  level at which one digs and finds the sand saturated with water. It generally marks the top of the zone where the pores
  in the surrounding rocks or sand are fully saturated. In Urbana-Champaign the water table is close to the surface since
  the area was a swamp for many centuries.

                                                          TBH utilizes a system that provides forced air
                                                          and radiant hot water. This dual system takes
                                                          advantage of radiant hot water sent to the

                                                          TBH uses large forced-air mechanical units
                                                          (see figure 4.2.3).

                                                          Large blower motors pull air from the outside

                                                          and blend this fresh air with air that has

                                                          already been supplied to the building. This is

                                                          referred to as return air, since it is returning to

Figure 4.2.2. Temple Hoyne Buell Hall mechanical          be reheated or recooled, depending on need.
systems zoning diagram. Read long description of figure.  This air is mixed together and some air is

exhausted from the system to get an appropriate mixture of fresh and treated air. This air is then

sent through filters before it is heated or cooled. The heating and cooling coils in the system con-

tain liquids that have reached a desired temperature to condition the air properly. As the air passes

over the coils it is conditioned and then sent to habitable spaces. These habitable spaces contain

thermostats that allow users to further control the temperature. The location where the air enters

the space is called the point of delivery. TBH uses a variable air volume (VAV) HVAC system. Unlike

constant air volume (CAV) systems, which supply a constant airflow at a variable temperature,

VAV systems vary the airflow at a constant temperature. So what does that mean? In a CAV system

the air is sent to rooms at a temperature created when the air leaves the central mechanical sys-

tem. Depending on the distance the air has to travel the temperature can change dramatically

before it gets near the end of its run. The VAV system seeks to resolve this problem by sending air

heated to a specified temperature and then allowing the user to vary the amount of the treated air

brought into the space. The VAV unit (also known as a VAV box) has a fan that is controlled by a

207 | Part 4.2. Mechanical Systems and Codes: Demand
local thermostat. The VAV box operates this fan and a damper system that allows or prohibits
treated air into the space. In addition to providing a VAV box at the point of delivery, TBH has
an added amenity. The VAV boxes have a reheat unit that uses radiant hot water supplied by the
primary hot water line. This additional heating coil can reheat the air before it enters the space.
Together the primary blower motors and the VAV units move the air to the spaces and provide
end users with a greater amount of thermostatic control. Ducts not only supply air to the spaces
but also evacuate air from the spaces. Just as it is nearly impossible to blow air through a clogged
straw, it is necessary to provide an exit tube for air to return to the mechanical room. This return
air is then recycled back into the system to complete the loop.

  return and extends to the roofline). The ducts service the faculty offices with both supply and
  return air. Once the air is taken to the roofline it is sent down between the walls of the faculty

  Near these tubes is the west-facing wall of the atrium. This floor-to-ceiling wall of glass is tem-
  pered by a line of radiators resting near the floor at the window wall. Each radiator appears to be
  a linear box with a pipe fitting into it at the base at each end. This folded metal box houses a fin
  tube. Appropriately named, a fin tube is a pipe that has a series of metal pieces, or fins, that are
  heated by the hot water supply coming from the mechanical room. The fins are spaced about ¼
  inch apart, which allows them to maximize the amount of air they come in contact with as an air
  current sweeps past them. This current of air--cool at the bottom before being heated--then rises
  along the inside face of the glass, effectively creating a thermal blanket for the building. In TBH
  every exterior window has a radiating fin tube mounted near its base. By coupling this technology

The mechanical systems chosen, in this case, also have aesthetic design considerations. When the
weather is benign it might be preferable to have a series of doors that could open to the court-
yard outside. Even the tiles of the atrium seem to sense this desire as the designers and installers
took effort to line the interior tile joints with exterior paving joints. Care was taken to match the
appearance of the interior vinyl tile with the exterior concrete pavers so that their sizes match
exactly. Here is the notion of a space that continues from inside to outside, just as the atrium
stairs (south side) line up with the cascading terraces outside. A conflict, however, arises when the
mechanical system of radiators does not allow the glass to be operable along the western face of

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 210
the atrium. The compromise struck is to have a visual connection but not a physical one. Instead a
small door is located around the corner for courtyard access.
Across the atrium is a long corridor that serves a computer lab and three seminar spaces. Unex-
pectedly, at the end of the corridor is an illuminated exit sign. Passing through a pair of doors
one encounters a short, wide landing before descending a full flight of stairs. This corridor exit is
the solution to a problem of fire safety egress. A corridor of greater than 20 feet that results in
a dead end is considered a fire hazard. Someone could get to the end of such a corridor and be
enveloped by smoke or fire (for example, smoke coming out of the computer lab). Finding the dead
end, they may not be able to escape the corridor before being overcome by smoke inhalation. In
this instance the solution was to descend to a level dedicated to the South Mechanical Room. The
exiting corridor then links to the south fire stairs, which ascend two stories before providing an
at-grade exit.
The South Mechanical Room is similar to the one on the north side but here is where the steam
from Abbott Power Plant enters TBH and is gradually turned into hot water. From both mechanical
rooms it is possible to enter their air intake spaces. These are the same spaces one passes when
entering the building through the south and west doors. The spaces are cubic caverns with cir-
cular oculi that frame the sky. On sunny days these oculi cast ovoid shadows across their spaces.
Constructed of cast concrete retaining walls and infill concrete block masonry units, the spaces
have a quiet, zen-like quality (see figure 4.2.6).

Figure 4.2.6. Temple Hoyne Buell Hall mechanical system air intake. Left: The south air intake. Middle: South air
intake looking up. Right: The north intake looking out.

211 | Part 4.2. Mechanical Systems and Codes: Demand
On the sides of the south air intake is a wall with air filters lining the top edge. These filters feed
fresh air into the nearby fire stairs. On exiting the mechanical room and ascending the stairs, one
is in a space dedicated to vertical circulation and fire safety. These stairs have glass that looks out
onto the exterior, but whenever the stairs abut an interior space the material shifts to concrete.

Fire stairwells are required to be designed to withstand a specified period of time before fire or
smoke can adversely affect the space and persons inside. The TBH fire stairs are considered fire-
rated enclosures. That means the spaces are designed so that someone could take refuge in the
stairwells for a time determined by code. Special fire-rated doors and construction assemblies are
used to protect people. The fire rating depends on a building's use and size and whether it has fire
sprinklers.

TBH has a fire rating designation of B, for business: "Business Occupancies include office, pro-
fessional, and service activities, and storage of related records and accounts. Business Occupancy
also includes educational facilities past the 12th grade, but does not include retail or wholesale

                                                                                                           4

sales, which are classified as Group M Mercantile."

The Plym Auditorium has an occupancy designation of A-3, for Assembly: "This group includes
recreational, amusement, and religious worship uses not specifically covered by other Assembly
subgroups, including, for example, galleries, churches, community halls, courtrooms, dance halls,

                                                                                                 5

indoor sports facilities without fixed seating."

Typically, any space exceeding an occupancy of fifty people is required to have two or more sepa-
rated exit doors. Plym Auditorium seats more than 200 people so it is required to have a minimum
of two means of egress. The width of these doors corresponds to the human occupancy load. This
is important in case of fire, but it is also a practical solution for people using the space. Several
studios in TBH also fall into this category, as does the Blicharski Atrium.

Since TBH is a four-story building it was required to have two-hour fire-rated stairway enclosures
when it was built. Typically, these spaces must have self-closing, fire-resistant doors to prevent
smoke infiltration and burn through. The code makes allowances to lower fire ratings if the build-
ings have sprinkler systems with the idea that the sprinklers will lessen the life-safety hazards and
therefore the duration one would need to inhabit the fire-rated enclosures would decrease. The
time designation is useful to consider in case of emergency. For example, a two-hour-rated enclo-
sure can be used to help determine the time one could expect for life-safety personnel, such as
firefighters, to have to save those awaiting rescue inside. This is particularly important for those
unable to ascend or descend stairs. In case of emergency, elevators are only to be used by fire-

5. Ibid.

fighters, who have special key access. All fire stairs are required to have positive air pressure with
their adjacent surroundings. This feature allows someone to open the door to the fire stairs with-
out allowing smoke to enter the space. The filters observed at the south mechanical air intake
serve to filter smoke before air is fed into the space with the fire stairs. Codes now require that fire
stairs have an area outside the zone of circulation for those unable to use the stairs as they await
assistance. This is referred to as the area of rescue and it must meet minimum space requirements
and allow others fleeing the building an unobstructed path to safety. The south fire stairs have a
heavy metal gate that opens from the basement in the direction one fleeing a fire would take to
exit the building. In the United States all emergency exits should open in the direction of travel
to flee the hazard. The gate at the ground floor of the south stairwell is there to protect someone
from an upper floor who might mistakenly attempt to descend beyond the fire exit door. In the
event of a fire this feature might save a disoriented person's life.

       Figure 4.2.7. Image of the Centre Georges Pompidou at the Beaubourg Area. Paris, France.

Leaving the south fire stairwell, one enters a vestibule between the inside and outside of the build-
ing. This space is also required to be fire rated, though it is not an area of rescue. The goal is to take
people from the fire stairs through a space that is not smoke filled. The door from a fire stairwell

213 | Part 4.2. Mechanical Systems and Codes: Demand
must not intrude into the path of those people from the first floor fleeing a fire. This vestibule and
corridor are sized to accommodate the number of people using the egress path as well as those
coming from upstairs and downstairs.
This walk now moves to the exterior of the building and the west parking lot. From this vantage
point one can see the glass curtain wall that encloses TBH's west side. Above the third floor one
can see a steel tube feeding the office spaces below. The roof pitches slightly from north to south
as the duct gets smaller and smaller. Since the duct is feeding fewer and fewer offices as it runs
south, its size is diminished. This can be seen in figure 4.2.4. The architect chose to call attention
to this by having the corresponding roof above slope to follow the diminishing duct and expose
the ductwork to view from the outside. The glass allows the observant visitor to understand how
the mechanical systems are playing a role in the visual and aesthetic aspects of the building.

HVAC systems are a large portion of a building's initial budget. When one considers the money
spent on controlling the heating and cooling of a structure, the costs increase dramatically over
the life cycle cost of the building. In the mid-1970s the Centre Pompidou in Paris was designed by
the architecture team of Richard Rogers and Renzo Piano (see figure 4.2.7).
The structure, dedicated in 1977, houses the largest collection of modern art in Europe. Built as a

     About the Walk

     All buildings need to maintain a "resolution of forces" to stand. Architects and structural engineers
     are concerned with creating stability in structures. Typically, this is done with four basic structural
     types. Edifices must resist stress and strain. Forces can be simplified into two basic categories: gen-
     eral and lateral. General forces typically deal with resisting gravity while lateral forces act sideways
     on buildings. Lateral forces are typically due to wind and earth (quake) movement. The four most
     prevalent issues structural members need to be designed for are bending, shear, torsion, and buck-
     ling.
     This walk begins at Huff Hall located at 1206 South Fourth Street in Champaign. The walk also visits
     the Armory at 505 East Armory Avenue, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility at 301 East Peabody Drive,
     and the State Farm Center at 1800 South First Street in Champaign.

Before 1850, most structures were masonry bearing wall buildings, which means the walls, made
of brick and stone, held up the building. With advances in cast iron, reinforced concrete, and steel,
the vast majority of structures built since the Industrial Revolution have been frame buildings. In a
frame structure usually a grid of columns and beams hold up the building. The walls, including the
exterior enclosing walls, do not bear the weight of the structure. There are four basic structural
types: post and beam, arched, tensile, and a combination (see figure 4.3.1).

A post-and-lintel construction is also called a trabeated systems building. The ancient Greeks
employed this technique in the Parthenon's exterior colonnades. An arched structure, typically a
bearing wall building, relies on the arch to distribute the weight of the building to the ground.
Examples of this structure date as far back as the ancient Roman Coliseum and Pantheon. Tensile
structures rely on cables or skins to transfer weight, such as a tensile rope bridge across a chasm.
More often, tensile members are used in composite structures such as the Brooklyn Bridge or a
tent structure. The bridge structure relies on cables in tension stretching to large vertical towers

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 218
that act as compression members delivering load to the ground, while the tent structure relies on
cables and a taut skin over a set of posts that are in compression (see figure 4.3.2).

                             Part 4.3. Orientation Map.

The many kinds of forces applied to buildings are usually thought of in two categories: gravity
loads and lateral loads (including wind loads and potential earthquakes).
Buildings withstand many types of forces, including shear, bending, and buckling. It is easy to
imagine these forces at work on a pencil. Shear occurs when two forces are applied adjacent to

219 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
one another but do not align. Children often play with pencils, hanging a pencil over the edge of a
table and then giving a swift karate chop to the pencil near where it hangs. The pencil breaks in a
clean shear. For this reason, a pair of scissors, which applies forces adjacent to one another, is also
called a shear. Bending occurs over the length of a member as force is applied either continuously
or in specific locations along the member's length. Using a new pencil, one may apply opposite
forces in the middle: the pencil bends, and if enough force is applied it will break. These two forces
are most commonly applied to beams--horizontal members that hold up floors and roofs. Buckling
occurs when an axial force is applied at either end of a member. If one takes a pencil and presses
in on either end it will begin to deflect--to buckle. Buckling is typically a concern for columns--the
vertical members that carry load downward to the ground (see figure 4.3.3).

          Figure 4.3.1. Four Basic Structural Types. A. Trabeated--post and lintel. B. Arcuated--arch. C.
          Tensile--cable or rope. D. Combination. Read long description of figure.

To understand bending one may apply forces to a wooden ruler. First, try bending the ruler from
the bottom of its narrow side. It is flexible and bends as force is applied to the flat side. An attempt
to bend the ruler along its longer side--by holding it vertically and pressing on it--however is far
more difficult. Though the ruler has the same amount of material, its form is able to resist the load
applied because of its orientation to the load. When force is applied to the ruler, the top half of

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 220
the ruler goes into compression (it wants to squeeze), while the bottom half of the ruler goes into
tension (it wants to stretch). The ruler resists the force based on the distance the material is from
its middle, or centroid (see figure 4.3.4).

             Figure 4.3.2. Examples of four basic structural types. A. Parthenon. B. Colosseum. C. Rope
             bridge. D. Brooklyn Bridge. The Parthenon is a trabeated system. The Colosseum uses a
             system of arches. The rope bridge is a tension system. The Brooklyn Bridge is a composite
             system using tensile cables and compression towers. Read long description of figure.

Thus a steel beam is designed to place a substantial amount of material at a distance from its cen-
ter (centroid) to strategically use the most economical amount of material to resist the greatest
load. In a wide flange beam, the outer material, the flanges, best resist bending, while the connect-
ing member, the web, resists shearing (see figure 4.3.5).

221 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
            Figure 4.3.3. Forces acting on a member and its deformation. Read long description of figure.

When spanning a significant distance, it is often desirable to use a member lighter and usually less
expensive than a single solid material. This member is called a truss. Trusses use the same prin-
ciples to resist bending as a wide flange beam: placing material as economically as possible in the
position to do the most work. A truss may vary in form dramatically, but its essential parts are con-
sistent: a top chord, a bottom chord, and a web that connects these two chords. Typically, the top
chord is designed to experience compressive forces and the bottom chord is designed to resist
tensile forces. Depending on the circumstances, materials, and function, a wide variety of trusses
are used (see figure 4.3.6).

            Figure 4.3.4. Diagram of compression and tension in a bending member.

Placing material at a distance from the centroid where the stresses are greatest allows the least
amount of material to do the greatest amount of work. Since a material such as steel is paid for by

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 222
weight, it makes sense that using a truss renders an economic advantage that exceeds the addi-
tional cost of fabrication.

       Figure 4.3.5. Cross section of a wide flange steel beam. Read long description of figure.

In most long-span structures, the center of the span is where the stress is the greatest. That is
why most trusses attempt to have the greatest distance between the top chord and the bottom
chord in the middle of the truss. Some trusses take on their form for additional reasons, such as
ease of construction or to create a form that sheds water efficiently, but the primary reason to use
a truss is to lighten the amount of material needed to span a distance efficiently. It is important to
note that trusses, however, do not address shear so designers are careful not to introduce shear
forces when using trusses.
Finally, different building materials have different properties. For example, steel is good in both
tension and compression. Therefore steel is often used in trusses. Some truss configurations are
more effective when made of wood than other materials. Stone and concrete are both very good in
compression but not in tension, thus, concrete and stone are often used when compressive forces
are the greatest concern. Concrete today is often reinforced with steel to specifically allow the
steel embedded within the concrete to resist the tensile forces of the structure while the concrete
resists compression.
In general, structure is charged with holding up an enclosure: the roof and walls. The best way
to understand a building's structure is to think of the building as having a system that allows the
sum of all forces to equal zero (when forces do not equal zero, movement occurs and movement
of buildings is rarely a good thing). This walk shall visit four long-span structures. For this walk we

223 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
will be considering gravity loads and the manner in which structures are designed to economically
span long distances for human use.

                              Figure 4.3.6. Examples of some types of trusses.

The primary role of a long-span structure is to enclose a large area for an intended use. Such
spaces often need to be rather high such as to allow for athletic play. For example, volleyball needs
a high space so it can be played with little or no interference. Some spaces must also provide seat-
ing for crowds to watch. Tall spaces also assist in ventilation when large crowds are present.
The structures to be visited are Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the
State Farm Center. In each one large interior spaces are created to allow unencumbered move-
ment, practice, and often spectators to view events.
The first building on this walk is Huff Hall at 1206 South Fourth Street near the intersection of
Gregory Street in Champaign. Designed by James White and Charles Platt, Huff Gymnasium, as
it was originally named, was built in 1925 to allow a large number of spectators to watch athletic
contests and to house classrooms and offices. Like all of Platt's structures on campus, the building
has a neo-Georgian exterior since the architect attempted to unify the campus design through
both style and material. Until 2002, Huff Hall's north side was left unfinished. At that time the Khan

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 224
  Pavilion was added in a postmodern style to balance the overall massing about the building's cen-

          1

  ter.

             Figure 4.3.7. Huff Hall truss diagram. The top chord of the truss accepts the roof load. The top
             and bottom chords along with the diagonal bracing and vertical members of the truss carry the
             load through the truss to the columns. Read long description of figure.

  Huff Hall's main space holds 4050 seats. The long-span structure must transfer the weight of the
  roof down to the ground through a series of columns. To accomplish this, the roof is composed of
  a thin metal sheet over wood sheathing that rests on a series of small members that transfer their
  load to the long-span trusses (see figures 4.3.7-4.3.9).
  These trusses deliver the load directly to columns or indirectly through girders that connect to the
  columns. The entire system is in equilibrium. The main space uses a system of long-span trusses,
  girders, and columns to create an internal court space with decent sight lines for all but those
  people unfortunate to be seated behind columns. Huff Hall has four freestanding columns on each
  side and nine trusses spanning east-west. The north and south walls also carry load. Every other
  truss rests on a column, while in-between trusses rest on a girder that transfers its weight to the
  column and/or outer walls.

1. The building was designed by Ratio Architects, which is also credited with the design of the Indoor Football Practice
  Facility.
  225 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
           Figure 4.3.8. Huff Hall structural members. Trusses either distribute their load directly to the
           columns or to a girder that carries the load over to an adjacent column. Using the girder
           alleviates the need for some columns, which improves the sight lines for spectators. Read long
           description of figure.

The best way to understand the entire system is to realize that the sum of all forces needs to equal
zero. To accomplish this, the roof is composed of a metal sheet over a wood sheet that rests on
a series of small members called purlins that transfer their load to the long-span trusses. These
trusses deliver the load directly to columns or indirectly through the girders that run perpendic-
ular to the trusses and connect to the columns. After the load has reached the column it is trans-
ferred down to the ground, which pushes back with an equal and opposite force. The manner in
which weight is distributed to the ground is called the load path. The equilibrium of the forces
creates a static building (see figure 4.3.10).
Just to the north of Huff Hall lies the Armory at 505 East Armory Avenue, built over a decade before
Huff Hall. When completed in 1914, the Armory was the largest structure in the world without a

                                     2

  center support. Designed by Illinois state architect William C. Zimmerman, it was updated in 1927
  when Platt and White added side masses wrapped in a neo-Georgian facade.

           Figure 4.3.9. Huff Hall load path diagram. A load is carried from the purlin to the beam to a truss
           and onto a girder. Half this truss load transfers to the column to the north and the other half goes to
           the south. Read long description of figure.

  At its peak the Armory is 98 feet high and encloses an area of 80,000 square feet. It uses a system

  the trusses touch. This pin is highlighted by a long, thin skylight at the apex of the structure (see

  The 1927 addition, which was part of the original design, then hangs from the trusses on each side.

  Moving south along Fourth Street, past the outdoor practice facilities adjacent to Memorial Sta-
  dium, is the State Farm Center. Located along Florida Avenue between First and Fourth Streets,
  the State Farm Center, formerly Assembly Hall, was completed in 1963 and until the opening of the
  Astrodome in 1965 was the largest domed structure in North America. Designed by Harrison and

  Abramovitz, Assembly Hall was a structural engineering first.

                        Figure 4.3.19. Detail of the mega truss.

3. Max Abramovitz (1908-2004) was a 1929 graduate of the Illinois School of Architecture. He also designed Krannert Cen­
   ter for the Performing Arts (see part 5.2).
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 234
Thus far, the long-span structures examined on this walk have relied on trusses to span the inte-
rior spaces needing enclosure. The State Farm Center uses a different approach in which a thin
shell of concrete is folded to provide a rigid, low-profile dome. The forces of the dome are inclined
to spread, that is, to push outward toward the perimeter. To resist these spreading forces a cable
was strung at the bottom of the dome. The cable was wound around the perimeter in a taught ring
that was then encased in concrete. This innovative approach put a modern twist on how the ten-
sile forces of a dome, wanting to push outward, could be controlled (see figure 4.3.22). Similarly,
in the Renaissance, Michelangelo and Domenico Fontana employed a chain to resist the spread of
the dome of St. Peter's Basilica.

Figure 4.3.20. Detail of the Indoor Football Facility's pin connections.

                         Figure 4.3.21. Detail of the Irwin Indoor Practice facility's wall.

235 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
To understand why a folded plate was used for this building one may use a piece of paper sup-
ported only at one end. If one tries holding the paper out flat, it sags. Cupping the paper somewhat
gives it some resistance to gravity and its own weight, but if one folds the paper into an accordion
shape the paper is made sufficiently rigid to withstand deflection (see figure 4.3.23).

Figure 4.3.22. Schematic section diagram of the State Farm Center. The roof appears to thicken because of the
section cut and the curvature of the roof and its folds. The tension ring sits on the supporting pylons. The top of
the roof is 128 feet above the floor. The floor is recessed 24 feet below grade. The dome is over 400 feet wide.

This fan shape demonstrates the principle of the rigidity of a folded plate. The next manipulation
is to hold one end tight and spread the fan folds 360 degrees to form a complete ring. The final
step is to bend the fan-like structure into a shallow dome. The dome further resists any downward
force applied to the top. Fortunately, concrete is an ideal material for this manipulation because
it is relatively malleable while under construction and follows the shape of a formwork. Repeat-
ing this formwork creates a completed dome. The forces of the shallow dome are held in check
by a wound suspended cable at the building's perimeter. This cable is embedded in concrete and
forms a tension ring that keeps the dome from spreading outward. A series of equidistantly placed
pylons are used to hold up the tension ring. These pylons are shaped to suggest that they pull the
perimeter of the dome back to the center of the structure to resist the dome's tendency to spread.

Figure 4.3.23 State Farm Center paper-hand diagrams.

                                                      Kevin J. Hinders | 236
Figure 4.3.24. Image of the Assembly Hall (now State Farm Center) from the west.

       Figure 4.3.25. Interior image of the Assembly Hall roof showing the folded plates. These plates are
       covered in a sound absorbing material called tectum.

237 | Part 4.3. Structures: Huff Hall, the Armory, the Irwin Indoor Football Facility, and the State Farm Center
  These large concrete pylons slope from the roof inward toward the center of the building. This
  visually counters the tremendous thrust of the dome and gives the State Farm Center the flying
  saucer profile so beloved by Illini fans. The folded roof seen from the exterior is echoed inside (see
  figures 4.24 and 4.3.25).
  These are only some of the many long-span structures on campus. There are numerous ingenious
  structural responses to unique circumstances. Often these creative solutions are not exposed to

                                                                                                                                                             4

  the inhabitant but may sometimes be deduced through careful analysis.

PART 5. INDIVIDUAL BUILDINGS AND
THEIR DESIGN

       Overview

       This part will explore structures on the Urbana-Champaign campus as they reflect specific
       approaches to design. To understand these approaches, a brief outline of the historical context that
       has led to today's designs is provided. Here shaping generalizations are used to assist in providing
       the context for the education of an architect and the changes in designers' approaches over time.

  Design has many influences. Historians point to each epoch and attempt to tie together the most
  influential aspects to explain what, why, and how things occurred. To gain a better understanding
  of our current built environment it is useful to clarify how past events and trends influenced the
  design of their time. For example, spatial preferences have changed as humans' understanding of
  the universe has changed (see part 3.5 for one example). Before looking at individual structures on
  the Urbana-Champaign campus, several shaping generalizations regarding patronage and educa-
  tion are necessary to understand.

  A first generalization is about the changes in patronage. The patron is the person or persons sup-
  plying the economic resources for the construction of the edifice. For several millennia the major
  patrons of architecture and urban planning were the nobility and the religious. Think of the pyra-
  mids of Egypt or the medieval cathedrals of Europe to understand the importance of sponsor-
  ship. The pharaohs' absolute control reverberates through time while the religious desires of those
  aspiring to build heavenly mansions for religious devotion can be seen across Europe. As our soci-
  ety has changed, so has our patronage. For example, In The Architecture of the Eighteenth Century,
  John Summerson writes: "The characteristic building types of the first half of the century were

                                                                                                                                                                                                         1

  churches and palaces, while those of the second half were public and institutional buildings." This
  statement underscores a shift that reflects the great changes brought by the Enlightenment and
  subsequently the Industrial Revolution. Summerson continues:

         The creation of types is a social matter. It is also a matter of cultural climate. The 18th Cen-

1. Summerson, Architecture, 7.
  239 | Part 5. Individual Buildings and Their Design
          tury was the century of the "Enlightenment", a familiar expression which is by no means
          self-explanatory and which, if we attempt to analyse it, becomes infinitely complicated.
          We may take refuge in the great names--Newton and Locke, Voltaire, Montesquieu, Hume,
          Rousseau, Diderot--but if we are considering architecture we shall find no obvious way of
          connecting their thought with what was going on in the building world. "Enlightenment"
          was not an all-pervading flood-light. It was a question of the formation of new attitudes by
          the acceptance of ideas filtering down from the philosophers to levels where they engaged
          with the practical business of living. . . . In other words, society could be visualized in an
          enlightened perspective in which technology, the arts, the law and its penalties, and the

                                                                                                                                                                                    2

          arts of administration began to be freshly seen, with a new humanitarianism.

  Summerson goes on to discuss new building types of the eighteenth century: theaters, libraries,
  museums, hospitals, prisons, and commercial buildings.

  Design changed as new societal structures were created to replace absolutism and feudal-
  ism--such as capitalism, socialism, fascism, and communism and governments that support them.
  New patrons required new building types. For example, the Industrial Revolution required never-
  before-imagined structures like railway stations and factories. Meanwhile new materials such as
  plate glass and carbon steel transformed the built environment by allowing longer spans, taller
  buildings, and different construction methods. Concurrently, rising populations led to new hous-
  ing needs filled by both private and governmental patrons.

  At the University of Illinois Urbana-Champaign one sees a different type of patronage. This land-
  grant institution, charged with education in agriculture and the mechanic arts and especially in
  the practical application of this knowledge, has changed since the university's inception. The mis-
  sion statement (approved by the Board of Trustees on January 31, 2019) reads: "The University of
  Illinois at Urbana-Champaign is charged by our state to enhance the lives of citizens in Illinois,
  across the nation and around the world through our leadership in learning, discovery, engage-

                                                                               3

  ment and economic development." Patronage of the university's build environment is left to the
  Board of Trustees, which is charged with, among other responsibilities, providing the structures

                                                     4

  needed for instruction. The university is fortunate to have both governmental partners and pri-
  vate partners. Governmental partners include granting agencies such as the National Endowment

2. Ibid., 105-6.
3. University of Illinois Urbana-Champaign, "About."
4. "The trustees shall have power to provide for the requisite buildings, apparatus, and conveniences; to fix the rates

   for tuition; to appoint such professors and instructors, and to establish and provide for the management of such
   model farms, model art, and other departments and professorships, as may be required to teach, in the most thorough
   manner, such branches of learning as are related to agriculture and the mechanic arts, and military tactics, without
   excluding other scientific and classical studies" (University of Illinois Act, 110 ILCS 305/7, chap. 144, par. 28, sec. 7,
   https://www.ilga.gov/Legislation/ILCS/Articles?ActID=1086&ChapterID=18).

                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 240
  for the Humanities and the National Science Foundation. Private partners--individuals and foun-
  dations--share their funds for the advancement of the university's goals. These partners may pro-
  vide resources to create or renovate structures for the good of the university. As a result, they
  support the Board of Trustees and indirectly become a part of the patronage to support the users
  of the university's facilities, students, faculty, and visitors.

  Parallel to this shift in political and economic power one can trace the changes in the manner
  in which the architect/designer was trained. The first master builders, such as those from the
  medieval period, trained first as apprentices. These individuals may have worked their entire lives
  on a single cathedral or may have, after a lengthy period of apprenticing, had an opportunity to
  inherit an existing project or become the master on a project in another location. These masters
  were commissioned by the nobility and religious to create their great works. During the Renais-
  sance patrons sought out artisans with the intention of creating art that would memorialize
  them. Thus, Pope Julius II commissioned the painting of the Sistine Chapel from Michelangelo
  and employed him throughout his papacy. Similarly, Pope Urban VIII financially supported Gian
  Lorenzo Bernini throughout his papacy. Michelangelo and Bernini, like their medieval predeces-
  sors, employed many apprentices and workers.

  With a shift in economic power, a change in worldview, and an expanding population brought on
  by the Enlightenment and the Industrial Revolution came a new means of educating architects
  and designers. Two schools dominated: the Beaux-Arts (originally the Académie des Beaux-Arts,
  founded in 1648, and changed in 1863 to L'École des Beaux-Arts) and the Polytechnique (École

                                     5

  Polytechnique). The former emphasized the arts while the latter emphasized engineering. These
  schools, based on meritocracy, educated students with faculty who each taught a different subject.
  Once a student demonstrated mastery of the material to the satisfaction of the faculty they were
  permitted to practice.

                                                                                                                                                                                                         6

  After World War I, the Bauhaus (1919-33) was formed as an avant-garde school in Germany. This
  school was a product of and a producer of modernism. Dissatisfied with looking to the past, its
  founder Walter Gropius sought to create a school for the arts based on design principles of com-
  position, materiality, color, and mass production. After an initial core course, masters of craft
  (blacksmiths, bricklayers, carpenters, etc.) and masters of form (renowned artists from many dis-
  ciplines) were brought in to teach studio courses. By the time the Bauhaus closed in 1933 because
  of political pressure from the Nazi Party, numerous instructors had relocated to the United States

                                                                                                                                7

  and brought aspects of the Bauhaus education with them.

5. See Drexler, Architecture of the École des Beaux-Arts; For a remarkable example of work on typology from the Polytech­
   nique see Durand, Recueil et parallèle des édifices.

6. See Whitford, Bauhaus.
7. See Kentgens-Craig, Bauhaus and America.

In many universities in the United States, Beaux-Arts and Bauhaus courses were adapted to fit a
system in which architecture faculty from a department, school, or college determine a standard
set of courses required to prepare students for the profession. After passing this prescribed set of
required and elective courses a student would receive a professional degree--now a requirement
to practice architecture in the United States. Typically, the student must then serve an intern-
ship period under a licensed professional and pass a licensure exam before becoming a registered

                    8

architect.

Thus, what began as a time-consuming system of a single master with many apprentices (only one

or two of which would themselves become a master) has become one that produces numerous

architects in a relatively short period of time. As population growth and building complexity has

increased, teaching methods have changed and the number of architects has risen to keep pace

                                       9

(see tables 5.1-5.6).

There is no singular path or approach to design. Today, some designers are associated with spe-
cific styles while many eschew such categorizations. Some designers choose to push the envelope
with new methods of construction, forms, etc. while others prefer to explore traditional forms and
their construction.

This part began with John Summerson, who went on to say: "Style is what immediately takes the

243 | Part 5. Individual Buildings and Their Design
                                                                                                               10

eye, excites curiosity and creates a receptive mood." Styles reflect thoughts about aesthetics and
construction. The values that patrons and designers bring to their projects dramatically affect the
resultant designs.

The next series of walks will address different approaches to design and will attempt to illustrate
principles that influence how individual buildings respond to their context differently. As has been
discussed previously, some buildings become figural and stand out from their surroundings while
others form the backdrop for spaces. This set of walks attempts to focus on specific aspects that
influence the form and design of the built environment. The campus structures selected to tour
provide examples of traditional building components (the orders), functional expression (Krannert
Center for the Performing Arts), meaning and metaphor (Smith Memorial Hall, the Literatures,
Cultures, and Linguistics Building, and the Carle R. Woese Institute for Genomic Biology), synthe-
sis of ideas (the Education Building), and sustainability (the Business Instructional Facility).

10. Summerson, Architecture, 7.

                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 244
Part 5.1. The Orders

       About the Walk

       The University of Illinois, by virtue of when it was constructed and its continuous planning, has its
       feet firmly planted in the past with its eyes on the future. As a result its built environment has many
       different stylistic aspects. One of the strongest influences on traditional architecture is the inclusion
       of the orders. The orders have been used by architects beginning with the ancient Greeks and
       Romans to create structural and aesthetic harmony. The five classical orders are the Doric, Ionic,
       Corinthian, Tuscan, and Composite. These orders and many derivatives can be found all over cam-
       pus and its surroundings.
       There is no formal walk for this part. Rather it explains the role of the orders in the creation of clas-
       sical architecture. The reader is instead asked to go on an architectural treasure hunt to find exam-
       ples of each order. Photographic examples are provided to assist in identification.

  Five orders are used in classical architecture. Tradition recognizes three as Greek: the Doric, the
  Ionic, and the Corinthian. The Romans adopted these three and created two others: the Tuscan
  and the Composite. There are two principle parts of an order: the column, which is the vertical
  supporting member, and the entablature, which is the horizontal spanning member. The column
  has three parts: the base, the shaft, and the capital. The entablature also has three parts: the archi-
  trave, the frieze, and the cornice (see figure 5.1.1).
  The classically established architectural column and entablature have long formed an important
  building component that sets the nature of the design. The orders help establish proportion sys-
  tems used by the designer.

         ORDER, in architecture, is a system of the several members, ornaments, and proportions
         of columns and pilasters; or a regular arrangement of the projecting parts of a building,

                                                                                                                                        1

         especially the column, so as to form one beautiful whole.
  The orders are described by Vitruvius in De Architectura, and Renaissance architects such as

1. Encyclopaedia Britannica, 1771, 442.
  245 | Part 5.1. The Orders
Sebastiano Serlio, Andrea Palladio, and Giacomo da Vignola all published their versions of the
orders. In fact, architects--ever interested in invention--have been tweaking the orders for cen-
turies. The proportions of the orders vary from structure to structure and age to age. A Greek
temple using the Doric order is easily distinguished from an Ionic temple, however, there is a great
variety among each type of temple. Over time the proportions of the orders and the manner in
which they are deployed have changed. For example, most Greek Doric columns such as those
used in the Parthenon in Athens did not have bases. The columns rested, rather, on a step (called
the stylobate) that unified the columns at the bottom. The Romans, however, translated the Doric
order and added a base as seen in the first floor of the Colosseum in Rome (see figure 5.1.2).

During the Renaissance architects often set the column on a pedestal. Again, one might look at
figure 5.1.2, which has a Doric order at the base, surmounted by an Ionic order at the second floor
and a Corinthian order at the third level. The second- and third-floor columns have pedestals,
while the first-floor Doric columns have bases but no pedestals. This use of the Doric order at
the base with more slender columns above has been used since antiquity. It reflects the structural
notion of a strong base becoming lighter as the wall ascends.

Given the fluctuation from age to age, it is surprising that a general consensus on the orders exists
at all. In the mid-sixteenth century the architect Vignola published a version that has been relied
on by many architects, especially American architects practicing in the early twentieth century
(see figure 5.1.3).

Figure 5.1.3 shows the proportions of the five orders according to Vignola. In this drawing the rela-
tionship between the height of the column and its entablature is four to one. Similarly, the rela-
tionship between the column's pedestal and the column (when a pedestal is used) is one to three.
The Tuscan and Doric orders, typically associated with the proportions of males, is one column
diameter to seven high in the Tuscan order and one column diameter to eight high in the Doric
order. The Ionic order, associated with female proportions, is one column diameter to nine high.
The Corinthian and Composite orders are all shown with a one to ten ratio.

The Tuscan order is similar to the Greek's Doric order and is the simplest and plainest order. Dat-
ing to pre-Roman times, it was standardized during the Renaissance and was included in pub-
lications by Renaissance architects such as Serlio and Palladio. The Doric order is often fluted,
meaning the column has concave undulations on its shaft, while the Tuscan order is not fluted. In
figure 5.1.3 all the columns are shown fluted except the Tuscan.

Each order has distinct characteristics. Observing the column capital is the easiest way to distin-
guish the order.

The Corinthian Order

The Corinthian column has leaves carved (or cast) to resemble those of the acanthus plant (see
figure 5.1.20). This order was sometimes transformed and stylized to reflect plants of a particular
region such as corn or tobacco (Benjamin Latrobe designed a tobacco capital for Thomas Jeffer-
son's Monticello).

The Alpha Tau Omega and Alpha Gamma Rho fraternity houses at the university have stylized ver-
sions of the Corinthian capital with palm leaves springing from the traditional acanthus leaves
that date back to an early Greek Corinthian capital called the Tower of Winds Capital (see figures
5.1.21-5.1.25).

The Composite Order

The Composite order combines the Ionic and the Corinthian. This order has both Ionic volutes and
the Corinthian acanthus leaves (see figures 5.1.26-5.1.29). This order is rare in central Illinois. The
Architecture Building at 608 East Lorado Taft Drive has a stylized version of the Composite order.

It is from these basic building blocks that classical architecture is derived. The orders often help
establish a larger set of proportional systems. The orders are associated with many styles includ-
ing Greek, Roman, Renaissance, Baroque, neoclassical, and subsequent revivals. Many traditional
buildings on campus, including the Georgian buildings by Platt, use the typical orders or have styl-
ized variations. The examples provided are only a fraction of the buildings that feature the orders
and yet the variety of architecture they help create is quite extensive.

261 | Part 5.1. The Orders
Part 5.2. Function and Design: Krannert
Center for the Performing Arts

        About the Walk

        To illustrate the principle that the form of an object can be related to its function, a tour of both the
        public and production sections of Krannert Center for the Performing Arts is taken. The four pri-
        mary interior theaters and one exterior theater are visited to understand the manner in which a
        performance both comes about and is viewed. Backstage sites include the scene shop, the costume
        shop, and practice rooms and how all these relate to each theater. Theater visits include discussion
        of sight lines, acoustics, and crowd control.
        This walk begins in the lobby of Krannert Center for the Performing Arts located at 500 South
        Goodwin Avenue in Urbana.

          Form ever follows function.

                                                1

          --Louis Sullivan
  Historically, two primary forms of music halls have been built in the last several centuries. One,
  like Carnegie Hall in New York, absorbs the stage and audience space into a larger mass. A grand
  entrance facade introduces the audience to a lobby. From the lobby one takes a procession to a
  seat. The Palais Garnier (Opera National de Paris) is a model for this type of performance hall. The
  second, like Finlandia Hall in Helsinki, expresses a performance hall as a distinct mass. Typically,
  the lobby and support spaces are kept low and horizontal in contrast to the sculptural perfor-
  mance spaces and audience seating. Perhaps the most famous of the second form were designed
  by the Finnish architect Alvar Aalto. Krannert Center for the Performing Arts (KCPA) is often com-
  pared with Aalto's work. When Finnish architect Juhani Pallasmaa was the Plym Visiting Professor

                                                                                                                                                                                           2

  for the Illinois School of Architecture he visited KCPA and said: "I feel like I am home."

2. Comment made to the author.

  Part 5.2. Orientation Map.

  Conceived in 1962 and opened in 1969, KCPA was the result of a $16 million contribution by Herman

KCPA is a world-class venue for the performing arts. Foellinger Great Hall has long been recog-

its well-designed space. There are five public stages in the venue: Foellinger Great Hall, the Tryon
Festival Theatre, the Colwell Playhouse, the Studio Theatre, and Stage 5 (Stage 5 is located in the
lobby and has no designated boundary aside from a raised platform). There is also the outdoor
Amphitheatre (see figure 5.2.1).

Figure 5.2.2. Foellinger Great Hall foyer.

A large lobby connects and services all the interior audience spaces. This lobby allows people going
to different performances to mingle. The lobby has a parquet teak floor throughout and a cor-
rugated metal ceiling that houses lighting and mechanical diffusers. Guests may also visit Inter-
mezzo café. A large counter that functions as the main ticket office is located in the lobby on an
axis with Foellinger Great Hall. Each of the three larger performance spaces--Foellinger Great Hall,

265 | Part 5.2. Function and Design: Krannert Center for the Performing Arts
the Tryon Festival Theatre, and the Colwell Playhouse--has its own secondary lobby located one
floor below the main lobby. These lobbies serve to enhance the atmosphere while distributing the
audience to the seats. Visitors enjoy a well-choreographed experience once they arrive in the main
lobby.

Figure 5.2.3. Foellinger Great Hall foyer.

Inside KCPA is a parallel to the concept of figure-ground that shall be called server-served. Just
like a theater has performers and audience, the architecture here is divided into public or front-
of-house spaces where visitors mingle and watch the performances--these are the served areas.
The production or back-of-house spaces serve the performance and the performers--these are
the server areas. While one is in the lobby the theaters are a kind of poché, as they give form to
the lobby. Meanwhile their figural quality--size, shape, and color--enhance the audience's expec-
tation for what they shall experience inside. The enclosing walls of the theaters act almost like a
first curtain before the commencement of the performance (see figure 5.2.1).
The architecture is used to heighten expectations and deepen the experiences of those attending
events even before the performances have begun. The secondary foyers for Foellinger Great Hall,

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 266
  the Tryon Festival Theatre, and the Colwell Playhouse further control entrance to the performance
  halls (see figures 5.2.2-5.2.5).

  Figure 5.2.4. Tryon Festival Theatre foyer.

  Once the audience has entered a theater, the figure-ground flips. The audience has left the rest of
  the world behind to be transported by both space and spectacle (see figure 5.2.6).

  Theatrical architect S. Charles Lee's unofficial motto was "The Show Starts on the Sidewalk." At
  KCPA it often begins in the parking garage. Though there are two primary pedestrian entrances
  off Goodwin Avenue and a number of secondary means to enter the building, a great many visitors
  arrive by automobile. KCPA has two levels of parking to the northeast and southeast of the the-

  aters that lead to oversized elevators linked to the main lobby.

to the Studio Theatre. This smallest of the venues is called a black box theater because it is
unadorned, painted dark, and designed for experimental works. It has 200 seats that can be
arranged in a variety of configurations such as a theater in the round (when the audience encircles
the stage) or as a thrust stage (when the stage juts out into the audience on three sides). It has
audio and lighting capabilities similar to those in the larger venues, but the small seating capacity
and experimental quality for students and performers is designed to be less intimidating (see fig-
ures 5.2.7 and 5.2.8).

Figure 5.2.5. Colwell Playhouse foyer.

Returning to the lobby, the next space to visit is the Colwell Playhouse, which was designed for
theater and dance events. Seating 674, it was created to specifically support works with speech:
the walls are shaped to reflect voices from the stage to the audience, but the back walls are echo
proof. This theater uses continental seating, which has aisles only at the perimeter. In typical con-
tinental seating the rows are spaced wider to facilitate people moving to their seats from the sides.
Because of the level changes in the main space, two balconies are provided for wheelchair seating.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 268
  The theater uses a proscenium--the part of a stage in front of the curtain--that can be configured

                                                                                                                                                                                                       9

  to fit the performance. The space is designed to provide excellent sight lines from all seats.

                  Figure 5.2.6. Figure-ground diagram of the four theaters (reversed). The four interior
                  theaters become figural interiors that remove the surrounding context and create an
                  exclusive environment for audiences to appreciate the performances. In the front of house,
                  one experiences the spaces set aside for different types of performance. When visiting the
