Progressing from the audience area to the stage one enters the world of the performers and stage-
hands. Directly above the stage is a large fly, or vertical space used for stage backdrops, that can
be moved to stage level from above. The fly also contains sections for lighting that can be manip-
ulated as required. The stage has access from all sides including the floor.

       Figure 5.2.7. Studio Theater ready for staging.

The Colwell Playhouse, Tryon Festival Theatre, and Foellinger Great Hall are all physically sep-
arated from the rest of the building to reduce vibration from being transmitted to the stages
and audience seating. Originally even the air conditioning systems were physically separated and
placed on the roof of nearby Illinois Avenue Residence Hall (KCPA is now served by a chilled water
line) to reduce the possibility of sound vibration in the served spaces.
Having descended to the stage area the viewer is now on level 2. This is the production level of the
building. After exiting stage left one is in a hall that is both wide and tall. The hall is looped around
the entire second level to link the stages of the Tryon Festival Theatre and the Colwell Playhouse
with the scene shop and the other production departments. The doors of the scene shop and
stages are oversized to allow completed theatrical sets to be moved into position without having
to be broken apart and reassembled. The internal corridors link the scene shop, the costume shop
(with over 40,000 costumes), and the dedicated rehearsal spaces with the performance spaces.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 270
Storerooms house costumes and props that are recycled and refitted for production needs (see
figure 5.2.9).

       Figure 5.2.8. Studio Theatre diagrams showing some of the possible configurations.

Past the costume shop is Foellinger Great Hall and its supporting spaces. Two corridors lead per-
formers to the stage from the rehearsal rooms. Nearby is a space designed to replicate the stage
of the Great Hall yet remain isolated from vibration to the performance space. Additional dance
and choral rehearsal spaces are also present. Near the entrances to each hall are dedicated dress-
ing rooms and makeup spaces. The hallway that connects the stages is made of concrete masonry
units that have been signed by performers (see figure 5.2.10).

271 | Part 5.2. Function and Design: Krannert Center for the Performing Arts
Figure 5.2.9. Plan Krannert Center for the Performing Arts, level 2. Courtesy of the University of
Illinois Archives. Read long description of figure.

                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 272
   Foellinger Great Hall, used for a wide range of musical groups (including symphony orchestras,
   jazz groups, choirs, and opera singers) seats 2,078. It is located on an axis with the Main Quad's
   Daniel Street cross axis mentioned in part 2.2. This hall is perfectly symmetrical. The walls are
   finished with butternut wood (also known as white walnut) from Indiana forests that was book-
   matched (the grains were matched in mirror images) across the hall to ensure the best possible
   acoustics. A false door at the balcony has been introduced to match an active door on the other
   side. Side walls have been kept nonparallel to control reverberation. The folded panel ceiling hangs
   inside the shell of the hall to provide optimum sound reflection. Cushioned seats in the space are
   covered with a heavy cloth calculated to absorb and reflect sound in the same manner as someone

                                                  10

   occupying the seats. Sight lines from a shallowly raked main area allow views to a slightly ele-
   vated stage, while balconies along either side and the back also have uninterrupted sight lines (see
   figure 5.2.11).

                    Figure 5.2.10. Corridor signatures of past performers.

   Continuing along the service corridor loop one reaches the Tryon Festival Theatre backstage area.
   This theater seats 974 on two levels and was designed primarily for operas. The acoustics have
   been optimized for vocal performance. The seating is split between a main floor serviced by four
   aisles and a balcony with continental seating for 264 with some obstructed view seats to the sides.
   The floor of the stage may be manipulated with trapdoors, so performers can emerge from almost

Ascending the aisles, one exits to a lower sublobby that then reaches the main lobby via grand
stairs. Crossing the lobby to the west side one exits onto a plinth overlooking Goodwin Avenue.
This plinth sits above the level 2 service area. The four theaters are sheathed in brick and terra
cotta and emerge out of the plinth on the four cardinal sides of the structure. Foellinger Great Hall
has a large glass curtain wall that illuminates its lobby while shining out toward the campus when
lit at night. The flies of the Tryon Festival Theatre and the Colwell Playhouse express their volume
and the presence of the stages as a counter to the curved enclosures of the audience seating. In
the foreground, between the two Goodwin Street entrances is the outdoor Amphitheatre. It has
a singular acoustic sweet spot from which it is easy to hear the presenter from anywhere in the
audience. From here one may ascend to the lobby roof and view the east side of campus.
KCPA is a striking example of a structure that communicates its use to passersby through clear
volumetric expression. The sculptural manner in which the theater volumes silhouette against the
sky allows KCPA to pull away slightly from the street and become a focal point for the area to the
east of the Main Quad (see figures 5.2.12-5.2.14). The main halls can easily be identified from the
street. This awareness can heighten visitors' anticipation of a performance and initiate memories
of past performances for passersby.

Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for
Meaning: A Study of Three Structures

       About the Walk

       This walk discusses a variety of approaches to the making of architecture. Smith Memorial Hall is an
       example of a traditional building arranged in a sequential manner to accommodate the visitor to a
       music performance. The orchestration of compressive and expansive spaces, spatial sequencing, and
       the crescendo of the performance halls is as integral to an event as the recital itself, while the lack of
       ornamentation and the functional qualities of practice and performing are equally represented. In
       the Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building one finds a structure very different from others
       on the Main Quad in some ways and very similar in others. The possible allegorical tale about the
       building as a response to the story of the Tower of Babel in Genesis 11:1-9 is examined. The Carl R.
       Woese Institute for Genomic Biology, a relatively new addition, illustrates a contemporary approach
       to ornament and stylistic minimalism while attempting to integrate into a complex context involving
       the Morrow Plots.
       This walk begins at Smith Hall located at 805 South Mathews Avenue in Urbana. It also looks at the
       Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building at 707 South Mathews Avenue and the Carl R. Woese
       Institute for Genomic Biology at 1206 West Gregory Drive in Urbana.

  Architecture is the product and creator of its context. Principles and precedents form the archi-
  tecture of the United States. The context that formed the design of the Midwestern United
  States is a product of a changing society and a sense of history derived from the blurring of
  three important nineteenth-century thinkers: Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel--philosopher--Karl

                                                                                                                                                                                        1

  Marx--philosopher, sociologist, and political activist--and Charles Darwin--scientist. Hegel intro-
  duced the notion of history into philosophy and the idea of epochs rising from one to another.
  Marx astutely took the idea of organic change and applied it to sociopolitical subjects. Finally, Dar-
  win added the theory of evolution. When combined, these three independent sets of ideas can be
  seen at the heart of early modernism. No longer content to explore the creation of new architec-
  ture based on precedents of some previous golden age, modernism sought to break from the past

1. For a more in-depth explanation, see Rowe and Koetter, Collage City.
  277 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
  and create architecture with aspirations for the future. It is noteworthy that the US was emerging
  on the world scene in the nineteenth century and became a super power in the twentieth century.
  Similarly, the University of Illinois was formed as a land-grant institution after the Civil War when
  these three concepts were being mentally stirred into a kind of intoxicating cocktail. Rather than
  emulate the past, modernists sought freedom from the shackles of precedent to create a fresh and

                                   2

  vibrant future. The promise of modernism was simple: Through science and design it would be
  possible to create a better future with the belief that we can create a utopian society on Earth.

                   Part 5.3. Orientation Map.

2. The US was an experiment in nonmonarchical government. It is important to note that the general shift away from
   monarchical systems of government and the divine right of kings was part of a fall of legitimacy, simply put the erosion
   of trust in a given institution that challenges the very foundational belief in a system's authority and justice, potentially
   leading to social discontent, unrest, resistance, and, in extreme cases, even systemic collapse parallels the rise of mod­
   ernism.
                                                                                                                                                                 Kevin J. Hinders | 278
  The nineteenth century is often called the Age of Eclecticism. Many different styles including neo-
  classicism, Greek revival, Italianate, Second Empire, Gothic revival, Romanesque, and Renaissance
  revival coexisted: "In fact, the singularity of nineteenth century Revivalism, as compared with ear-
  lier revivals, was that it revived several kinds of architecture at the same time, and none of them
  was ever authoritative or fashionable enough to vanquish its competitors, or even to supersede

                                                                                                                              3

  the kind of architecture which had previously been built."

  And yet there was a sense that the age demanded its own style:

          One of the most curious and far-reaching phenomena of the mid-nineteenth century was
          the insistent and widespread demand for a new architecture, which reached its climax
          about 1853. After that date, the idea became dormant for half a century, mainly because
          it had by then become apparent that every argument had been expended without a new
          architecture seeming any nearer; for in point of fact, it was impossible for a truly new uni-
          versal architecture to establish itself before the invention of new structural systems, and
          this only occurred in the 1890's, with the commercial development of steel and reinforced

                                                   4

          concrete frames.

  The call for an architecture that represented the age was made as early as the 1830s. The idea that
  an age had a spirit, a zeitgeist, had been presented by Hegel and would have a profound effect on
  the twentieth century.

  The early decades of the twentieth century had numerous new styles including art nouveau, art
  deco, and the international style. Architects began exploring the possibilities of innovative mate-
  rials, construction technologies, and programmatic demands. Steel frames and elevators allowed
  for structures to span greater distances and be built higher. Novel building types such as commer-
  cial warehouses and skyscrapers were developed. It seems the style of the cladding was left to the
  architect to determine as one might dress a mannequin. In all this upheaval architects argued that
  new building needs demanded new building forms.

  In 1923 Le Corbusier, a prominent architect, painter, and writer, brought together a series of pam-
  phlets into the collection Vers un Architecture, which was translated into English as Towards a New
  Architecture. For Le Corbusier it was clear: Architecture was to be of its time and in opposition to
  the architecture of the past. Just as others had done before him, he called on architects to emulate
  engineers. He had chapters on planes, ocean liners, and automobiles. Writing shortly after World
  War I he stipulated that the problem of the epoch was housing and he warned an eager audi-

3. Collins, Changing Ideals, 62.
4. Ibid., 128.

   279 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
                                                                                                                                                                        5

  ence of architects: "Architecture or Revolution. Revolution can be avoided." By maintaining that
  the problem of the epoch was housing, Le Corbusier empowered the architect. There was no hid-
  ing his reference to the Communist Revolution in Russia. According to many modernist architects,
  architecture was a cure for the problems of the age--the secular, messianic figure of the architect

                                                                                      6

  would lead society to a promised land.

          Figure 5.3.1. Smith Memorial Hall east entry. Architect: James McLaren White.

  In his work, Le Corbusier replaced the five orders with his own five points of architecture that
  were in direct opposition to the authority of the orders. His five points were piloti--columns that
  structurally bore the weight of the building rather than masonry bearing walls; free plan--in which
  the walls, no longer needed to hold the building up, could be used for spatial enclosure indepen-
  dent of the structure; free facade--where the facade could also be hung from the horizontal floors
  and no longer have any structural responsibility; ribbon windows--since the exterior wall could

5. Le Corbusier, Towards a New Architecture, 267.
6. Hermann Finsterlin, a German expressionist, wrote in 1919: "He (the architect) will build his rampart out of the Will. He

   will conquer the centripetal spirits of the air, stretch and spring over the ether mantle which envelops him like a skin,
   shed layer after layer and climb higher and purer over and above each of these transcended remains...Thousands of
   naked souls, thousands of lesser souls and diminished souls await the goal which should gape in front of them, the king­
   dom of heaven on earth" (quoted in Rowe and Koetter, Collage City, 11). Thus we see the vision of the architect leading
   humankind to utopia.

                                                                                                                                                                Kevin J. Hinders | 280
be hung from the horizontal structural slabs, large horizontal windows could be designed to let in
larger amounts of light and ventilation; and roof gardens--because it was no longer necessary for
roofs to be pitched, the horizontal roof surfaces could be commissioned to serve as garden space.
The purity and simplicity of these five points were incredibly seductive.

5.3.2. Smith Memorial Hall east entry elevation. Courtesy of the University of Illinois Archives. Read long
description of figure.

The architecture of the revivalist period, which will be referred to as traditional architecture here,
had characteristics that can be juxtaposed with those of modern architecture. Traditional archi-
tecture was based on precedents from the past, often stressing symmetry and typically alluding to
bearing wall construction though often using the latest in construction technology, such as struc-
tural steel or concrete frame. The designs incorporated the conveniences and necessities of the
day. Meanwhile, modern architecture was associated with the avant-garde; eschewed precedent;
and utilized principles of composition, utility, and innovative materials. The compositional prefer-
ence was for asymmetry and the visual dispersal of elements to the periphery. This is reflected in
the art of the cubists and the purists.
During the 1930s there was an intense debate over the appropriateness of traditional versus mod-
ern architecture. Magazines such as Western Architecture often had articles espousing one over
the other in the same issue. Even the depiction of buildings in architectural drawings seemed con-
fused. The Beaux-Arts's ink washes, typically associated with traditional architecture, were some-
times employed to delineate modern architecture, and the simple line drawings associated with
modern architecture were employed in the depiction of traditional structures. The debate contin-
ued up until World War II with modern architecture gaining wider acceptance as the years passed.
Several factors helped push the pendulum in favor of modernism in the United States. In 1933 the

281 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
Bauhaus closed under pressure from the Nazi Party. Members of the famous Bauhaus School, a
design institution established by Walter Gropius in Germany, left Europe and immigrated to the
United States. Several faculty members became influential leaders in US schools such as Harvard
(Walter Gropius and Marcel Breuer) and the Illinois Institute of Technology (Ludwig Mies van der
Rohe and László Moholy-Nagy). The Bauhaus teaching methods were also adopted at numerous
US schools. The second factor was World War II itself. The war demanded mass production using
new materials, new machines, and new delivery systems. Ornamentation was considered an unim-
portant affectation in the industrial buildings of this era. These industrial buildings and their prod-
ucts proved vital to the victory over the Axis powers. As a result, modern architecture was no
longer relegated to factories and utilitarian structures. It became celebrated in public buildings
such as embassies and post offices. The advancements in designing and building airplanes and
battleships, with their machine aesthetic derived from functional necessities, fueled a postwar era
that sought the new and improved. Modernism celebrated streamlining.

       Figure 5.3.3. Plan of Smith Hall parti. Read long description of figure.

The international style--one type of modernism--had been dominating architecture for several

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 282
  generations until, beginning in the mid-1960s, there began a questioning of whether modern
  architecture and its partner, urban planning, were delivering on their utopian aspirations. By
  1966 two events signaled the significance of this questioning. In 1966 the US Congress passed
  the National Historic Preservation Act (Senate Bill 3035), which signaled a shift in the value placed
  on not just new architecture but the architecture of the past. The law was the most far-reaching
  preservation legislation ever created in the US. That same year Robert Venturi published Complex-
  ity and Contradiction in Architecture. In his text, Venturi questions the modernist value of mini-
  malism and suggests revaluing traditional architecture and nonmodern precedents. Books on city

                                                                                                                     7

  planning and urban design echoed these sentiments.

          Figure 5.3.4. Smith Hall section diagram. Read long description of figure.

  In the 1970s people became curious about both personal and national history. The US bicentennial
  sparked an interest across the country in events surrounding the Declaration of Independence and
  formation of the United States. "Bicentennial Minutes" describing historic events 200 years earlier
  were televised each evening. The book Roots: The Saga of an American Family (1976) was made into
  a miniseries (1977). It brought the history of one African American family into living rooms across
  the country and set in motion a coast-to-coast fascination with genealogy. The pendulum of inter-
  est swung back to exploration of the past as designers looked to historic works for ideas. This

                                                                                               8

  period became known as postmodernism. In theory, we are still living in a postmodern world as

7. Aldo Rossi's Architecture of the City, published in 1966, also sought to debunk the minimalist notions of postwar mod­
   ernism. Colin Rowe and Fred Koetter's Collage City explains the rise and fall of modern architecture, urban planning, and
   urban design.

8. At the time there were debates over the spelling of the term and its meaning. "Postmodern" is used here but "post mod­
   ern" and "post-modern" were also used. Each version stressed an aspect of the period. Today "postmodern" is typically
   used to describe a style.
   283 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
no new philosophy has replaced this questioning of modernism. Stylistically, however, the pendu-
lum shifted back toward modernism in the 1990s. Architects explored parallels in literary criticism,
decomposition, and deconstruction as means to produce design and as a style. Many attribute this
shift to the power of the millennium- and ideas about the future. When the year 2000 approached,
fears of technological disaster resulting from the Y2K computer bug that could crash global sys-
tems were mixed with an optimistic sense for what the future had in store. As the twenty-first
century approached architects sought unconventional forms. Aided by computers for both spatial
exploration and manufacturing, architects created forms previously unimagined. Later there was
a shift to selective memory from the past, as celebrations and sales of midcentury modern design
became ubiquitous in both design and marketing.

       Figure 5.3.5. The south wall of the Memorial Room at Smith Hall.

This is a very brief cultural context for the campus buildings to be visited and explored. The Uni-
versity of Illinois Urbana-Champaign campus is composed of both traditional and modern struc-
tures.
Smith Memorial Hall is a clear example of a traditional structure designed in the Beaux-Arts style.
It was designed by James White and Edward Wright and was dedicated in 1922. Named for Tina
Weedon Smith (there is a large painting of Smith in the south stairwell), it was built with funds

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 284
  donated by her husband, Captain Thomas J. Smith, who had been on the university's Board of
  Trustees (1897-1903). Smith Hall was the public face of the Music Department. As such it turns its
  facade out toward the Urbana community at the corner of South Mathews Street and West Nevada
  Street. The visitor enters from the east up a grand set of stairs and through a monumentally scaled
  doorway framed by Ionic columns (see figures 5.3.1 and 5.3.2).

   Figure 5.3.6. The skylight of Smith Hall, north stair hall.

  The building receives the visitor through a long cork-floored lobby before one enters the large
  performance hall. This grand wood-paneled space seats 650 on the main level and 450 in the bal-
  cony. The hall is lined with mahogany to provide a fully occupied space with a period of rever-
  beration of 1.75 seconds. There are provisions to lower the period of vibration when the hall is

                              9

  unoccupied.
  The parti of Smith is simple: it has a central performance hall surrounded by a corridor with class-
  rooms and practice spaces to the outside (see figures 5.3.3 and 5.3.4).
  Moving upstairs on beautiful curving stairways to the south and north of the lobby one ascends
  to the second floor, where a second recital space sits above the entry and lobby. This space is the

9. Aguayo, Schmitz, and Arel, "Smith Memorial Hall."
   285 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
   jewel of the school. The Memorial Room is a drawing room used for chamber music and student
   recitals. This elaborately decorated, stone veneer space is lined with marble and has crystal chan-
   deliers. The space itself is a simple rectangle with two fireplaces at each end. The room overlooks
   Mathews Street to the east (see figure 5.3.5).
   Leaving Smith Hall through the north exit one moves under the stairs landing. Before exiting one
   can look up and see the coffered ceiling with an inset skylight. The skylight has been blocked, but
   its presence in the ceiling is still apparent (see figure 5.3.6).

                                              Etemenanki Ziggurat.

   To the north of Smith Memorial Hall is the Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building (LCLB;
   this was formerly the Foreign Languages Building and commonly referred to as FLB). This struc-
   ture was designed by Holabird and Root in the late 1960s and was dedicated in 1970. Stories abound
   about the LCLB to explain its appearance. It is rumored that the building's shape was determined
   by the presence of an early supercomputer housed in the basement. This story claims that should
   there be a catastrophe, the building's shell would fall outward, thus saving the computer below. In
   reality, this structure is similar to other buildings designed in the 1960s such as the Art and Archi-
   tecture Building at Yale University by Paul Rudolf, completed in 1963, or the gigantic Boston City

                                                                                                                                 10

   Hall by Kallman, McKinnell, and Knowles, finished in 1968.
   Many feel the structure is out of place on the Main Quad. It certainly looks different from the other

   buildings. It does, however, maintain the massing necessary to reinforce the figural quality of the
   Main Quad. Its massing has an interesting interpretation worth exploring.

          Now the whole world had one language and a common speech. As people moved east-
          ward, they found a plain in Shinar and settled there.
          They said to each other, "Come, let's make bricks and bake them thoroughly." They used
          brick instead of stone, and tar for mortar. Then they said, "Come, let us build ourselves a
          city, with a tower that reaches to the heavens, so that we may make a name for ourselves;
          otherwise we will be scattered over the face of the whole earth."
          But the Lord came down to see the city and the tower the people were building.
          The Lord said, "If as one people speaking the same language they have begun to do this,
          then nothing they plan to do will be impossible for them. Come, let us go down and con-
          fuse their language so they will not understand each other."
          So the Lord scattered them from there over all the earth, and they stopped building the
          city. That is why it was called Babel--because there the Lord confused the language of the

                                                                                                                                                                                                 11

          whole world. From there the Lord scattered them over the face of the whole earth.

   The Tower of Babel story from Genesis describes the existence of different languages after the
   Great Flood. Given the architecture of the time and the location suggested in Genesis, it is believed

11. Genesis 11:1-9.
    287 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
                                                                                          12

   that the Tower of Babel was a ziggurat. The first ziggurats were built approximately 5000 years
   ago (3000 BCE). They were stepped pyramids predating the Egyptian pyramids (see figure 5.3.7).

                 Figure 5.3.9. Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building looking west at first floor.

   The LCLB is a spatial inversion of the Tower of Babel. Instead of a solid stepping up to the heavens,
   a spatial void steps down from the heavens to the ground floor (see figures 5.3.8-5.3.11).
   The poetry of the space is linked to this idea of spatial inversion. The light of the heavens shines
   through to the spaces below, illuminating the inhabitants:

           It is language, in the sense of a structured form of contact, communication, description
           and argument through signs and symbols, that makes us human not only as a species but
           as individuals: that for each one of us the acquisition of a language in this sense makes full

                                                                                                                                             13

           human consciousness, the consciousness of self, possible.

12. The Etemenanki ziggurat in ancient Babylon was rebuilt in the 600s BCE. This structure is said to have been 91 meters
    square at the base and 91 meters tall with seven levels. At 300 feet tall it was an enormous structure built of mud and
    baked bricks (see George, "Tower of Babel").

13. Magee, Philosophy and the Real World, 58.
                                                                                                                                                                  Kevin J. Hinders | 288
             Figure 5.3.10. Literatures, Cultures, and Linguistics Building, looking east from third floor.

Language is seen as the means to create and hold society together. The ability to understand one-
self and one another is crucial to the function of a university. The expression of the LCLB's desire
to bring the people of the world together in an ability to communicate and share is, indeed, a noble
act. The flags lining its corridors represent a sample of cultures and their languages.

                            Figure 5.3.11. Plan diagram of the first floor of the Literatures, Cultures,
                            and Linguistics Building. Note the structural piers of the building are
                            outside the building's envelope. Read long description of figure.

289 | Part 5.3. Campus Buildings and a Search for Meaning: A Study of Three Structures
Did the architect really intentionally invert a Mesopotamian ziggurat to symbolically tie the LCLB
to the Tower of Babel and a story about the advent of the different human languages? It is uncer-
tain. What is certain is that the building is what it is. Its form is open to interpretation. Much
like a Picasso cubist painting evokes different reactions from viewers, so can architecture. In this
instance, the interpretation of this symbolic inversion may affect the visitor's opinion and yet
the building may not ever be interpreted in this manner. One aspect is clear. The building lacks
the ornamentation of Smith Memorial Hall. It has a limited material palette of brick, glass, plas-
ter walls, and acoustical tile ceilings. Floors are now carpeted. The building's striking appearance
comes from its exterior, multistory piers and its large interior volume.
Leaving LCLB one can move south to the Carl R. Woese Institute for Genomic Biology (IGB). This
building is a relative newcomer to the Urbana-Champaign campus. A strategic site was chosen for
its construction that is both important and difficult to manage, as it is just east of the historic Mor-
row Plots. As a result the structure could not be allowed to throw a significant amount of shade
on the plants. The building's massing was carefully studied to avoid adverse consequences to the
research that has been ongoing for over a century. The IGB is now directly opposite the Library,
and faces the South Quad east of the Morrow Plots (see figure 5.3.12).

                  right in the photo were carefully studied to avoid adversely affecting the
                  experimental fields. Architects: CUH2A

Designed by CUH2A (now HDR) and dedicated in 2006, the building deliberately shares exterior
physical characteristics with nearby Georgian buildings such as Mumford Hall (see figure 5.3.13).

The basic parti of the building can be seen as three linked pavilions (see figures 5.3.14 and 5.3.15).

The IGB has a terrace to the west framed by a freestanding pavilion to the north and an attached
pavilion to the south. The lower-level plan shows labs and office spaces under the terrace. In the
north pavilion on the lower level are a small café and meeting area, along with conference and lec-
ture spaces (see figures 5.3.16 and 5.3.17).

Similar to the Temple Hoyne Buell Hall atrium stairway discussed in part 4.2, the stairway here
serves as both circulation and decoration. Though comparatively small, the atrium is a reception
space that juxtaposes materials to bring attention to the nature of the building. The stairs are
sleek, stainless steel with glass. One senses the clean, polished qualities of an efficient lab, while
the south wall is a simple, sumptuous wall of wood veneer and the floors are polished limestone.
The architects have contrasted natural materials--the limestone complete with fossilized remains
at one's feet and the wood panels with the grain patterns set onto a regular grid--with refined,
machined materials--a perforated metal screen contains images of genetic research material (see
figure 5.3.18).

The material palette is limited throughout the building. The same vocabulary used for the atrium
stairway is repeated in the north pavilion stairway that links the lobby with the lower level (see
figure 5.3.19).

   Smith Memorial Hall, the LCLB, and the IGB stretch across almost a century of construction on
   campus. Each strives to be a structure built for its time with the expectation that it will make sig-
   nificant contributions to the university for years to come. The attitudes toward the built environ-
   ment and the manner in which meaning can be presented to the user is apparent in each
   structure--even as our societal values and the manner in which we use our structures change.

           We shape our buildings: thereafter, they shape us.
           --Sir Winston Churchill

Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education
Building

       About the Walk

       The Education Building shows the vision of A. Richard Williams, professor emeritus of the Illinois
       School of Architecture. The building's design simultaneously attempts to merge itself with the exist-
       ing conditions while providing a very different vision and path for the university's future structures.
       The manner in which the building folds itself into the cultural and physical context, along with its
       superb gardens, shows the depth to which architecture and landscape architecture can become
       integrated.
       This walk takes place at the Education Building located at 1310 South Sixth Street in Champaign. The
       walk begins at the front door of the Architecture Building located at 608 East Lorado Taft Drive in
       Champaign.

  Designers synthesize many ideas when creating a part of the built environment. Ideas are often
  formed by looking at the work of others, exploring new mediums, and trying different materials,
  and these choices often reflect the values of society at the time of creation. The designer can be

                                                                                                                                           1

  seen as either the leader of culture or the mirror of our society.

  Three-dimensional design has been greatly influenced by drawing and modeling approaches
  throughout time. Newly developed techniques allow the designer to explore and understand
  the creation of the built environment in a different light. The Italian painter Giotto di Bondone
  (1269-1337) used layering and scaling of figures. Similarly, late medieval architects used the same
  principles of layering and positioning in their work. During the Renaissance one-point perspective
  was invented, which dramatically influenced painters and architects. The Florentine architect Fil-
  ippo Brunelleschi (1377-1446) first explored this technique of utilizing vanishing points to create
  the illusion of depth and three-dimensional space. This understanding of perspective led to a
  greater ideality in city design and the buildings that supported public spaces that then translated

1. John Meunier made this remark in his opening lecture as dean of the College of Architecture and Environmental Design
  in 1987 at Arizona State University.
                                                                                                                                                                Kevin J. Hinders | 296
to buildings that stressed frontality (the front directly facing the viewer) and eventually led to
the tragic scene discussed in part 3.6. In the 1700s the use of two-point perspective during the
Baroque period had an influence on stage set design, which then translated into an architec-
ture that was no longer frontal but was meant to be seen as an object, such as the royal hunting
lodge in Stupinigi by Filippo Juvarra (1678-1736), completed in 1731. Indeed two-point perspective
influenced many architects from Frank Lloyd Wright to Mies van der Rohe. The Carceri (prison)
series by Giovanni Battista Piranesi (1720-78) even had an influence on the design of Temple Hoyne
Buell Hall in the late twentieth century. In the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, Paul Cézanne
(1839-1906), Pablo Picasso (1881-1973), Piet Mondrian (1872-1944), and others began shifting away
from perspective-based painting. In architecture, work began to reflect an asymmetrical aes-
thetic and a variety of architectural investigations that incorporated a drawing technique called
axonometric to depict structures. Just as Matisse explored combining perspective and axonomet-
ric drawing, architects experimented with this method as a new way to see the world and then
created novel designs. Gerrit Rietveld (1888-1964) created the Rietveld Schröder House in 1924
as a three-dimensional realization of Mondrian's paintings and realized axonometric space. This
movement was called De Stihl. More recently a whole new manner of spatial exploration based
on computer studies has been taking place. The computer has assisted in the realization of work
on many levels, most notably the ability to create structures unimagined until now and the abil-
ity to produce these designs using innovative materials and methods of production. Frank Gehry
(b. 1929) has created his forms using digital capture and computer-aided manufacturing of skins
and structures. The "blob architecture" begun in the mid-1990s was created using computer-aided
design techniques in parametric modeling. Today digital fabrication again is pushing the envelope
as designers rethink the nature of construction (see figures 5.4.1-5.4.5).

Historically, the orthogonal grid has been extremely influential for architects. The Romans utilized
it to great effect. Renaissance, neoclassical, and Beaux-Arts designs continued this tradition. The
use of axis and cross-axis organizational patterns is readily apparent on the Urbana-Champaign
campus, and the rectilinearity of the continental grid is ubiquitous in the inhabited areas of the
United States. One aspect of the history of the continental grid that puzzled designers for years
was the layout of Greek spaces, such as the Acropolis in Athens. One line of thinking is that the
buildings were no longer on a grid due to earthquakes. In 1937, the Greek architect Constantinos
A. Doxiadis (1913-75) published his ideas on Greek space planning in Architectural Space in Ancient
Greece. In this text, Doxiadis convincingly presents the theory that Greek sacred precincts are laid
out on a radial system that reveals the site to viewers sequentially. Buildings are arranged so as
to be seen as objects. The Parthenon, for example, is designed to be seen from the entrance gates
(called the Propylaea) at a three-quarters view. This is the classic image of the Parthenon. Inter-
estingly this is also the backside of the Parthenon. According to Doxiadis the viewer is meant to
proceed along a "sacred way" that draws them toward the entrance on the opposite side. From
there the viewer would see two "horned mountains," which represent the breasts of Mother Earth.

297 | Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education Building
Thus the Acropolis is arranged to hover over Athens and make the viewer aware of this sacred
landscape. Doxiadis's book revealed this ordering system for numerous ancient Greek sites. Con-
trasted with the Roman axis it would become an important revelation for the designer of the Edu-
cation Building.

     Part 5.4. Orientation Map.

The Education Building shows a variety of influences incorporated by A. Richard (Dick) Williams
(1914-2018) of the Illinois School of Architecture. The design simultaneously attempts to merge

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 298
itself with the existing conditions while providing a very different vision and path for the univer-
sity's future structures. The manner in which the building folds itself into the cultural and physi-
cal context, along with its superb gardens, shows the depth to which architecture and landscape
architecture can become integrated.
Williams's vision for the South Quad was different from its current state. He lobbied the university
administration to plant a grove similar to the Illini Grove over the entire South Quad and part
of the military axis. The space would then contrast with the Main Quad to provide a completely
different experience for future generations to enjoy. This arrangement of trees would form a tall
canopy over the entire spaces south of the Undergraduate Library. Buildings would occupy space
beneath the shading canopy and merge with it. In the early 1990s Williams proposed a new art
and architecture library at the crossing of the military axis and the South Quad axis where the
McFarland Memorial Bell Tower is now located. This same vision played a role in his design of the
Education Building.

301 | Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education Building

via Wikimedia Commons, public domain.

Built in the early 1960s, the Education Building is a product of its time and contrasts with Wohlers
Hall (formerly Commerce West), which was built concurrently. Both are steel frame structures.
Wohlers Hall was designed to complete a composition with David Kinley Hall and the Architec-
ture Building (see part 1.1 and figure 1.7). Wohlers Hall has a steel frame structure designed to look
like a brick bearing wall building in the Georgian style. By contrast, the Education Building is a
steel frame structure deigned to look like a concrete frame building in the modernist style known
as brutalism, a subset of modernism that was popular from the 1950s to the mid-1970s. During
this period structures with coffered concrete floors and ceilings were being constructed around
the world. Architects were returning to symmetrical compositions, at least externally. Indeed,
Erlanger House (see part 3.6) is a residential-scale building constructed at the same time as the

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 302
Education Building. Jack Baker, the architect of Erlanger House, and Williams were close peers. It
is certain they shared their investigations.

The siting of the Education Building merges numerous ideas and concepts. In the early 1960s the
field of education anticipated growth. The Baby Boomers were completing their families and edu-
cation for a growing middle class was essential to the mission of the university. The design of the
Education Building included a long-term plan: to build two relatively identical structures that sat
across a shared public space. The precedent for this was seen at Mumford Hall and David Kinley
Hall and Charles Platt's unrealized plan for the Undergraduate Library and Main Library pairing.
Later the Undergraduate Library used the same idea in its entrances, which straddle a lower-level

                      2

courtyard. In the envisioned education complex the lower levels of the structures were to open
up to a shared sunken courtyard space.

Figure 5.4.5A. Plate from Le Carceri d'Invenzione by   Figure 5.4.5B. Interior Blicharski Atrium of Temple
Giovanni Battista Piraanesi (b. 1720-d.1778). This is  Hoyne Buell Hall by Perkins and Will, 1994.
an imaginary prison scene. (Carceri-VII-Zugbrücke).
Wikimedia Commons, public domain.

The two buildings were to form a space that framed the Architecture Building to the north and
the Natural History Building to the south along a central axis (see figure 5.4.6).

2. Williams and Undergraduate Library designer Ambrose Richardson of Richardson, Severns, Scheeler, and Associates
   were contemporaries and friends. Richardson was involved in the design of numerous University of Illinois buildings
   including the Art and Design Building, the Florida Avenue Residence Halls, the Children's Research Center, the Civil Engi­
   neering Hydrosystems Laboratory, Krannert Art Museum, the Law Building, the Music Building, and the Water Survey
   Research Building (see Tate and Franch, Illini Place).

In addition the Education Building was designed to align with the spaces adjacent to the Archi-
tecture Building. The first floor of the Education Building is sized to correspond to the opening
between the Architecture Building and Wohlers Hall. As an avid sailor, Wiliams related the Educa-
tion Building to a ship that rested in direct alignment to the "boat slip" between the Architecture
Building and Wohlers Hall. Thus the complex relies on the strategies used by earlier campus plan-
ners: axis, cross axis, alignment across a figural void, and an attention to the landscape.

What sets the strategy apart is the attention to siting based also on strategies outlined by Doxiadis.
The Education Building is sited to be approached at three-quarters view, similar to the Parthenon.
Just as Platt had been extremely conscious of designing for architects when creating the Archi-
tecture Building, Williams sited the Education Building to be viewed at a specific angle from the
Architecture Building's main entrance. The propylaea view of the Education Building (a monumen-
tal gateway to a sacred precinct in ancient Greece) is from the landing in front of the Architecture
Building (see figures 5.4.7-5.4.9).

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 304
           Figure 5.4.7. Diagram. Left: Site plan of the Architecture and Education Buildings. Center: Site

           the two plans. The platform in front of the Architecture Building provides a three-quarter view
           of the Education Building similar to the Athenian precedent.

               Figure 5.4.8A. Three-quarter view of the Parthenon from the Propylaea.

  Figure 5.4.9A. Head-on view of the Parthenon. Photo by George E. Koronaios via

Seen in this manner the Education Building can be interpreted as a modern Parthenon, stripped of
its perimeter columns. The upper floors of the Education Building are cantilevered using the steel
frame that renders the perimeter colonnade of its precedent unnecessary. Like the Parthenon, the
building has a monumental presence, raised on a platform, above the ground plane. In this aspect,
the Education Building is more like a Roman temple. Roman temples had axial stairs on one side,
whereas Greek temples had a platform that could be approached from all directions. This works
well in the case of the Education Building as the grade slopes down from south to north toward
Boneyard Creek, which allows for an accessible entrance on the south. The platform is at grade
to the south and has a commanding, axial staircase at the main entry on the north side. Adjacent
to the west side of the Education Building is a discreet garden. Walled off from the noise of Sixth
Street, this space is a quiet oasis for patrons. It is often used as an outdoor classroom on pleasant
days.

Ascending the north stairs deposits the viewer under the building canopy provided by the third-
floor overhang. A large picture window frames the view back toward the Architecture Building's
gates. Two concrete walks are consciously designed to relate to the gates across the way to the
north (see figure 5.4.10).

The large glass floor-to-ceiling window is intended to blur the distinction between interior and

307 | Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education Building
exterior. On the outside the concrete sidewalks have been sandblasted to reveal different-sized
concrete aggregate. The interior spaces use a similar-sized terrazzo aggregate that moves from
inside to outside at the entries. This careful manipulation of the ground plane's concrete treat-
ment surrounds the building (see figures 5.4.11 and 5.4.12).

Figure 5.4.10. View from Education Building looking north. The Education Building
resembles a ship, while the space between Wohlers Hall and the Architecture Building is a
metaphorical boat slip.

scape similar to Frank Lloyd Wright's extended overhangs and terraces in his Prairie Style houses
such as the Robie House in Chicago (see figures 5.4.13 and 5.4.14).

At the north end of the building, symmetrical entries are shifted off axis to align with circulation

                       5.4.16. Plan comparison of the deck of the Queen Mary, 1936, with two

                       Shading reveals the ship's basic circulation/parti. Center: Plan of the second
                       floor of the Education Building. Shading reveals the basic parti and its

This shift allows for central spaces on both the north and south ends of the building. Williams's
nautical analogy is made again. Just as a ship has decks fore and aft, the Education Building main-
tains its important communal spaces at each end (see figures 5.4.16-5.4.18).

                                       stairwell with the west garden.

                         to allow light from above to filter into the lower levels. The stair risers are

311 | Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education Building
Moving up the stairs to the third floor one encounters the faculty offices at the perimeter of the
plan. Like lifeboats, these offices are suspended over the terraces below (see figure 5.4.19).

             Figure 5.4.19. Comparative diagram of the Queen Mary and the Education Building. Top: Plan
             of the Queen Mary's sports deck with lifeboats. Bottom: Plan of the Education Building's
             third floor. The offices open onto a continuous perimeter walkway.

Economy, like in a ship's cabin, is used when allocating office space. The resemblance to a ship
becomes complete as the core links to a double-loaded corridor. The offices have a low roof
with wooden baffles in the ceiling. These baffles appear outside the building envelope in another
attempt to merge the interior and exterior environments. Today, the exterior baffles are well worn,
like material on a sea-faring ship, while the interior baffles remain unweathered.
The design of the Education Building masks the central core by lining the interior side of the cor-
ridors with wooden baffles. These baffles help soften the acoustics in the corridor and reduce the
echo from the hard surfaces. The baffles also mask the numerous vents that open into the cor-
ridor. Here Williams is using a precedent from the Finnish architect Alvar Aalto, seen in multiple
works, including the interior of Finlandia Hall, that was used later at Krannert Center for the Per-
forming Arts as a treatment on the walls between the lobby and the Coldwell Playhouse and the
Tryon Festival Theater. (see part 5.2).
Thus, though radically different from its surrounding structures in appearance, and notably part

                                                                                                                                                              Kevin J. Hinders | 312
of an unfinished composition, the Education Building has woven itself into the campus fabric in
a unique manner. Temple Hoyne Buell Hall has supplanted a second, symmetrical building for the
College of Education. Yet there remains the germ of an idea that may someday lead to a court-
yard space between the two structures and perhaps the introduction of a grove. This is the nature
of design: it builds off the past with a hope for the future. The future becomes the present and
designers choose what to draw from and how to be inventive in their own time. Dick Williams
synthesized disparate concepts, construction technologies, and siting principles as he worked to
create an architecture of his time, built for future generations.

313 | Part 5.4. Synthesis of Ideas: The Education Building
Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and
Conservation: Business Instructional
Facility

About the Walk

     The University of Illinois has pledged to build LEED-accredited structures in the future and to
     become a leader in sustainability. An explanation of the basic ideas and strategies of sustainability
     and LEED accreditation is followed by a walk through the Business Instructional Facility to illustrate
     the principles, successes, and failures of the university's first LEED-accredited building.
     This walk takes place at the Business Instructional Facility located at 515 East Gregory Drive in
     Champaign.

       As the flagship public university in the state of Illinois, the campus has a moral and ethical
       responsibility to lead, to set aggressive goals, to work to meet them, and to serve as a
       model for the community, state, and nation. The [Illinois Climate Action] Plan represents a
       roadmap to a new, prosperous, and sustainable future for the University. It outlines strate-

                                                                                                                                                                                                         1

       gies, initiatives, and targets toward meeting the stated goal of carbon neutrality by 2050.

Sustainability in architecture is the idea that a building can allow humans to coexist with nature
in a maintained balance. The notion continues: in creating this balance one generation will not
overburden future generations with a built environment that cannot be continued without great
hardship to either humankind or our natural environment. Whether perceived as a moral issue or
an issue of species survival, sustainability has become an international concern. A graduate archi-
tecture class (Architecture 577) on the Urbana-Champaign campus was asked about sustainability.
When questioned about the path necessary to achieve an environmental balance, the overwhelm-
ing majority felt that new technologies will solve current problems. A minority felt that funda-
mental changes, such as behavioral shifts toward conservation, are needed to reach a sustainable

1. iCAP Portal, "Make Climate Commitments."

                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 314
  future. It is now becoming clear that both new technological approaches and conservation strate-
  gies are necessary to meet the goals set forth in 2008, when the University of Illinois Urbana-
  Champaign signed the American College and University Presidents' Climate Commitment:

          We believe colleges and universities must exercise leadership in their communities and
          throughout society by modeling ways to minimize global warming emissions, and by pro-
          viding the knowledge and the educated graduates to achieve climate neutrality. Campuses
          that address the climate challenge by reducing global warming emissions and by inte-
          grating sustainability into their curriculum will better serve their students and meet their
          social mandate to help create a thriving, ethical and civil society.

          We further believe that colleges and universities that exert leadership in addressing cli-
          mate change will stabilize and reduce their long-term energy costs, attract excellent stu-
          dents and faculty, attract new sources of funding, and increase the support of alumni and

                                                        2

          local communities.

  This signing committed the campus to carbon neutrality by the year 2050 and set a series of short-
  term goals. To fulfill this self-imposed responsibility the university created the Illinois Climate
  Action Plan (iCAP) in 2010, 2015, 2020 and a new version, the Illinois Climate Action Plan will be
  released in October 2025. Thes examine outcomes and set more goals. Specifically:

          iCAP 2020, the third update to the Illinois Climate Action Plan since 2010, contains 56
          SMART (specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-based) objectives organized
          into eight key themes: Energy, Transportation, Land & Water, Zero Waste, Education,
          Engagement, Resilience, and Implementation.

          With the dedication, passion, and hard work of our campus and community, the University
          of Illinois Urbana-Champaign can become a model for innovative, progressive environ-

                                                           3

          mental stewardship.

  The 2015 iCAP outlined a path to carbon neutrality that uses conservation strategies (reduce,
  reuse, recycle) such as those outlined in the 2013 Energy Conservation Incentive Program, which
  encourage conservation through building improvements and behavioral changes. The 2015 iCAP
  estimated that half the emission reductions needed to get to carbon neutrality will come from
  conservation. The other half will come from a wide variety of strategies including alternative forms
  of energy generation, better management of transportation (especially air travel), better water
  and waste management, sequestration methods, curricular education, outreach, and sustainabil-

2. "American College and University Presidents' Climate Commitment."
3. Institute for Sustainability, Illinois Climate Action Plan.

   315 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
ity. The Illinois Climate Action Plan 2020 followed with a robust 200 plus page document that has
a comprehensive list of over 50 objectives for the campus and its inhabitants.

                         Part 5.5. Orientation Map.

In another attempt to be able to react to be responsible, the university committed to creating a
resilience plan in 2016, outlining how the campus will react to atmospheric conditions and climate
change:

       Illinois is a Charter Signatory of the Second Nature Climate Commitment, which combines
       a Carbon Commitment the campus signed in 2008 with the newly signed Resilience Com-
       mitment. The full Climate Commitment formally acknowledges that the effects of climate
       change are already felt--and that universities and colleges must pursue both mitigation
       and adaptation to combat the unfolding crisis.
       By adding the Resilience Commitment, Illinois has made a pledge to evaluate campus vul-

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 316
          nerabilities to a changing climate in its landscapes, natural resources, and energy produc-

                                                                                                                                                               4

          tion--and to make an action plan that addresses those weaknesses.

  Managing this complex endeavor to incentivize conservation and promote smart growth is com-
  plicated. In 2017 the Urbana-Champaign campus began a master plan update. The Core Planning
  Committee, along with Smith Group, the consultant hired to create the document, met with cam-
  pus leaders. In one meeting with the deans of the colleges it became clear that iCAP's stated policy
  that the campus would not increase its building square footage was at direct odds with their plans.
  Many of the deans were not even aware of iCAP and were concerned that iCAP's edict about square
  footage ran contrary to their ambitions for growth. The iCAP strategy to limit physical growth to
  help limit energy consumption did not correlate to most colleges' intentions to increase enroll-
  ment by as much as 30 percent. The 2018 approved master plan seeks to remove inefficiencies
  by renovating outdated structures and replacing obsolete buildings with more sustainable ones.
  The largest inefficiency addressed in the plan is the use of existing campus spaces. The review of
  classroom, office, and research space use was pivotal in exploring how the university could better
  use its existing assets. Rather than build new structures it seems the university can benefit from
  scheduling and assigning space more efficiently.

  As the importance of building sustainably has increased so have the means to encourage intel-
  ligent design choices. Energy codes in Illinois have addressed the construction of commercial
  buildings since the 2004 Energy Efficient Commercial Building Act (based on the 2000 Interna-
  tional Energy Conservation Code). Since then the 2012 Illinois Energy Conservation Code and sub-
  sequent versions have required designers to meet basic energy conservation goals.

  In 1993 the United States Green Building Council (USGBC) was created. In 2000, Leadership in
  Energy and Environmental Design (LEED)--a green building certification system--was first used.
  The USGBC states that "LEED established a baseline--a universally agreed-upon, holistic system
  for reducing environmental impacts, saving resources, impacting human health, reducing carbon

                                                                                              5

  emissions and addressing climate change."

          We're committed to transforming how our buildings are designed, constructed and oper-
          ated through LEED.

          Our vision at the U.S. Green Building Council (USGBC) is that buildings and communities
          will regenerate and sustain the health and vitality of all life within a generation. Our mis-
          sion is to transform the way buildings and communities are designed, built and operated,
          enabling an environmentally and socially responsible, healthy, and prosperous environ-

4. ISEE, "Illinois a Green Campus Leader."
5. U.S. Green Building Council, "LEED: The Past, Present, and Future."

   317 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
       ment that improves the quality of life. Through our community network, continuous col-
       laboration with industry experts, market research publications and LEED professional
       credentials, our global staff is working every day to help advance spaces that are brighter

                                                                                                          6

       and healthier for us to live, work and play in.

       LEED . . . is an internationally recognized green building certification system, providing
       third-party verification that a building or community was designed and built using strate-
       gies aimed at improving performance across all the metrics that matter most: energy sav-
       ings, water efficiency, CO2emissions reduction, improved indoor environmental quality,

                                                                                                                                               7

       and stewardship of resources and sensitivity to their impacts.

The USGBC created this system to assist designers in making decisions to create more sustainable
environments. Grading systems establish four levels of certification for a structure: Certification
(basic), Silver, Gold, and Platinum (the highest level). There is also a LEED certification called "Net
Zero" which can be achieved by purchasing enough renewable energy certificates to equal the
annual energy use for 5 to 10 years. The University has done this with the Campus Instructional
Facility, and Wymer Hall. The grading system updates periodically as industry standards, materi-
als, and construction methods change. Two issues should be underscored: that the LEED system
uses third-party verification to score and certify a building and the building's owner must pay for
this certification.

                                                                                                                                                                                              8

In 2009 the Business Instructional Facility (BIF), designed by Pelli Clarke Pelli Architects, became
the first LEED-accredited building on campus. In 2025 over 12% percent of the gross square
footage on campus was LEED accredited and 35 buildings had achieved LEED certification with

                                                                                                      9

several others in an active certification process.

The Gies College of Business's Business Instructional Facility at 515 East Gregory Drive opened in
2008 and was certified with a LEED Platinum rating in 2009. The structure received 52 of 69 pos-
sible points in the system used at that time. The LEED scorecard indicated the building was rated
highly in all areas: 10 of 14 in the sustainable sites category; 4 of 5 in the water efficiency category;
13 of 17 in the energy and atmosphere category; 7 of 13 in the materials and resources category; 13
of 15 in the indoor environmental quality category; and 5 of 5 in the innovation category (see the
appendix for the scorecard).

The BIF tour begins in the basement. Unlike Temple Hoyne Buell Hall, whose mechanical rooms

are cramped with equipment, the BIF has almost the entire basement area set aside for mechanical
equipment (see figure 5.5.1).

                                               Figure 5.5.1. Business Instructional Facility
                                               lower-level plan. Architects: Pelli Clarke
                                               Pelli Architects. Courtesy of the University
