The building is serviced by both a steam line and a chilled water line, which alleviates the need for
some equipment that might otherwise be needed on-site. The basement also serves as an area of
refuge in case of a tornado so it must have space suitable for occupants in case of emergency. The
building has numerous air-handling units to service multiple zones so that different areas of the
structure can be supplied with heating and cooling simultaneously--depending on the loads. For
example, when the auditorium is filled to capacity it may require cooling at the same time a small
classroom on the other side of the building requires heating.
Moving upstairs into the atrium one finds the main space with a window wall facing south. The
tall, four-story atrium organizes the building with a U-shaped parti that faces south (see figures
5.5.2 and 5.5.3).
The atrium's glass wall is carefully screened in two ways. A large series of trusses extend a can-
tilever beyond the building envelope to the south. This canopy shades the atrium in the sum-
mer months, allowing light into the space while preventing solar heat gain. The second screen is
a computer-controlled series of vertical shading devices just inside the glass curtain wall. These
shades are programmed to prevent heat gain when it is undesirable and, when it is cool, to allow
solar radiation into the atrium to passively warm the space. Ideally the shades would be located
externally for maximum efficiency (by mounting them outside less heat is gained in the interior),

319 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
however it is impractical to have exterior, operable shades because of cost, maintenance, and
weathering (see figures 5.5.4-5.5.7).

                                                Figure 5.5.2. Business Instructional
                                                Facility's first floor partí diagram. The
                                                courtyard faces south to maximize winter
                                                sun infiltration for solar heat gain.

In the winter months the atrium acts as a large solar collector. The heat rises to the ceiling using
a convection HVAC system that reduces power consumption. The operable mesh solar shades are
controlled to allow daylight into the space. Photo sensors on lights help reduce energy consump-
tion as outside light entering the room often alleviates the need for the use of electric lighting. The
building employs triple-pane windows that absorb less solar radiation than traditional windows.
Surrounding the atrium are classrooms, support spaces, and offices. A staircase is suspended into
the atrium to serve the first three floors. Breakout/lounge spaces overlook the atrium on the east
and west sides. These spaces lead to corridors extending south. Along the west corridor on the
first floor are bathrooms and a series of changing rooms for patrons who run or bicycle to the
building. The rooms help promote alternative methods of transportation other than automobiles
and are necessary to secure points for LEED accreditation. They are located near the entrance and
are close to bike racks outside.
Down the corridor is a weather vestibule that gives access to the exterior garden and a 300-seat
auditorium. The garden has been designed to minimize water consumption and uses native plant
species. A goal was to make the landscaped area very low maintenance but patrons complained
that the space was "weed infested" and asked for some grassy areas. The garden today is a com-
promise.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 320
From the courtyard one can see a photovoltaic array on the roof of the auditorium (see figure
5.5.8).

Figure 5.5.3. Business Instructional Facility first floor plan with poché. Read long description of figure.

These solar panels provide a fraction of the building's electrical needs but are symbolic of the
efforts toward creating a greener future. The photovoltaic panels harvest solar energy and convert
it to electricity as a clean, renewable energy source. To illustrate the complexity of sustainability it
is necessary to point out possible contradictions. One university facilities engineer was concerned

321 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
about the wisdom of the photovoltaic array. He believes the number of roof penetrations and the
likelihood of needing to replace the roof will far exceed the value of the array's energy savings.

Figure 5.5.4. Building section through the Business Instructional Facility atrium. The summer sun is shaded from
the building by the large overhang. This reduces cooling costs. The winter sunlight is allowed to penetrate deeply
into the space. Using the natural sunlight in the winter for passive solar heat gain lowers the amount of energy
the building needs to receive from Abbott Power Plant. Read long description of figure.

Above the southwest entry vestibule is a green roof, also visible from the courtyard (there was
once a roof garden above part of the third floor on the east side of BIF that was removed for an
addition). These gardens are equipped with native species plants that are already adapted to the
climate and also promote biodiversity. The purpose of a green roof is threefold. First, the plants
help absorb and slow down rainwater before it is discharged into the stormwater system. This is
an important aspect in cities where most of the soils have been replaced by hard surfaces such as
buildings and asphalt or concrete. Recognizing this, designers are turning to strategies that allow
for more absorption of rainwater into the soil surrounding buildings. This helps deter flooding and

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 322
assists in groundwater recharge, which helps sustain underground aquifers. The second purpose
of a green roof is to help reduce the heat island effect. Plants shade the roof and maintain evapo-
rative cooling during summer months and can assist in insulating. The third feature of a green roof
is that it is an attractive alternative to a large expanse of rubberized roof membrane. Roofs can be
made a visual and sometimes occupiable amenity for a structure's occupants.

                        Figure 5.5.5. Interior view of the Business Instructional Facility atrium from
                        the ground level.

Water management is an important aspect of green buildings. The roof gardens, permeable paving,
and native plantings help with stormwater control. Little irrigation or potable water is used for
BIF's landscaping. On the supply side, BIF uses low-volume showerheads, toilets, and faucets to
reduce water consumption. These fixtures reduce the amount of potable water used and thus
reduce the load on the Mahomet Aquifer, which supplies Urbana-Champaign.
When choosing building materials there are important considerations to be made. Long-term
resiliency is a university tradition. It is understood that the university builds structures to last
100 years and beyond. As a result, the choice of durable materials is an important one. Moving
through BIF one is confronted with a limited palette of materials specifically chosen to help create
a healthy, long-lasting environment. For example, BIF's zinc roof is employed to reflect heat away
from the building to lower cooling costs and it is expected to last 100 years or more.

323 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
Figure 5.5.6. Interior view of the atrium from the third-floor level.

Figure 5.5.7. Exterior view of Business
Instructional Facility roof overhang
designed for solar shading of the
atrium.

                                                                       Kevin J. Hinders | 324
   All materials require energy to extract, transport, manufacture, and install. Later these materials
   may need to be disassembled or deconstructed or they decompose. This total life cycle is referred
   to as the embedded energy (also referred to as embodied energy) of the material. Materials such
   as nonrecycled aluminum take a large amount of energy to extract, produce, and transport, which
   leads to a high embedded energy rating. In contrast, concrete produced nearby has a relatively low
   embedded energy rating. Choosing materials that balance their embedded energy and durability
   is essential to creating a more sustainable environment.

                        Figure 5.5.8. View of the Auditorium solar array and green roof.

   Some building materials may also have unhealthy characteristics. Volatile organic compounds
   (VOCs) can be found in caulks, adhesives, and paints. These VOCs are harmful to work and live
   with as they negatively impact air quality. Some materials can emit VOCs for years. Other mate-
   rials that may cause problems contain formaldehyde, such as some plywood and carpeting. The
   BIF has carbon dioxide monitoring to help sustain long-term occupant comfort and health. The

                                                                                                                       10

   building uses high-performance, low-VOC insulation. BIF scored high for its indoor environmen-
   tal quality because of the careful selection of nontoxic substances and this monitoring.
   BIF attracts students to its atrium for meetings, studying, and snacks. It has become a hub of activ-

10. In the Urbana-Champaign area the single most cost-effective investment one can make to lower energy usage is in insu­
    lation.
     325 | Part 5.5. Sustainability, LEED, and Conservation: Business Instructional Facility
ity to gather and exchange ideas. The building's success is that it has created a place people enjoy
and want to come to while helping to lower the university's carbon footprint through carefully
designed and executed construction.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 326
Epilogue

          In a time when a new formalism represents the avant-garde, we, like Nathan Ricker, look
          beyond current fashion, striving to leverage technical virtuosity in the service of perfor-

                                                                                                                                             1

          mative design, aesthetic expression, and service to society.

  These walks have been designed to provide insight into why the built world looks the way it does.
  The goal of exposing the reader to contextual influences--historical, political, economic, tech-
  nological, and legal--that manifest themselves in how humans create structures and adapt their
  world to fit the situations of their time is at the heart of these pages.

  Though limited to the local context, many of these concepts translate to other places. Some of
  the influences are demographically derived. Most are determined by aesthetics--the tastes of the
  time and location. These continue to change as the university and its surrounding communities
  become more and more international in nature, with an increasingly diverse faculty and student
  body. When the university began as the Illinois Industrial University it had only a handful of build-
  ings--all of which are gone. The shift from freestanding buildings in a landscape garden or agricul-
  tural setting to enclosed quadrangles set into the urban grid of two adjacent cities has taken less
  than 150 years and continues to be altered to suit the needs of the present. As the university has
  grown, it has shifted from an institution focused on educating the peoples of Illinois in agriculture
  and industry to a leading global research 1 institution serving both the citizens of Illinois and the
  people of the world. The university, true to the nature of a top academic research institution, now

                                                                                      2

  features sixteen major academic units.

  Functional needs today have dramatically changed since the first classes were held in 1868.
  Greater control of interior environments has become a necessity. Teaching methods, technolo-
  gies, research facilities, and services have had to keep pace with the ever-changing world. Labora-
  tories, lecture halls, classrooms, and offices, and the infrastructure needed to support them, have
  all been adapted for the digital world of the twenty-first century.

  Structural innovations and changes in construction have long been incorporated into the univer-
  sity's built environment and surroundings. For example, new cladding techniques continue to be

1. University of Illinois School of Architecture, "History."
2. The major academic units are the Carle Illinois College of Medicine; the College of Agricultural, Consumer, and Environ­

   mental Sciences; the College of Applied Health Sciences; the College of Education; the College of Fine and Applied Arts;
   the College of Law; the College of Liberal Arts and Sciences; the College of Media; the College of Veterinary Medicine;
   the Division of General Studies; Gies College of Business; the Graduate College; Grainger College of Engineering; the
   School of Social Work; the School of Information Sciences; and the School of Labor and Employment Relations.

   327 | Epilogue
explored, particularly to bring contemporary buildings into harmony with existing structures and
create a holistic environment without stifling innovation and appearing regressive.
Underlying all these shifts lies a single hope: That the designs created today are leading to a better
environment. This approach to design can result in a nonstatic, ever-changing world that will
adapt, adjust, and keep pace with the needs of its populace for their time. That is the nature of
design: to create something for the present, based on the lessons of the past, that will be found
valuable in the future.

Glossary

Aedicule

A small temple, room, or structure used as a shrine or a niche for a statue in ancient Roman
architecture. This became translated to mean an opening such as a door or a window, framed
by columns on either side, and a pediment above. In classical architecture a window or door-
way was celebrated as an opening in a wall with such a column and pediment treatment.
In contemporary architecture an aedicule can mark/celebrate a special location such as the
crossing of two circulation zones.

Aggregate

The inert material (sand, rocks, gravel, rubble, parts of broken sculptures, etc.) that is bound
together with lime mortar and cement to form concrete. Romans often used old pottery
shards, rocks and brick fragments along with sand as their aggregate. Today rocks and sand
form the bulk of aggregate used in the Midwestern United States.

Arcuated

Arched construction. This construction technology normally uses arches to play a structural
role, but that is not a rule.

Axis

A central or pivotal structure, typically a line, about which a composition is arranged.

Axis Mundi

Also referred to as a world axis: a line or stem through the earth's center connecting its sur-
face to the underworld and the heavens and around which the universe revolves.

Beam

A rigid member or structure supported at each end, subject to bending stresses from a     direc-
tion perpendicular to its length.

Bending

A force that tends to cause a member to deform (bend). In architectiure bending forces are
usually attributed to gravity loads which cause mambers to deflect (change their original

329 | Glossary
      form). A bending moment is the reaction induced in a structural element when an exter-
      nal force is applied to the element causing the element to bend. The most common or simplest
      structural element subjected to bending is the beam.

Buckling

      A deformation which occurs in a member when forces are applied axially to its ends. A column
      may deflect and buckle if the load applied to it is greater than its capacity to resist the force(s).

Buttress

      An exterior masonry structure that opposes the lateral thrust of an arch or a vault (abutment).
      When the buttress disengages from the adjacent pier it is called a flying buttress.

Centering

      A wooden framework on which an arch or dome is supported until the keystone in an the arch
      or the oculus in a dome is in place. Once the keystone (the top voussoir) is placed the center-
      ing can be removed and the compressive weight of the stones will hold the arcuated compo-
      nent in place.

Column

      A rigid, relatively slender, upright support. An engaged column is a column which is partially
      embedded in a wall.

Composite

      Made up of various parts or elements. A Composite Structure uses combinations of arcuated,
      trabeated and/or tensile systems to create a built structure.

Compression

      A force that pushes inward on a mass.

Concrete

      Mixture of lime + water + aggregate + cement. Cement is not concrete. Cement is a binding
      agent in concrete.

Concrete Masonry Unit (CMU)

      A prefabricated concrete member, much like a brick, which is used in construction. CMU can
      be be used as a load bearing element or to fill in between structure.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 330
Dome

      A hemispherical vault (arch rotated 360 degrees on its vertical axis).
Elevations

      The outer vertical surfaces of a structure. The idea is that the plan is `elevated' into the third
      dimension in order to give spatial closure.
Façade

      The `face' of a building, especially the principal front that looks onto a street or open space.
Figure/Ground

      Relating to or denoting the perception of images by the distinction of objects from a back-
      ground from which they appear to stand out, especially in contexts where this distinction is
      ambiguous. This principle of allows viewers to determine and give preference to one figure (or
      set of figures) over another. There exists a drawing type referred to as a Figure/Ground.
Flange

      The top and bottom chord of a beam. This upper and lower portion of a beam or truss is used
      to resist bending.
Girder

      A large beam for supporting a structure. A girder typically carries point loads- that is loads
      which are not evenly distributed across the entire member.
Hierarchy

      A system of prioritization or ranking of things in order of one over another.
Keystone

      The wedge-shaped stone at the apex of an arch or vault. It locks all the stones into position
      and allows the arch to be self-supporting. It could look like the other voussoirs or be highly
      decorative.
Loggia

      A gallery or room with one or more open sides, especially one that forms part of a structure
      that has one side facing onto an open space such as a garden or plaza.

331 | Glossary
Oculus (plural occuli)

      In architecture it refers to the round central opening of a dome or a small round window (such
      as in a church).
Ompholos

      Greek word meaning `navel', the term can be used to describe: a religious stone which may
      provide a direct connection with the gods; the `center of the world' often believed in ancient
      Greece to be at Delphi; the symbolic navel of the world which then allows for the ordering
      of all else. Example-or a University of Illinois graduate the Main Quad could be seen as the
      ompholos.
Orders

      The primary column systems used in Classical Architecture. These columns have proportions
      and attributes which make them distinct. The Orders are: Doric, Ionic and Corinthian (typ-
      ically attributed top the Greeks) and Composite and Tuscan (typically attributed to the
      Romans). The Composite is a combination of the Ionic and Corinthian Orders.
Parti

      The basic idea or organizational strategy of a design.
Pilasters

      A shallow rectangular feature projecting from a wall, having a capital and base and usually imi-
      tating the form of a column.
Poché

      Noun- that which gives shape, for example, the walls that give shape to a hallway; Verb- to fill
      in the area between walls and spaces.
Proportion

      The relationship of one thing to another in terms of quantity, size, or number; ratio.
Quoin

      Rectangular stone set at the outside corners of walls. The effect is to strengthen the ends and
      make a neater line. During the Renaissance, quoins are sometimes purely decorative.

                                                                                                                                                             Kevin J. Hinders | 332
Revetment

      (French for "recover") In architecture it refers to a covering (stucco, marble, etc.) over a less
      attractive construction (such as brick or concrete). The Romans often covered their important
      structures, such as the Pantheon and the Colosseum in marble and/or stucco.
Rhythm

      A particular pattern formed by regularly recurring elements.
Shearing forces

      Unaligned forces pushing one part of a body in one specific direction, and another part of the
      body in the opposite direction. A scissors, sometimes called a shear, uses this principle. Shear
      forces can cause a member to fail where the forces pass by one another.
Span

      The distance between supports.
Spring Line

      The point where the arch begins to rise from a support (column, pier, foundation, impost
      block, etc.). The Spring Line is where the arch has horizontal as well as vertical components.
Tensile

      Relating to tension; capable of being stretched out. A tensile structure relies on members
      being in tension, such as a rope bridge.
Tension

      A force that tends to stretch a mass.
Thrust

      The outward force exerted by an arch or vault that must be counterbalanced by a buttress,
      pier or wall in order to resist movement.
Trabeated

      Post and lintel construction. This construction technology system refers to the use of hori-
      zontal beams or lintels which are borne up by columns or posts. The name is from the Latin
      trabs for beam.

333 | Glossary
Vault
      A masonry roof or ceiling constructed on the arch principle (extruded arch).

Voussoir
      A wedge-shaped stone used in building an arch, vault or dome.

Web
      The area of a beam or truss which resists shear forces. The web typically is used to separate
      the beam flanges so as to allow the beam to resist bending.

Wythe
      A continuous vertical section of masonry one unit in thickness. A wythe can be independent
      (as in a veneer) or interlocked with other wythes to form a thicker wall.
